“ THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Untque Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A Suite Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
Anew Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition frpra its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red coverbis equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact —
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Subscription (payable strictly in advance), Six
Shillings and Sixpence per Quarter, or One Pound
per Annum.
Business communications to be addressed to
William Macleod, Managing Director, to whom
all remittances are payable. Editorial com,muni _
aliens to be addressed to J. F. ARCHIBALD.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or

is Lo Beng-ula, and that the Matabele should be

A new civilising agent has turned up in
the shape of the Salvator machine-gun, an Austrian
invention, which is claimed to be as simple and
effective as the Maxim, is only half the weight,
and costs 50 per cent. less. Apparently, the time
will soon come when every Anarchist can afford
to buy his own machine-gun, and in some moment
of aggravation they will turn a thousand of them
on London or Paris, and mop up the population
promiscuously. Dynamite isn’t even a patch on
the mechanical destroyer which blazes away by
itself so long as the owner has the money to buy
cartridges.
Dear Bulletin, —A few words of comment
on “ X.L’s.” par. re the temperament and teaching
of Christ : Your correspondent assumes that He
was an aristocrat—taking the word in its best
sense (“Faith, for the worst is filthy,” says Ape-
mantus; “and would not hold taking, I doubt
me.”)—that He denounced only the bourgeoisie,
and that He was not a democrat. First let us
carefully verify our terminology, so that shifting
definitions may not lead to confusion of issues :
What is an aristocrat ? What is the distinguishing
quality of this Brahmin caste? Superior ability,
education or principle are not postulated, yet there
is something. That something is a nebulous
aloofness, a sort of divine right to do dirty work
by proxy, and eat bread in the sweat of some one
rise’s brow—a quality identifiable in the present
day only by its evil odour. Aristocrat, being an
old word, has attained a green old age, particularly
where the hair is coming off in flakes. It dates
from Burke’s “ Age of Chivalry,” when Christ-like
English Tories made it safer to kill a man than a
hare —when the classes had to be taken at their
own estimate —when the parson and the squire
persuaded our forefathers, per medium of the
stocks and the beadle’s whip, that the word
denoted something high. This impression of high-
ness isstill retained, but isnowseized, you will notice,
by one of the physical faculties. Owing to
enlightenment, it is not a name to conjure with—
in fact, it is impossible to touch it without a pair
of tongs and a respirator, or contemplate it with-
out an ounce of civet to sweeten the imagination ;
and the time is not far distant when it will be
carried out on a spade and buried in quicklime, at
midnight, in some secluded place. So much for
the aristocrat. What aloofness can anyone
find in Christ’s favourite title, Son op Man?
Why, the Nazarene artisan, meek and lowly in
heart— who came not to be ministered unto, but
to minister —who washed his followers’ feet —who
called them not servants, but friends—struck at
the very root of caste when He established a new
aristocracy of usefulness, in which he that was
greatest should be servant of all. Whereas, the
best aristocrat, in his best form, feels for the poor
and illiterate exactly as Father Martin felt for the
brute creation. The bourgeois, or Fat Man, needs
no definition ; we all know him —most of us to
our sorrow. He is a slave to the lust of the eyes,
as the aristocrat is to the pride of life—degener-
acies equally offensive to democracy, and, according
to the strange allegory of the Temptation, equally
repudiated by Christ. The Sermon on the Mount
is permeated by the most perfect spirit of demo-
cracy ever breathed on earth, whilst the last awful
denunciation of Jerusalem impartially embraces
aristocrat and bourgeois, and no other. In the
parable of the rich man and Lazarus, Oriental
hyperbole is exhausted in favour of the vagrant,
whilst in the parable of the wayfarer on the
Jericho road, the comparison between the Samari-
tan —the Chinaman of Palestine—and the priest
and Levite is to the infinite discredit of the two
aristocrats. For evident reasons, the aristocrat
and the bourgeois are never placed in contrast;
but each repeatedly suffers damaging comparison
with the lowly. “X. L.” will pardon me for
noticing that, though in the first place he draws
such a sharp distinction between the Fat Man and
the person whose offence is rank and smells to
heaven, yet toward the end of his short par. the
two characters become entangled and merged.
This confusion is not surprising; indeed, it is
difficult to be avoided, seeing that the resources of
the bourgeois are required to support the waste of
the aristocrat. Falstaff—himself an aristocrat,
and, in a too liberal sense, a Fat Man—had occa-
sion to wish that his means were greater and his
waist slenderer. Did Christ “often dine with rich
Pharisees and the like?” He dined with a
Pharisee named Simon —and, almost as a matter
of course, gently reproved his host for rudeness to
Himself and exclusiveness toward others. He
dined with the bourgeois, Zaccheus—and took
occasion to commend this objectionable person.
He dined at the house of Matthew with “a great
company of publicans and sinners.” He also
dined with the family at Bethany—and this com-
pletes the record. The founder of a religion which
aimed at making things new by the obliteration of
meretricious distinctions could not count aristocrat,
bourgeois or pauper, cbmulon or unclean. All
were potential material for the perfect democracy
He purposed. Again, the law of liberty commands
no one to sell all that he has and give to the poor.
This is purely discretionary. But the man who
feebly fences with the question has already made
the great refusal, and has every reason to go away
sorrowful; for no more is to be expected from him
who fears poverty than from the respecter of per-
sons —and heaven knows that is little enough. It
is just here that Christ applies the uncomfortable
metaphor of the camel and the needle’s eye.
“X. L.’s” reference to Joseph of Arimathea
must be inadvertent. Surely he has noticed that
when Christ’s career was finished—when the or-
ganisation He had lived and died to inaugurate
was established by his duly-commissioned apostles
—J. of A. became of necessity either an apostate or
a communist.-

One Hamlet runs a pawnbroker’s shop in
Brisbane. “My prophetic soul, my uncle !”
A Maoriland paper’s obituary on a suicided
remittance man :—“ He was in receipt of money
from Home at various times and enjoyed himself
thoroughly every time the cheque arrived.”
A' London .magistrate lately sentenced a
man to six weeks imprisonment for biting a num-
ber qf pieces out of his illegitimate daughter, aged
about six years. Six weeks for cannibalism on a
live relative seems cheap.
Exchange : —Mrs. Honeymoon : “Do you
love me ?'* Old Party (confidentially, from other
seat, to bridegroom): “That makes 47 times she’s
askedyou. I get out here, but I’ll leave the score
with this gentleman by the window.” Which
recalls the commercial traveller in the top berth of
a sleeping-car listening to the newly-married
couple underneath. She : “ Kiss me again,
Charlie, and then I’ll go to sleep.” Traveller :
“Yes, kiss her again, Charlie, and then we’ll all
go to sleep.”
Dear Bulletin, —Re Queensland Kanakas*
In ’6B I had charge of 25 Kanakas on Hon. Louis
Hope’s sugar plantation at Cleveland, about 28
miles from Brisbane, opposite Stradbroke Island,
I believe these were the first Kanakas introduced.
When I arrived very extensive machinery had
been erected ; the fields were worked by Kanakas
under a boss, the crushing, refining and distilling
was, done by whites paid by piece work, including
bagging the sugar. Hope received a grant of
land frpm the Government for successfully culti-
vating sugar-cane. By-the-by, he was the man
who fought the last duel on English soil, and a
good type of the fine, old English gentleman, all
of the olden time. —Yours, Reginald Meek,
Bourke, 28/2/’94.
Re the story of Sandow tearing in halves
two packs of cards at once. All the showiest
feats of “strong men,” remarks a Bulletin cor-
respondent, are feats requiring a knowledge of
physics. That is the meaning of “knack.” Per-
haps the hardest feat for any strong man to per-
form, simply because it can’t be done by brute-
strength, is this. The strong man lies on a level
floor flat on his back, with his arms also stretched
flat on the floor above his head. A second man
lays himself flat across the strong man’s hands.
Together they form a great capital T. The feat
is for the strong man to lift up the other, and,
without dropping him, lay him down just beyond
his feet so as to reverse the former position—thus,
j,. A man of ordinary strength can do it, if he
knows a little physics, but not even Sandow could
do it by sheer brute-strength.

SATURDAY, MARCH 31, 1894.
The “ Anglo-Saxon Peace.”
At almost every public function where the
American and the Britisher or the Australian
meet and feed amicably together, some
orator or other disinters the golden legend
of a good time coming, when a great Anglo-
Saxon confederation will keep peace
throughout the globe, from China to Peru
and from Franz Josef Land to the
waters of Magellan. No Anglo-American
gorge would be complete without the repeti-
tion of this ancient story. Therefore, when
the direful chestnut transpired for the
several billionth time at a recent ceremonial
at Sydney, over the foundation-stone of the
New York Mutual Insurance Co.’s new
offices, it was only what might have been
reasonably expected. On that occasion a
mellow and fraternal gathering drank the
toast of “ Our American Kindred,” and in
response the voluble Consul Bell we
had almost written Bull successor of
poor soaring Gilubrov Wells Griffin,
proceeded to tell all over again the thread-
bare story of the “ Anglo-Saxon Peace.” In
the time to come, according to this legend,
the British empire and the United States
are to be federated, and thus will be
formed a solid nation of 110,000,000
white men, all speaking the English
language ; and this nation—the largest,
strongest, freest, most enlightened and most
solid on earth—will issue its fiat that there
is to be no more war upon the surface of this
vexed planet. The Anglo-Saxon confedera-
tion will then overshadow the globe, just as
Rome did in the days of Augustus, and it
will suppress strife with a strong hand, and
bring about the millennium generally.
• • •
The Bulletin is tired—very tired —of
this oft-told tale of the Anglo-Saxon peace,
and it proposes now to examine the situa-
tion soberly. (Hitherto it has only been
examined at public dinners and with the
accompaniment of drink.) In the first place,
the proposed confederation, if it ever comes
off, will not be Anglo-Saxon at all in any
sense worth speaking of. The Anglo-Saxon,
if there is such a being, will be only an item
in the category. The chief parties to the
league will be Great Britain aud Ireland, the
United States, Canada and Australia. Of
the former, Ireland, Wales, most of Scotland,
and the extreme west of England are Celtic,
and the population of the remaining fragment
is only partly Anglo-Saxon though the
supposition that it is even partly Anglo-
Saxon is somewhat disturbed by the historical
theory that the conquerors of Britain were
really men of Swedish origin, and that no
Saxons ever reached her shores at all. The
white population of the United States is pro-
bably one-half, or almost one-half, of the
same dubious and mongrel Anglo-Saxon
race, while the balance is Celtic, Slavonic,
Magyar, Croat, Latin, and of many other
nationalities. The inhabitants of Canada
are largely of French descent, and the
balance more or less Saxon, and those of
Australia are of many different origins.
These races mostly use the English language,
which is about as much Latin as alleged
Saxon, aud instead of manifesting a desire to
federate and impose peace upon the world
they have instead a hungry yearning to cut
each other’s throats at any convenient oppor-
tunity. The Irishman aad the Welshman
both hate England with a great, solid,
enduring hatred. The Scot is starting a
Home Rule movement, and would rather
separate than federate any further. The
Englishman has no love for any one of the
three. Also he has an old grudge against
the American, and the latter reciprocates
the sentiment cordially. The United States
itself is not united at heart ; Canada is
divided into two factions—the faction which
hates England and the faction which cordially
loathes the United States ; and Australia is
not especially loyal to any fox-eign state or
any foreign ideal. Therefore there are three
distinct and separate difficulties in the way
of the Anglo-Saxon Federation of Peace :
(1) It couldn’t, by any possibility, be Anglo-
Saxon ; (2) It couldn’t federate at all, for its
heterogeneous elements hate each other
much too energetically ; and (3) even if it
started its impossible career it couldn’t en-
force peace in any case.
Viewed through a mellow atmosphere of
provisions and drink there is, no doubt,
something beautiful in the picture of an im-
possible nation, 110,000,000 strong, issuing
its fiat that there is to be no more w r ar or
trouble or any kind of disturbance from now
till the latter end of Time. But, apart from
the drink and the provisions, the vision is
mere foolishness. The nation which com-
mands universal peace can only enforce its
commands by crushing, with military force,
every state or combination of states, which
begins war. Not only this, but it must
crush it so effectually that it can afterwards
compel it to disarm as the only possible
guarantee against a perpetual repetition of
the trouble. To do this, it must keep up a
larger army and navy than those of any
combination which is likely to be brought
against it, which, on a rough estimate, means
that the Commonwealth of Peace will be a
vast camp with almost every able-bodied man
perpetually under arras, and that the mil-
lennium will be the most bloodstained epoch
in history. And when it has fought with
and defeated all the great powers of the
world in succession, and thus made all the
rest of the earth its enemy, it must keep up
such a military and naval establishment as
will qualify it, at any moment, to fight all the
balance of the human race combined. It
must do this, not merely to keep the
universal peace, but to save itself from con-
quest and extinction. Its position will be
practically that of Napoleon I. in a seriously
aggravated form. Napoleon set out, not to
secure the peace of the world, but merely to
secure the peace of Europe, by the process of
disarming every nation which made war—
the same process which the mythical Anglo-
Saxon Federation will have to pursue. And
he ended as most practical apostles of univer-
sal peace do end.
# • »
The proposed millennium is a brilliant ex-
ample of the festive bumptiousness of that
person who calls himself Anglo-Saxon for
want of any better information concerning
his own origin. The picture of the com-
bined English-speaking races, 110,000,000
strong, putting down war with a strong
hand, is interesting as an example of spread-
eagleism, but nothing more. The whole
110,000,000, scattered loosely through three
continents and over a thousand islands,
would be barely equal to the one solid mass
of the Russian nation. All the available
force which it could collect in one spot at
reasonably short notice would hardly make a
dent in the solid front of the Triple
Alliance. That Alliance is now busy stand-
ing off two closely massed nations something
like 140,000,000 strong, and the prospects of
its being scared into submission by the fiat
of a much smaller power, which straggled
loosely over half the earth and had no
visible centre, are exceedingly limited. The
English-speaking races, in fact, suffer from
the common disease of being much less im-
portant than they imagine, and if ever an
Anglo-Saxon confederation, being drunk or
possessed of an evil spirit, starts out to
enforce universal peace, the experiment is
likely to end in the utter crumpling up of
the confederation and all its works.
« « •
Moreover, apart from the fact that the so-
called Anglo-Saxon peoples are not Anglo-
Saxon, that the alleged English-speaking
races to a great extent don’t speak English,
and that the proposed league of peace
couldn’t keep the peace oven if it tried,
there remains the painful fact that it doesn’t
want to try in any case. At the beginning
of the present century, England spent some
£600,000,000 to suppress the only universal
peacemaker that Europe has produced since
the days of the (Lesars, and it is still so
inflated with pride over the performance
that its appearance in the same character
would be unspeakably grotesque. Of all
the scattered sections of the alleged
English-speaking races the only one which
lies within the region of perpetual w'ar
alarms is Britain itself ; to the others it is a
matter of comparatively small consequence
whether the world is at peace or not.
Therefore, the prospect of spending a billion
or so in pursuit of an impossible ideal,
which would be of very little interest even
if it could be attained, is not attractive.
Further, the “English-speaking” races
wouldn’t federate, even apart from all their
ancient hatreds, and even supposing that uni-
versal peace was to be acquired gratis. If they
did federate, the centre of political power
must go with the centre of population, and
before the Englishman would consent to be
governed from Washington by the nominee of
an American majority, he would ally himself
with the Russian and the unspeakable Turk,
and send out a new Armada with orders to
burn New York and hang the President from
the roof of his own White House. And
apart even from these considerations, the
assertion that it is the Anglo-Saxon’s mission
to help on the era of all-round brotherhood
doesn't seem to rest on any solid evidence.
There is no visible proof, in fact, that he has
any “mission” at all, or, at least, that he
has it more badly than anybody else. Also,
there is a great lack of definite explanation
as to what a mission consists of, and no one
seems to have ascertained with certainty that
there is any such thing at all. Probably
enough there isn’t. Nations, as a rule, seem
to drift from no well-defined starting-point to
no very definite conclusion. A serious con-
sideration of history rather suggests that the
most of them never had any mission what-
ever, but were merely a series of accidents,
and even if the Anglo-Saxon is built on the
same plan as the rest he has no special
reason to complain.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Squatters and Shearers.
The N.S. W. Pastoralists’ Union is evidently
spoiling for a fight. It has given public notice
that its members “ will make no engagements
for the coming season under the shearing agree-
ment adopted in Sydney, August 7th and Bth,
1891.” This agreement brought to a close a
long and disastrous warfare between pastoralists
and shearers. There were two parties to it,
the Pastoralists’ Union and the Shearers’ Union.
The pastoralists have now', without reference to
the shearers, without stating any grievances or
asking any redress, without giving any oppor-
tunity for conciliation or compromise, put a
deliberate end to the joint agreement, and sub-
stituted another to suit themselves. If trouble
ensues, whose will the blame be ? What
would be said if the shearers had thrown
over the agreement without consulting the
pastoralists? Would not the capitalist dailies
have raved of their breach of courtesy, of
their breach of faith, of their disregard for
the community, of their efforts to create new
strife? There is no breach of faith. True ;
neither party to the agreement undertook to
keep it in force for ever. But the least that
either can expect from the other is some notice
of intentions, some endeavour to make change
acceptable if insisted on. Employer and work-
man, landlord and tenant, maintain their agree-
ment and relation until either sees fit to annul
them ; but the law' rightly holds that the party
who wishes a change must give sufficient notice
of his intentions. It cannot be said that the
pastoralists’ action is justified by the decision
of the shearers’ conference, because it was con-
templated, if not decided, long before the
shearers’ conference took place; and in any event
the shearers’ decision not to accept references,
and to make engagements direct, in no way con-
flicts with the 1891 agreement. The plain truth
is that there are in Australia many unemployed,
and the coming winter is looked forward to as
a time of great hardship for workmen. The
Pastoralists’ Union apparently means to take
advantage of these circumstances to break down
the Shearers’ Union and starve the men out of
independence into submission to their masters’
terms. The plan is cowardly and tyrannous,
and will re-act to the ruin of the squatters
themselves. The workers have now full Par-
liamentary representation in the Assembly, at
all events, and Parliament is not likely to for-
get, in dealing with the pastoralists, this last
claim which they have established to be re-
garded as enemies to the interests of Australian
manhood.
Jury ‘‘Fixing.”
A professional jury -briber is alleged to be
flourishing in Melbourne. His name is on men’s
tongues, and he is said to mix “ jury-squaring”
with magistrate-nobbling. He is mostly for the
defence, of course, but in civil cases the plain-
tiff may engage his valuable services. He has
acquiredareputation for making things “right,”
and it is remarked that the stamp of man who
seems least accessible to “ reason ” is gradually
disappearing from the Melbourne jury-system.
Possibly honesty is disappearing from the land
altogether. Jury-bribing is an old institution
in more countries than one, but Melbourne
must surely be the one aud only lax-ge city
which accepts the idea of corruption in the
law courts without some sort of protest. Belief
in one’s fellow citizen died slowdy of “ revela-
tions ” and was buried, aud not five per cent, of
the population wept at the funeral. Self-
respect departed from Jones when he found
hat Brown and Robinson were publicly com-
mended for their roguery, and when Jones
grew tired of evincing disgust the sentiment of
honour passed into a vulgar ’ commercial jest.
The learned profession of the law has done not
a little to bring about the demoralisation of
Melbourne. Barristers clothed in the majesty
of wig and gown have been permitted to estab-
lish the theory that the criminal who goes un-
prosecuted is an excuse for his particular form
of crime. Persons of reputed good character
have asked his Honor the Judge to take note
of the fact that “ they all did it,” and
were, therefore, all right, and the Judge, in a
general way, has stared vacantly into space
instead of annihilating the hideous argument on
the spot. Moreover, Melbourne is so full of
legal firms and their managing clerks—you can-
not heave a brick without hitting a managing-
clerk—that honest opinion even in private lite
is overruled by the authoritative doctrines of
those who find more profit in confounding jus-
tice than by enforcing it, and wdio are the
natural enemies of fair dealing by reason of
their calling. In older countries lawyers are a
class by themselves. Here they mix much in
society, and society is infected by them. Too
much knowledge of lawyers produces a general
contempt for the juror, and the juror who
pockets his convictions consoles himself with
the reflection that he has quite as much prin-
ciple as other people who are working the case
for all they can get out of it.

Some More of the Hampton Plains
Shiudykit.
The leprosy of Foreign Capital is steadily eat-
ing away the inmost soul of West Australia.
That great province is mostly run by British
syndicates, and with a view to the “ opening
up of the country ” that good old phrase
which covers so many swindies —slabs of
territory, the size of a decent European princi-
pality, have been sold for a few pounds to
speculators who do not open them up, but
simply leave them in their natural state and
wait for a chance to sell them back again to the
idiotic Australian at 10,000 per cent, profit.
The Hampton Plains syndicate is one of the
most glaring of these many frauds upon
posterity. This bloated concern, which has its
head-quarters in England, somehow negotiated
the right of buying 216,000 acres of W.A.
land, with minerals thrown, in for £27,000.
It was allowed a number of years in
which to select its land so that it might take
advantage of anything which turned up, and it
only paid upon selection, so that the province
didn’t even get the miserable £27,000 at once,
but had to wait about six years for the money.
The syndicate did no improvements what-
ever, and spent no money, in the country;
it merely waited until there came the first
rumours of the prospective riches of Cool-
gardie, and then it selected most of the held,
only missing Bayley’s Reward claim by a mile,
or rather less. The Government could now, in
all probability, have got several times as much
for 640 acres at one end of the Hampton Plains
estate as the awful syndicate pays for ti e
entire 216,000. Also, the syndicate is exempt
from all taxes for 12 years, and, considerh g
the vast possibilities of the region, it
thus, in all probability, gets its money
back twice over in exemptions, in which
case the province will have paid £27,000
for the privilege of losing 216,000 acres
of land, and, possibly, a second Ballarat thrown
in. The latest developments of this hideously
brazen job are especially interesting. A
London company, with a capital of £lOO,OOO,
proposes to lease 600 acres of the syndicate’s
land for mining purposes, and will pay to the
syndicate one-third of the nett profits for the
right of doing so. If the company meets with
anything like decent fortune, the syndicate
may thus easily get, from one-three-hundredth
part of its property, a profit of 400 per-
cent. per annum on its whole outlay.
Then it will probably sell similar conces-
sions to other companies on equally good
terms. Next, towns will spring up on the
syndicate’s land, and the syndicate will be the
sole ground landlord, and will scoop in many
more thousands per sheut per annum. Further,
if gold is discovered outside its boundaries it
has the lease, with option of purchase, of
another million acres at the same rate, and
thus may extend its operations almost in-
definitely. And all these thousands or tens of
thousands per shent will go to England as the
price which Australia has to pay, and to keep
on paying for ever and ever, for the use of that
original miserable £27,000 of foreign capital,
which never did anything whatever to “ open
up the country.”
News from ‘‘New Australia.”
The latest news from “ New Australia ” comes
in the shape of a letter from C. H. Mannino,
one of the seceded settlers. The following are
a few extracts :
We were scarcely three weeks on the ocean when Lane
began to show himself in his true character—that of an
autocrat. For some reason, which he refused to make
known, he issued an order that all single girls were to go
below after sundown. You will readily understand the
arbitrary nature of this order when I tell you that, with
one exception, all the girls on the boat were under the
protection of parents or guardians. . . . We dis-
covered that instead of the land being only 15 miles from
Villa Rica it was at least 30, and nowhere near the Tihi-
cuari River, of which so much was made in our pro.
spector’s report. There was no timber which would split,
and so we were compelled to build mud huts and thatch
them with grass. Grass for this purpose was not easily
obtained, and so, although three months at the settlement,
some few families did not attain to the luxury of even a
mud hut. All this time the breach between th-se who
were content to live under absolute despotism and those
who claimed the right of having a say in the management
of their own affairs was growing wider every day. We
were split into two sections, known respectively as the
“Royalists” and the “Rebels.” The “Rebels”
were in the majority, and at the second last
meeting held_ at the settlement a vote of no-
confidence in the chair was carried Lane
proceeded Assuncion and registered the associa-
tion as a joint stock company on totally different, lines
from those We agreed to in Australia. He did this without
consulting us ; in fact, it .was purely by accident we
learned that he had done so. His absolute power was
now legalised, and it became patent to us that he had
succeeded in “dishing the Whigs.” . . . On Fiiday
evening, of the 15th inst., a troop of cavalry and special
constables swarmed into the settlement and proceeded
forthwith- to gxecute an order given by Lane to eject
three men whom he considered to-be ringleaders. It was
the moat brutal exhibition power I have ever witnessed.
One of the victims, Mr. A. T. Westwood, a man well-
knowu in Adelaide, had for a fortnight or more been sat


feeing from dysentery. : On Saturday morning he was
forced from hie bed, and given half-an-hour to pack up
his effects. Many of the women called upon us to resist
the troopers. But I feel sure we adopted the wiser plan,
as nothing would have pleased some of Lane’s satellites
better than the sight of the police firing upon us. The
job was evidently distasteful to the captain, but he was
compelled to obey the orders of the Czar. For days pre-
vious some of our brutal mates persisted in using
the most filthy language to our wives, brandished
revolvers, and threatened to shed our blood
“New Australia” was started ostensibly to show the
world an object lesson in how to lead happier and better
Ihes. It has commenced by turning a large number of
its members adrift in a land where they cannot speak the
language of the people—adrift, and almost penniless. It
has yet another lesson to show the world—how to eviot
the native population. On the land claimed by the
association more than 300 families are living. They have
all got orange-groves, small plots which they cultivate,
and a few cattle and sheep. They are honest, peaceable
peasantry, and in many respects superior to those who
are about to despoil them of their homes. I roust confess
that, almost to a man, the members of the association
have raised their voices against driving these poor people
out. Not so the officers. Arrangements have been made
with the Government by which, after a certain date, the
natives will be cleared out, and “New Australia " will
have shown to the world object lesson No. 2
An acre of land on the Hunter or on any of
the eastern rivers is worth more than three
of the very best in Paraguay. Paraguay can beat the
world in the production of oranges, but other fruits do
not thrive. A man with a little capital might do well
raising cattle, as large blocks of land may be purchased
at Is. per acre. If a man gets hard up here he is doomed.
If he succeeds in getting a job he must work for from Is.
to Is. fid. per day, out of wiiich he must find his food.
Yet there is not a man or woman amongst us who regrets
their having broken away from the despotism of William
Lane. The crowning indignity heaped upon us by that
gentleman was his issue of an order that our boxes and
other luggage should bi searched before being put upon
the drays, lest we should take away association property.
No less a personage than the deputy chairman was cut
out for this dirty job. And he did it well. Women were
compelled to open up their boxes and exhibit their baby-
linen and underclothing to his vulgar gaze. . . . It is
all moonshine to say that all the requirements of civilised
men can be produced in Paraguay. So that, whatever
dreamers may say to the contrary, New Australians must
find a market for the things that can be produced in the
country. And the finding of this market will be one of
their greatest problems.
What a change from the picture of three short
mouths ago —The enthusiastic crowds at Assun-
cion, the orange-groves, the moonlight on the
river with guitar accompaniments, the Para-
guayan officers who bowed to the ground and
called the New Australian girls “senorita,”
the serenades, and all the rest of the poetry.
But the worst change is in Lane himself— Lane
who now writes to point out that the troubles
all arise through “ materialism ” and the lack
of “ belief in God.” This lamentable snuffle is
suggestive of Stead, the dirty saint of the
Review of Reviews, and, besides, it is al-
together out of place. Lane didn’t snuffle before
he started, and his doing so now is a clear
violation of the spirit of the agreement.
The Necessity of Anarchy.
Vaillant, the anarchist, justified his name,
valiant to the last, going to death as a man may
go to sleep, and crying with his last breath,
“Death to the bourgeois society, and long live
anarchy!” “Let my body,” he directed, “be
given to the doctors as soon as possible, that
they may be able to study in the interests of
science the semi-vital phenomena which disap-
pear after the disintegration of atoms.” “No
hatred,” he said, “ no malice animated me when
I threw the bomb into the Chamber of Deputies.
I struck not, at individuals, but at Society,
which is hostile to humanity; and I acted but
as the surgeons do who hesitate not to sacrifice
a limb to save a life.” This was not the calcu-
lated bombast of despair ; it was a reflection of
the man’s character perfectly consistent with
all his other actions and expressions. What
terrors has death for such as Vaillant ? The
fate of Ravachol deterred him no more than
the fate of Clement deterred Ravaillac;
the fate of Jaureguy, Gerard; the fate of
Booth, Guiteau. Men of Yaillant’s type
have always existed, and the pressure of modern
conditions, increasing nervous degeneracy, has
proportionately strengthened the type and
added to its exemplars. Society must readjust
its methods in the light of science. Its stability
is dependent on the existence of a normal
majority—average men and women, neither
virtuous nor vicious, drawn by commonplace
pleasures, deterred by commonplace pains,
living and dying without varying greatly from
the current standard of humanity. The normal
man rules. He makes laws to suit his class,
and in the main they are obeyed, because they
are suited to most of those who are called upon
to obey them. On the whole, he is satisfied
with his life ; and the idea of death is abhorrent
to him. So he places death at the head of the
penalties which he imposes for breach of his
laws. But he forgets that both above and below
his preponderant class there is a class of
abnormal men and women, partly born so,
partly recruited from the normal ranks by
stress of individual struggle or influence of
individual environment. Upon this class laws
have little or no hold. Laws are only effective
m proportion to their fitness for the society in
which they exist. Most people are restrained
from vice and crime, not by law, but by per-
sonal character, by social influences, by a
deliberate calculation that, apart from law, the
game isn’t worth the candle. Law is chiefly a
secondary influence, strengthening and con-
firming existing tendencies. It is only because
a majority are essentially non-criminal that
laws are of value. If criminals were a
majority, the standard of virtue would be
altered, laws would inevitably support the
cnminal, and the failure to commit crime
would be an offence against law and right,
bociety will never abolish crime until it realises
that the chief offenders against its laws are the
very persons upon whom laws have no effect—
until the normal man realises that the criteria
y which he rules his life can never be those of
ie abnormal man. Punishment is the very
least of the influences which play upon crime.
° u v way to cure crime is to prevent it,
ana the only means of preventing it is to
Decrease, as far as possible, all the precedent
influences which go to make a criminal, and
increase, as far as possible, all the precedent
influences which go to destroy the criminal in
ie germ. Whether Vaillant is punished by
imprisonment, or by death, or by torture, makes
nitie or no difference to the class of men from
winch he springs. Being what he was, he was
• n n f .^ 0 do what he did; and his comrades
• , J°w hmi in the path of destiny as surely
a night follows day. The only way to destroy
anarchy is to destroy the conditions which
create it.

P ULLETI! ? I s ready to bet, re the recent arming of
the Sydney police with pistols, that a policeman will com-
mit suicide with his revolver, or shoot a fellow-constable
or an innocent citizen, or be shot with his own weapon by
some criminal who has snatcbedTt, before any bona-fide
burglar or murderer has bitten the dust per medium of
the newly-served-out weapons.
And, last week in Sydney, a man did succeed in
snatching a policeman’s pistol but was fortu-
nately at once laid out by the officer with a fist-
blow. Public panic and the hysterical action
of the authorities have laid in Australia the
foundation of the reign of the revolver. The
revolver feeds the gallows and the gallows feeds
the revolver. Within two years in Australia
people will probably be shooting each other all
over the place. If they aren’t, it won’t be the
fault of the authorities.
H. H. Lusk, barrister, struck off New South
Wales law-roll last week by the Supreme Court,
for malversation of trust-funds, was formerly a
prominent provincial politician in Maoriland.
In that country he was latterly unfortunate,
and when in his old age he came to New South
Wales, his desperate necessities compelled him
to incur the deadly hostility of the local bar by
seeing clients without the intervention of an
attorney. But there can be no excuse for the
lawyer who rooks his customers, and as the
Pull Court, presided over by Chief Justice
Harley, deals out equal measure to all
“ cronks ” haled before it, Mr. Lusk may be
taken to have earned his terrible sentence.
When The Bulletin says that N.S. Wales
Pull Court deals out equal measure to all legal
offenders arraigned, it, of course, doesn’t mean
to say that the Court takes steps to see that all
the “cronks” are arraigned. According to
public opinion, the rich and aggressive barrister
who, after accepting a big fee, throws over his
client simply because somebody has handed him
a bigger bribe—the lawyer calls it a “ fee is
quite as much a robber as a poor, weak, broken-
down old counsel like Lusk, who scratches out
a living in all sorts of unorthodox and devious
ways. The barristers as a corporation exult in
the wiping-out of the broken Lusk, but The
Bulletin deeply regrets that the old man is
not accompanied to Limbo by a number of the
cheeky horse-haired Highwaymen who so glibly
talk about “ the honour of the Bar.”
One of the hideous jokes of the last Mercantile
Bank case was the non-appearance of Gyles
Turner as a witness for either side. The whole
business pivoted upon that £lOO,OOO “loan”
from the Commercial, yet,manager Turner was
this time omitted from the cast of the little
play. Instead of calling Turner as a witness,
Matthew Hayies called his relative, Fuller, a
more or less private secretary, who narrated a
conversation that took place between himself
and Matthew regarding the terms on which
the “satisfactory increase in deposits” had
been borrowed, and this conversation—it might
as well have been an account of what the chair-
man said to a stranger in Collins-street—did
duty for important evidence. Survey this extra-
ordinary matter from the defence standpoint.
The Mercantile Bank thought it had obtained
£lOO,OOO from Gyles Turner’s bank for a year,
certain. Evidently it thought wrongly. Turner
is not called to account for the wrongness of the
Mercantile thinking, which would have been a
very shaky apology for the error, but a casual
individual named Fuller comes along with a
cheerful declaration that he was the first per-
son to inform the Mercantile chairman of the
real arrangement made between Turner and the
busting bank. Fuller threw not a glimmer
of legal light upon the boiled-down case.
Muntz’s cook might have told the jury how
Muntz said, “Bless my soul! You astonish
me,” when she informed him that she had
learned from the milkman’s uncle the truth
about the £lOO,OOO “loan.” And the cook’s
evidence would have been quite as much to the
point.

Trial by jury has received another jar, of a
novel kind, in Marvellous Melbourne. In con-
nection with a libel-action brought against the
Evening Standard Newspaper Co., the fore-
man of the jury called at the Standard to
purchase a copy of the back number containing
the alleged libel. A charge of sixpence was
made tor the paper. The applicant objected to
paying sixpence, interviewed the acting-
manager, and being told that he couldn’t get
the paper at a lower price, stalked out of the
office, saying : “I am one of the jury on the
case, and will bear it in mind.” Naturally, the
Standard applied for, and obtained, the
discharge of the tribunal presided over
by this irate person. The Judge acquitted
him of any wrong motive in interviewing
the defendants’ representative on a trum-
pery matter of sixpence, but ordered a new
jury all the same. Here was a very clear
instance of a man’s mental unfitness to be a
juror. He lacked the sense of propriety which
should have kept him out of the Standard
office, not to mention the manager’s room,
under such delicate circumstances. He had
seemingly such an absurd notion of his dignity
and importance as foreman of the jury that any
trivial incident might have made his angry
passions rise against one side or other, in the
case which he was expected to consider with calm
impartiality. If a J udge or magistrate made a
similar ass of himself he would be declared in-
competent to hold his important position. Yet
no considerations of this kind have thus far
been allowed to touch the old, respectable jury
system.
At the banquet to mark the erection of the
Mutual of New York’s big Sydney building,
nobody bothered to mention that thejMaoriland
Government Life Insurance Department is
steadily pressing competitors out of existence,
because its premiums are low, its bonuses are
high, and its security is absolute. Yet to
this complexion must Australia also come.
Even our best companies take more or
i less speculative liabilities, while Heaven
and their directors only know what the
others are doing, since no adequate statement
of their position is ever made. In the two
or three cases where full returns are given,
none but an expert can judge of their value,
and the unexpected never happens so frequently
as it happens to the expert. The man in the
street is compelled to take everything on trust,
and rely on the chapter of accidents. Hitherto
Australian insurance offices have shared in
general Australian prosperity, as did Austra-
lian banks. Yet, a day came when most of the
banks fell, and there is no certain guarantee
that in their time of trial the insurance com-
panies will prove absolutely stable. With
Government insurance there is such a guarantee,
for when you get State security you insure your
insurance. And as security to the assured is
the chief thing needful, this consideration must
outweigh all others. Its preponderance enables
Government to almost dispense with touts,
agents and all the paraphernalia which swell
the expenses of the ordinary office. There is
machinery ready to hand in the existing Civil
Service, The Maoriland Government Life
Insurance Department has been established
since 1870 ; it has two millions of accumulated
funds, and an annual income of £342,000 ;
there are 28,000 policy-holders, who divide
the profits as bonuses every five years.
The average rate of interest earned is
£5 Bs. lid, per cent., although the department
can only advance one-half of its funds on mort-
gage, and then only up to one-half the value of
the property ; the remainder being invested in
the securities of Government or of local authori-
ties. The scheme has only to be stated to
secure support, while Maoriland’s experience
proves it both practicable and profitable. The
widespread loss and distress accruing from the
failure of a powerful insurance company is
terrible to contemplate. Yet, under existing
conditions, no man can say that, despite the
honesty and carefulness with which our best
offices have apparently been conducted, a
failure, or a series of failures, is utterly im-
possible.
A CORRESPONDENT :
Tha N.S. Wales Disorderly Conduct Suppression Bill,
w.tli its proposed clause to practically make Judges of
ordinary mortal policemen, is to be revived. In this
drastic measure it is provided that small boys may be
seized by large policemen, and charged with disorderly
conduct, it being only necessary for proof of disorderliness
that the policeman shall think it so. Th 3 responsibility
of thinking is a terrible one to impose upon an ordinary
constable. But the consequences to the police are
nothing to those likely to happen to the small boy.
Marbles, tops, kites, the federation of tin-pots to
dogs’ tails, exploiting orchards of unripe fruit, all
that boys look forward to, may become crimes under
this Act, and may, in the opinion of the intellectual police-
man, be disorderly and fioggable offences. But having
entrusted these large powers to the police, why not go
further? N.S. Wales has now armed them to the teeth,
so that they may be protected from the promiscuous
burglar, whom they are to capitally punish on the spot.
Along with the revolver for the burglar why not serve out
a cat-o’-nine-tails to each policeman, so that under the
provisions of the new Act the offender of smaller growth
can be flogged instanter ? If he is too big to flog, let
him be shot, and let the post-mortem charge be altered to
burglary. This will save a lot of red tape and expense.
If a portable gallows could also be served out (one that
would fold up and serve as a stretcher for accidents or
shooting cases, or a bed on which the police could have a
nocturnal nap) the hangman, another expensive civil
servant, might also l)e dispensed with, his salary saved,
and summary and complete justice done. Things would
then run smoothly until a Legislative Councillor or a
Minister of the Crown was hung by mistake, or shot bv
accident, or their children flogged in error by a new and
zealous officer. In any of these cases New South Wales
might return to the old and effete methods of trial by
jury before a Judge. Otherwise, the policeman of the
future will be expected to have large heads, instead of, as
at present, large feet.
The Victorian Government, under the guidance
of Shambles Patterson, is busy “ settling the
people on the land.” According to the assur-
ances of the discordant meat-man who runs it,
its one great ambition is to “ settle the people
on the land.” The way it accomplishes this end
is to auction land in little six-acre blocks, so
that the farmer has to buy about ten of them to
make a farm which is large enough to live upon.
Then it gives him ten separate receipts for his
money, with a 20s. stamp on each, the agricul-
turist having to pay for the £lO worth of
stamps. Also, it charges him £lO 10s. for
ten separate parchment deeds at a guinea each.
One recent purchaser objected to this extortion
and explained that all he wanted was one
receipt and one deed for the whole farm, but he
was sternly informed that the above-mentioned
swindle was “ the uniform custom of the depart-
ment,” and couldn’t be altered. Then he applied
to the Government for redress, and the Govern-
ment inquired into the matter, and presently
wrote to him, in a large, round hand, stating
that the aforesaid robbery was strictly “in
accordance with the regulations,” and as the
regulations are fixed and cast-iron and abso-
lutely immovable, there is nothing more to be
said. Meanwhile, the Government’s yearning
to settle the people on the land continues un-
abated, and Patterson has eaten no less than
eighteen banquets on the subject within the last
six weeks in different parts of the province, as
solid evidence of his sincerity.
♦
N.S.W. Government should act decisively on
the opinion expressed by its pet official, Joseph
Greer, to the effect that all labour registry
offices should be licensed and placed under
Government supervision. Mr. Greer alludes
only mildly to the iniquities practised by many
vicious spiders w'ho call themselves labour-
agents. These levy fees on both employers
and applicants for billets. Many labour-agents
will not even impart the slightest scrap of
detailed information to a work-seeker until the
fee—usually ss.—is paid down. When it is
paid, sometimes after the pawning of
clothing to raise the fee, applicant, as
often as not, finds that he is only one out
of a score sent after the one solitary
billet. Some labour-agents copy out “wanted”
advertisements from the dailies, and despatch
their clients to published addresses, the clients
finding that the agent had no authority what-
ever to act on behalf of the advertiser. When
a sure and pretty fair billet is really entrusted
to the hands of one of this class of agents, he,
after naming the salary and describing the
supposed duties, but keeping dark the address,
demands a bonus amounting to a week’s, a fort-
night’s, or even a month’s wages. And he
won’t take the applicant’s promise to send down
the amount; he demands spot cash. Th«
labour-agent certainly wants roping in; or,
better still, the Government should establish
State Labour-agencies throughout the land,
charging only moderate fees, and being willing
to garnishee the successful applicant’s wages if
he cannot pay in advance.
3>kar Bulletin, —Bishop Laniqan affirms that
“ the teaching of the Public Schools is intrinsic
cally dangerous to faith and morals.” Then
why doesn’t the R.C. Church forbid R.C.
parents to allow their children to enter the
service of the Public Instruction Department ?
If it is an evil for R.C. children to be taught in
godless public schools, then it must be an
infinitely greater evil for R.C. children to
become teachers in said schools. Yet there are
vast numbers of R.C. teachers scattered all
over Australasia; and in country townships
the majority of them are perfect treasures to
their pastors. In return for their usefulness in
fund-raising they are often rewarded by hear-
ing their priests and bishops abuse in un-
measured terms the system under which they
gain an honourable living. By the way, it is
greatly to the credit of the Public Instruction
Departments that their R.C. teachers get a fair
field and no favour.—T.Y.K.

Alhambra Variety Show.
Sydney Royal re-opened on Saturday with
a somewhat desolate comedy entitled, for no par-
ticular reason, “As Large as Life.” Nobody in
it is any larger or any more life-like than the
average, and if the piece had been designated any-
thing else, or if it hadn’t been designated anything
at all, it would be quite as appropriate. Its two
leading features are a red-headed Italian and a
man who appears.in pink tights at the Zoo. He
has no visible reason for appearing in tights at the
Zoo., but it is characteristic of the comedy that
nobody in it has any visible reason for anything.
There are sundry carefully-elaborated complica-
tions in the course of the three acts, but they are
unconvincing and not at all interesting. They
don’t arrive naturally, but are dragged in by the
hair, like an insensible drunk being brought
in by a policeman. The trouble all begins
through the resemblance of a very commonplace
young man to three separate portraits, and as he
doesn’t resemble the portraits, this damages
the illusion. A lady insists, for no earthly reason,
on saying that this dismal youth is her husband
and a notorious lion-tamer; a doddering old man
chooses, on no evidence whatever, to regard him
as his nephew in disguise; and a fatuous johnny
decides in what he thinks is his mind that the
same dreary person is a ballet-girl. And when
these things have all happened, the youth himself,
for no apparent cause, undresses in a lady’s
coal-cellar and goes off, in flesh-coloured tights, to
the Zoo. and stands in front of the bear, where
everybody accepts him as a matter of course, and
professes not to notice his tights. There he
announces, for no apparent cause, that he is a
thief and steals buns, and the comedy finishes.
# • *
The co. is very much like the comedy,
inasmuch as there is no visible reason for it
either. An exception is here made in favour of
Mias Polly Emery, who would be a bright oasis in
a three-act desert if it wasn’t that she has got
no part worth speaking of. A sketchy-looking
personage, described in the bill as Mr. Nelville,
plays the man in tights at the Zoo. with profound
seriousness and with the easy grace of a church-
warden, and most of the others wrestle with
their parts after the airy fashion of a person who
carries coals up a steep hill. A one-act comedietta
called ‘’Bluejackets’ 1 is a little better than its
predecessor, mainly because there is a little more
of Polly Emery in it, and rather less of the other
people.
** * *
Sydney Gaiety, so long abandoned to rats
and gophers, re-opened on Saturday as the Bijou,
under the management of Mr. W. J. Wilson,
formerly in partnership with the ill-fated Signor
Majeroni. Mr. Wilson is a scenic artist of a high
order, and his new act-drop is a vast improvement
on the somewhat mouldy publication which used
to occupy the front of the stage. The Bijou is
devoted to comedy, and opened with “ The
Strategist,” a good and riotous production which
has vanished for so many years that the new
generation has practically forgotten it. It
turns mainly on the wickedness of a ver-
satile young man who, for his own pur-
poses, disguises himself as his own father, and
afterwards as the father of his best girl, and
presently has quite a number of causes—including
a large bowl of senna—to wish that he hadn’t done
it. The show is run by a good co. which plays
very evenly throughout. Frank Hawthorn, Harry
Leston, and H. N. Douglas, and Misses Gladys
Leigh and Beatrice Lyster are the principal
features of the aggregation. The new venture
begins vvell, and the only unpromising feature
about it is the inborn melancholy of the Gaiety,
which, though it may be called the Bijou unto
seventy times seven, will keep on looking like the
same old Gaiety just the same.
** * *
“ Hans the Boatman ” continues his placid
and pathetic career at Sydney Criterion, and little
baby Arnold’s chirrupy, audible prattle when she
appears in the explosion scene might be a lesson to
a lot of mumbling grown-up actors. The juvenile
Jessie Mead corrals the approval of even the most
inveterate hater of the staring stage-child, which
generally makes a feeling person long to hustle it
into bed. Charles Arnold, as Hans, gives tongue
to fewer melodies than of yore, which is a grievomf
circumstance.
** * *
Mrs. Potter’s Australian tour is postponed
owing to misfit dates. Her big success in India
was as Charlotte Corday pinking Marat (Bellew)
as he takes his tub. The Potter-Bellew always
clung to the undressed drama, but the stage spec-
tacle of Bellew in a bath takes the hot-cross bun.
“ Hero and Leander ” went closest to the knuckle
before, and a Sydney d.c. was almost petrified
once when it really looked as if something had
gone wrong with Kyrle’s blue bathing-scarf.
* *- * *
Fillis’ Circus remains, as it was last week,
a commendable and popular entertainment. The
Bulletin, however, has begun to realise the im-
possibility of saying anything new about circuses
and hereby for ever gives up the attempt. The
lion never strikes upon any new reading of his
part, but simply drags his head and tail in the
dust in the same old way, and the other beasts
give a painstaking representation of their res-
pective characters but lack versatility.
** * *
Excepting for the fact that George Rignold
has taken the Irish part of the vanished Sweeney,
there is no change to note in the hunky melo-
drama at Her Majesty’s. G. Rignold in a broguey
character is a novelty ; also, in some sense, a
revelation. These statements aren't intended to
commit The Bulletin definitely, this paper not
having made up its mind about Mr, Rignold’s
brogue, and being still open to conviction. The
great feature of the “ Shamrock and Rose ” is Mr.
Harcourt’s Irish bailiff-man. Harcourt is the
most realistic and brutally-repulsive, but withal
humorous, Irish villain seen on the stage for years.
** * *
The Brough and Boucicault Co, returned
to Sydney on Saturday, and even the big Lyceum
was hardly capacious enough to hold the crowd
which gathered to have another look at “The
Amazons.’’ Pinero’s comedy looked as bright as
on its first appearance, save in one particular—the
substitution, as the mother of the three Amazons,
of Miss Romer for Miss Edith Blande. Miss Romer
doesn’t look large or muscular or horsey enough,
or sufficiently bounceable to fill the character.
Miss Bessie Major, however, is an improvement
on the previous sergeant, which makes up.
Otherwise the comedy is the same as of yore.
Boucicault’s Frenchman and Cecil Ward’s high-
born idiot are still the two most finished per-
formances of their kind that The Bulletin re-
members.
** * *
“Paul Jones,” for all present and early
future production, is an opera handicapped by
memories of the original Australian cast. The
revival of a popular piece with a greatly changed
company invites severe criticism, for the reason
that first principals, if well placed, become identi-
fied in the minds of audiences with the characters
they assume. First principals are best principals
until they be superseded by others much more
convincing. There is no denying that the com-
pany picked for “Paul Jones ”(by Nellie Stewart),
in the first instance, were judiciously selected.
Marian Burton, with her fine presence and, at that
time, rich voice, gave a touch of poetical interest
to the hero. Snazelle, an unctuous comedian and
singer, was an amusing contrast to Leitch, whose
crushed “ Insective ” appearance provided full
scope for the de haul en has manner of the longer
man. Aggie Kelton, although scarcely equal
to Chopinette’s music, had a bouncing
coquettish way with her which contributed more
to the general success than Violet Yarley’s one
successful song. In other important parts Satur-
day’s revival at Melbourne Princess’s showed two
slight improvements and a couple of distinct
changes for the worse.
# * *
J ohn Gourlay was the bounding Trocadero
of old, quite as nimble and acceptably grotesque
as before. He doesn’t act the Spanish potentate
or speak his lines with any regard for the circum-
stances they relate to, but his vigorous clowning
seems to justify itself, and he makes the last act a
mad joke. Miss Nellie Stewart having lost her
top notes through overwork, Miss Young took her
place as Yvonne, and scored considerably. In
fact, Miss Young was very good up to passion-
point, where she always stops dead. Physically
wooden no longer, she is spiritually hard, and her
separations from Paul were borne with unneces-
sary fortitude. Ryley was a picturesque Jones—a
gallant baritone —anardentwooer —a thing of manly
beauty. Yet a handsome contralto lady, capable
of wearing the breeches with easy grace, is an
even more fascinating personality than Ryley in
all his glory. Moreover, the baritone’s voice is
rather heavy for the music, and his ballads were
not warmly received on Saturday. Tapley did
well in the unthankful villain capacity, also the
uncle who dozes over his Aegus was safe in the
hands of Howard Vernon, and the chorus and
dancers came fairly up to tradition. The inevit-
able disappointments were Lauri and Courtneidge,
as Bouillabaisse and the Insect. Art is unable to
make the one comedian several inches taller than
the other. Lauri didn’t suggest any constitu-
tional scorn for his follower, whilst Courtneidge,
not being bullied, seemed conscious of having no
real purpose in life. Probably they will work out
an effective scheme of relationship as the nights go
on, and arrive at the point of their dialogue by a
new route. When they operated on level terms,
as in the “ Mariners Bold ” duet, they rose to the
summit of the opportunity. “Paul Jones” is
down for a two weeks’ run, and should have
plucked up spirit all round by the time this
sermon is printed.
** * *
At the Alexandra they carried “ The
Shaughraun ” over Saturday with flying colours.
Easter Monday, however, was to bring “Uncle
Tom’s Cabin ” and a long catalogue of special
attractions suitable to the moss-grown narrative of
that coloured martyr. Many negroes, both real
and blackened for the occasion, were starred on
the bill. Altogether Uncle Thomas promised to
have his sorrows alleviated by more variety than
usual, so he ought to die cheerful.
** * *
“Struck Oil” turned money away from
Melbourne Royal on Saturday. Likewise “the
performance afforded every satisfaction to a
crowded house” in accordance with time-honoured
custom. “Struck Oil” is a drama that ostensibly
turns upon certain papers hidden under the
hearthstone, but it really turns a good deal upon
the popularity of Maggie Moore, the other portion
of its revolution being conducted by a fat re-
minder of Rip Van Winkle. Oily Deacon Skinner
—who in this instance discloses his hypocrisy to
the orchestra with excessive candour —is mainly
the man who greases the machinery for Lizzie
Stofel and her fader. Of Maggie Moore’s oft-
told impersonation one may say that it is
better than when last seen at the Royal, in-
asmuch as she looks less matronly, an im-
portant matter even in the case of an artist
whose art remains as young as ever. Perhaps
there is a slight sense of “popular prices ” about
Miss Moore’s gaiety. She may lay the fun on a
little thicker than our old nobility would like to
have it. All the same, Maggie Moore goes
straight to the heart when required, and the glory
of her mouth organ is unabated. John Forde’s
nasal version of the Dutchman has few convincing
features, his second wife is scarcely the sort of
young woman to accept him for a husband, and
the voice of Augustus Glover is like unto a melo-
dious lion lifting the roof off the wilderness.
Deacon Skinner, despite the fault aforementioned,
reflects credit upon J. P. West. The scenery also
reflects credit upon somebody, and “The Chinese
Question ” rounds off a huge financial success.

You are threatened, says The Bulletin’s London cor?
respondent, with a visit from another of the “hasbeens’
—old Sims Reeves, to whom overtures have been made for
a concert tour in Australia. Poor old chap, it is not his
fault that Nature has asserted herself, and that he no
longer has a voice ; but his admirable method and his
charming mode of singing even the simplest songs makes
it a treat to hear him, especially after listening to those
whose vocal attempts consist principally of sound and
fury.

the manager who has to deal with 10 or 12 ladies, each of
whom thinks she should have been cast for Rosalind or
Orlando 1

There is one Downes singing in London, just now, who
announces himself as “ the coloured Australian tenor.”
Charles Harding tackled him, the other night, after
having dined well, with “Who the are you to call
yourself an Australian tenor? You are neither an Aus-
tralian nor a tenor.” “Keep your hair on, old man,"
replied the coloured party, “ I have as good a right as
some people to call myself an Australian. Why I was out
there nearly three weeks.”
To advertise his show a provincial English manager sends
up a rocket every night and admits the person who finds
the stick. We have nothing like this here. In Australia
you don’t usually find the stick till you get inside.

Rosina Vokes—the Firm wanted to import her for a
season, but negotiations fell through—will never make an
Australian tour, because she is dead. Her age is given as
36-a few years under the mark—and she was. in any
case, decidedly the youngest of the Vokes Family. When
they first played “Belles of the Kitchen,” and Rosina
appeared as the slavey who chipped in with an illumin-
ated saucepan representing the “pale moon”—“Pale
moon, arise !” said Fred Vokes, and up went the sauce-
pan—she couldn’t have been more than 15. How the
Family did sing and dance and throw themselves about in
those days ! Only one of them, Victoria, is left to tell
the tale ; the other four—Fred, Jessie, Fawdon and jolly
little Rosina—have gone under, or over. The gods loved
them and they died young.

case. The central interest of the play turns upon the
theft of the sacred fire from a temple by Izeyl, a lady of
easy virtue.

Pretty Florence Saville, writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, is a rival of Sir Boyle Roche’s bird. A
musical critic recently told me that she had sung a
few evenings before at a city glee club concert. I
believed him, and duly mentioned the fact. The
next day I read in the papers that she had, the same
night, made a great hit in grand opera at Monte Carlo or
Nice, I forget which. It’s all right, though, about Melba
being engaged for the Handel festival at the Crystal
Palace, and it is also true that her flat in Paris has been
denuded of effects by her caretakers, and also that she
has been reconciled to Marohesi—that is to say, if she ever
fell out with her teacher, as was reported.

When John Rogers (Mr. Minnie Palmer) was in Aus-
tralia as manager for the fluffy little soubrette, he bore
the reputation of having more gall than all the rest of the
world put together. Jimmy M‘Mahon was advance-agent,
and on one occasion he was on some distance ahead filling
up the local editors with the most awful assertions con-
cerning Miss Pairner which his vast imagination could
supply. Suddenly he remembered having once heard a
strictly true story about a shot that entered the window
of a railway carriage in which Minnie was sitting, and
seeing material in it for more advt. he wired John for
particulars. The reply came back promptly : “Pull par-
ticulars by post. If can’t wait, explain Minnie only
actress visited by Queen, Prince and Princess Wales and
all nobility. Minnie regular visitor at palace which caused
riots in Ireland, last August, rioters firing on train.”
John was never an artistic liar, but for Oriental gorgeous-
ness of fiction he had few equals.
A well-known actress, and a very medium actress at
that, was going up the lift, the other day, with several
other people, at a big Sydney hotel, to her rooms on an
upper storey, when she discovered that one of the bar-
maids was among the group. Thereupon she sent for the
manager and intimated that she wasn’t accustomed to go
up lifts along with barmaids, and unless these girls used
the stairs in future she must leave the hotel. Conse-
quently, the tired Hebes now toil up four flights of stairs
to their rooms just under the roof, and think kindly of
that very medium actress at every step.
Organist Best, lately in Sydney, has resigned his post
as organist at Liverpool St. George’s Hall. He has heart-
disease badly and is 68.
W. J. Lincoln writes from London to say that “The
Bush King ” isn’t ghastly, and w’asn’t cursed, and didn’t
deserve to be cursed ; and quotes a number of alleged
press notices which convey very moderate eulogy of its
merits. The average London critic, with a dim belief
that Queensland is the capital of Melbourne, and the
kangaroo a most ferocious kind of cannibal, cannot be
blamed for failing to realise the unique horrors of the
average Australian melodrama.

Dear Bulletin,—All very well for their Parlia-
mentary pals to agitate for the release of the jugged
legislators of bank and building society notoriety, but
one has to live in a working-man suburb like Balmain to
see the hideous ruin brought,'about by these institutions
and their crooked-minded promoters. In my house are
three children penniless. Yet their father left them a
comfortable sum invested before his death in So-and-So’s
bank because So-and-so was a prominent man in church
and lodge circles. Over the way a woman Ins sold her
little home, sent her eldest boy to sea, and gone to ser-
vice herself, because of another legislator's connection
with these banks. Only that these rascals lent their
names for a consideration, the swindles would have been
comparatively harmless. To release imprisoned lawmakers
at the request of lawmakers, some of w'hom have not yet
been properly found out, would be as reasonable as to re-
prieve Bill Sikes on the petition of Toby Crackett.—V.

A Melb. bank the other day issued notices of

* * * •
The Bulletin makes the Corpulent Person a
present of the most shining example of thrift yet dis-
covered in the shape of a bank clerk at Port
Douglas (N.Q.) who lives on 3s. 4d. a week, and publicly
brags about it. This lusus pecuniae boards himself in an
old humpy and feeds on bananas and rice, with the result
that he is able to rise up at hospital committee meetings
and denounce the extravagance of patients who cost the
community 7s. and Bs. a week. We won’t be ruined by
Chinese cheap labour while such heroes flourish. The
bank should put the interesting specimen in a glass-
case and send him round to encourage its customers.
They, poor wretches, have to do from necessity what he
does from choice.
