The A.M.P. office in Collins-street, Melb., is
upon the exact spot where John Batman erected his first
house. The A.M.P.S. has had a large copper panel affixed
to the front wall recording the fact.
♦ » * *
Sydney Stock Exchange holds several sittings
daily, and the announcement, “ No sales were recorded at
Sydney Stock Exchange yesterday,” is almost as regular
as clockwork. Some 60 sraileless stockbrokers walk up
and down Pitt-street with books which are seldom opened,
and between ’Change sit wearily in their offices awaiting
clients who never come. In Melbourne, in the midst of
this, the greatest known financial slump, that gorgeous
pile, the Stock Exchange, is, at any rate, put to some use.
Scores of tenanted offices in one part of it, the big cham-
ber where the brokers congregate and do business in
another, hundreds of jobbers who pay for the privilege
rendezvous in the vestibule, and the club, which consists
of over 2000 members, keeps the basement bars and res-
taurant lively. Over 400 members lunch there daily, and
the lunch is admittedly the best obtainable in Melbourne
for the money. Melbourne is still the financial centre.
♦ » * »
The first result of MTlwraith’s visit to England
is the formation of a syndicate for the construction of two
out of the multitude of land-grant railways, with which
Queensland is to be gridironed by-and-bye. The only varia-
tion from the original scheme which the syndicate insists
upon, is that it is to have all the land on both sides of the
line at the rate of 3500 acres for every mile constructed,
the State giving up its claim to retain alternate blocks.
The reason for this condition is that the State might sell
its alternate blocks at 10s. or £1 an acre, and then the
syndicate would have to do likewise, or else retain its land
until Govt, areas were disposed of. Under the proposed
arrangement, however, the British capitalists will have no
competition, and will charge the miserable Australian
settler just as much as they can screw out of him, without
blocking settlement altogether.
* * « *
Melbourne Mutual Store held its half-yearly
meeting of shareholders the other day. It was a pleasant,
refreshing meeting, one-half football “scrum” and one-
half cats’ convention. There was no div. and a loss of
about £l7OO. When the chairman had made these state-
ments a fellow-director casually called him a liar, or
words to that effect. A minute later the chairman was
called a liar again, and presently was alluded to as
an old fool. Then the same director described the chair-
man once more as being, to all intents and purposes, a liar,
and someone else arose and called everybody a liar pro-
miscuously. Just about this time the chairman grew
suddenly excited and offered to bash a director over the
head with the water-bottle, whereupon the meeting rose
upon its excited hind legs and yelled “ You’re a liar 1”
and “ Shut up !” and “ Chuck ’im out 1” for the space of
five minutes. When order was restored somebody in the
body of the hall raised a point of order about a tortoise-shell
kitten, and while he was doing it the board started a fear-
some row on the platform, and two directors called each
other liars eight times over, and shook their fists at each
other. They were dragged asunder with difficulty, and
aftersixvot.es of censure had been moved and lost the
meeting broke up, while one director was calling
for somebody to bring him half a brick that he might
bounce the same off the skull of a colleague. The aver-
age Melbourne shareholder is soured by misfortune these
times, and the harmony of the good old days, when 10
per cent, dividends were declared and the proceedings
wound up with fizz, has altogether departed.
The Federal Coffee Palace (Melb.) called a
meeting of anguished teetotallers last week. Loss for the
half-year, £ll6B and some odd coppers ; this, added to
previous losses, makes £3BOO to the bad. Also the board
has lost all hope of paying off, out of profits, the £10,781
still remaining of preliminary exes. ; that amount being
carried to general losses, makes £14,585 in all. Only a
grog-license can save the desolate white elephant.
Ginger-pop spells insolvency.
* * ♦ ♦
The Melb. Y.M.C.A. building is about to be
seized by the mortgagees, and unless the saints pay up
they will be cast out into the street. The Y.M.C.A. is
another victim of the boom. Some years ago it sold its
building in Russell-street for £20,000, raised £lO,OOO by
subscription and £25,000 on mortgage, and spent the
whole £55,000 upon a garish tabernacle in Bourke-street.
Now it is £2OOO in arrear with the interest, and the
tabernacle will probably hardly fetch enough to cover the
mortgage. The Christian young men have gambled
away their old premises and all their ready cash, and
have absolutely nothing left.

Lazzaroni—nobody thinks of paying anybody.”

“ Whether Victoria has her lottery or not, it is
not likely to make the slightest appreciable difference in
the barometric rise or fall of her national morality.”—
The Age. Exactly. Having swallowed a camel, what
matters a gnat ?
* * * *
Boring for coal at the Bluff (Melb.) is conducted
by a spiritualist under instructions from a “medium.”
The spirit’s revelations as to ground strata were suffi-
ciently practical to find a colour of truth at the first
boring, but of late the medium, or his ghostly advisers,
seem to be making mistakes. The enthusiasm displayed
by the Age over this wild coal chase has a somewhat
uncanny appearance.

History— i.e., post-strike history—repeats itself.
Junction directors, finding that the Port Adelaide smelter
hands accepted the proposed 10 per cent wage reduction
without trouble, extended it to Broken Hill, the alterna-
tive being mine-closure. Other mines will follow suit.
The second reduction since the strike.

Wyalong is already overmanned. The condition
Imnn”' wa3 . ne ver better shown than by the fact of over
7000 men rushing away to a new quartz-field where the
reefs, (hough numerous, are small, and the shoots short,
though, so far, very fair in gold. Already there are

will soon set in.

The cry of gold’s a human lever,
That raises dual evils—
The min ing and the miner fever,
The worker and the weevils.
Again is told the story old,
The bait and the designer ;
The miner crushes out the gold,
The pubs, crush out the miner.

* * * *
Things are said to be looking a little better at
the Spa, another of the Shoalhaven sluicing oo.’s. John
Wallace, David Syme, and others know they will stand
improvement. Over £40,000 has been lavished on this
concern, and so far the hope of ever seeing it again is
remote. Ground quite good enough for gravitation, but
not rich enough to stand the great cost of lifting millions
of gallons daily.
* ♦ * ♦
The “Eclipse” patent gold-saving machine,
manufactured by R. T. Ball and Co., of Goulburn, N.S.W.,
is the latest thing of its kind, and has impressed the
public at show-trials. The proprietors claim that it can
put through 20 to 25 loads a day with a minimum of
water, that it saves all free gold without quicksilver, that
it works by hand, steam, or horse-power, and that it can
be made to deal with any variety of wash. It is 6ft. 6in.
long and 2ft. 6in. wide, weighs scwt., and can lie trotted
about in an ordinary spring-cart with ease, also the price
is only £35. The proprietors challenge the “Jerger,” or
any machine, to a public test—which, if it comes off, will
leave things very much as they were. Show tests answer
no practical purpose. What the country wants is to have
the “Eclipse” and the “Jerger” and all their rivals
carted out to some poor but honest alluvial field, and
there put through a few days’ steady work by practical
miners. The result will be a lot more interesting than 10
years of exhibition at county' shows and the like.
• • * *
Carcoar gold claims are looking well, and Kind
and party, at Flyer’s Creek, have some exceptionally
rich stone. Milchell’s Creek crushed 249 tons for 198 oz.
This mine should pay handsomely if worked on a big
scale. Mount Gahan, at Pambula, 255 tons for 92 oz. At
Canowindra the Lo ulon reef has struck good gold at
281 ft., and the shareholders’ smiles go right across the
horizon.
* * * *
Shoalhaven Consolidated sluiced 10,000 yds. for
about 30 ozs.—poor, but payable with a gravitation water-
supply. Manager Froggart still holds that a very slight
percentage of gold is lost. If he is right then the ground
is much poorer than was believed.

The yields of Low’s Log Paddock freehold,
Mudgee, N.S.W., must be a grotesque disappointment to
those who believed the newspaper reports about it, “If
they only managed to get down and keep the water out,”
&c. They got down. Latest—“ Washed up 10 fathoms—
-3 ozs. 1 dwt.”

At Hillgrove, Sunlight crushed 575 tons, 2240 z.;

* * * *
Golden Diorite : “Tributors are on splendid
gold. Cannot say if likely to continue.” You never can
with any Pambula mine. Rich as Croesus to-day ; poor as
Lazarus to-morrow.
Of course, all MTlwraith’s attempted land-grab
railways have to be submitted to Q. Parliament.
The most inviting field for such jobs (jobs they cer-
tainly will be under the Q. Act) is the big N.Q. section
from the Towers to Normanton and Herberton to Clon-
curry. This is the richest mineral country in Australia—
not even excepting Coolgardie. There are scores of
proved shows worth, in the aggregate, hundreds of
thousands, for which the owners have refused good offers
in the hope that the railway will touch them some day.
If syndicates get hold of this country it will be the
biggest loot on record. And if N.Q. miners let them get
hold of it (on M'Hwraith’s terms) they will be the biggest
fools on record. Only Separation can end the present
risk, and the miners are gradually getting sense enough
to see it. Yet even now the Charters Towers Register,
the mining organ of the biggest Australian mining field,
opposes Separation from jealousy of Townsville. Nose-
cutting, face-spiting!

Now that an undoubtedly payable gold-reef has
been discovered in Sud-Est, British New Guinea may
become perforce a white-man’s country, instead of being
locked up in the interests of a few quasi-imperial billet-
holders and the trading missionary who, under the
lamb’s-skin of gospel-patter, hides the wolf-shape of the
commercial buccaneer.

♦ * * *
Awhile ago “Joe ” Parkes, Inspector of Mines,
reported enthusiastically on a new gold find at Mount
Pleasant (S.A.). A rush ensued, but the diggers could
not find more than a few specks, and nearly lynched Mr
Parkes, who is an old Bendigo digger himself. Never-
theless, he stuck to his first opinion, but the field was
deserted until lately, when a syndicate found rich gold
in ironstone, and in a month turned out £15,000. They
are now making over £4OO a week.
* 0 * *
During the Plattaburg (N.S.W.) mining strike,
the local council (mostly miners on strike) decided that
the Co-operative Coal Co.’s tram-lines were dangerous,
and that, having been laid without legal sanction, they
should at once be uprooted. These lines cross several
streets, and were they permanently removed the mine
would be isolated and made worthless. Next day the
lines were torn up and the embankments levelled by the
council’s labourers; and now Plattsburg Council is in-
volved in a big law-suit with mine-owner Laidley, who
claims that by the Council’s year-long acquiescence in the
existence of the lines, it is practically out of court.
* * * *
A correspondent writing to The Bulletin from
Coolgardie before the rain :— Dear Bulletin,— Coolgardie
is not a poor man’s country. It cost me £6O from Ist
Sept., ’93, to Ist Feb., ’94, to batch—yet I "don’t
booze.” When the “ wet weather ” really sets in, it gives
three months supply to prospect with. Men camp hero
not for what can be fossicked, but to be on hand in case of
a rush, though an odd one gets a fluke now and then. I
really believe everything carries gold here—clays, slates,
washes, quartz, ironstone, hematite. One needs to use
division and subtraction in dealing with reports from this
field. Yet a man viewing it with a glass eye would per-
ceive it good enough for investment. When a miner buys
the Westralian G. P. Act, he gets Is. worlh of false
representation and contradictious, as the officials impro-
vise law without worrying the legislature.
Lord Percy Douglas has returned to Coolgardie.
He and party brought a patent dry-blower made in Eng-
land—a useless thing, in my judgment, after seeing it at
work. It is a typical J. Bull machine: one man feeds, the
other grinds, and the gold drops into a money-box sort of
affair with lock attached. They evidently have their own
opinion of Australians.
The only things to be had gratis at Coolgardie
are salvation and mice. These later are prolific. The old
’uns turn young ’uns out so fast that they can’t find time
to bore holes and they make extempore nests in bread, &c.
A great number suicide in the water-buckets - as luxurious
an ending almost as the ancient royal double murder in
the Malrasey-butt.
The latest novelty is a wheel with a frame on
which is packed Bcwt. It is propelled by four men, two
in front and two behind. We have donkey-teams with
waggons, and packs, camel teams, horse teams, bullock
teams, human teams; all we now want is teems of rain.
They have uncovered a couple of decent gold-
bearing reefs which cross the dray-road from Coolgardie
to Bayley’s.
’Tis a bad omen—those jackaroo miners. Their
sympathies are all with the blacklegs, and we know what
that means.
Should the rush anticipated occur, there may be
a repetition of the terrible Kimberley disaster, but
far more severe. You should advise the cookies and
teamsters to stick to their properties, in N.S.W., Vic., or
elsewhere, for ’tis a passing phrase here that we can go
to where they come from.
We have six candidates for Yilgarn electorate,
and judging by the questions put to some of them who
have addressed us they will know we want no luke-warm
representative.
A man on his way to Siberia, hearing how
things were, stopped near Coolgardie, took possession of
a camp (the former owner having gone on)and fossicked
for miles around. Seeing some colours got just above his
tent he started near his abode and raked in about 30oz. —
15oz. of which were raised from beneath his bunk. The
other day an Irish new-chum enquired of an old hand as
to the most likely place to try, and was directed to a spot
where heavy gold had been found. On his way home he
was asked if he had got anything, “Just a bit of a
speck,” replied Mick. It proved to be a 950 z. speck.
There are about 10 genuine leases “ known ” up
to date out of some hundreds, but there are lodes that
bog the intellects of experts, and I am of opinion that
some of them will turn out real swingers.
There is nothing up to now to justify a big rush,
Hannan’s and Coolgardie are the leading features to date,
and these two places turned out, when at their best, 75
per cent, of stiffs. Of the others, Black Flag was but a
patch of surfacing of 150 oz.; Broad Arrow, one good gully
followed on to surface, thence to reef which shed same ;
White Feather, one good gully with fair surfacing from
reefs ; I O U, a very small patch with good leader ; 90-
Mile, fair gold scattered with good reefs ; Siberia, a little
gold, hard to get on ; Yindi, said to be a one-gully find.
Now, considering the distance between these finds, the
time one has to try them, “ three months out of twelve
months,” and the spaces between being a network of
prospectors’ tracks, it is not on the whole promising for
diggers or would-be diggers. You have had the “ toff’s ”
view ; this is the tramp’s view.—Fossicker.

In one week over 1400 gold-fevered passengers
left Melbourne for Westralia, and 3000 others are already
hooked in advance to follow.
* * * *
The granting of 12 days’ exemption at Cool-
gardie to enable leaseholders to get men would seem to
indicate that wages-men were scarce. Nine out of ten
miners who go to Coolgardie will prefer prospecting for
themselves to working for others even at £4 a week.
* ♦ * *
The alleged striking of the reef in Bayley’s in
the 200 ft. cross-cut, and the declaration that the stone is
as rich there as elsewhere, caused the shares to jump
from 18s. to 275.; then they fluttered back to 20s. The
tendency before announcement was decidedly backward ;
had it not come for another week, the stock would pro-
bably have gone back to 15s.
* » » *
There was some consternation in Melb. last
week when it was rumoured that E. J. Dunn, late of Vic.
Mines Department, had condemned several Murchison
mines. Now said that Gibbs, Bright and Co. have declined
to take over the Emerald mine, Yalgno, owing to Mr.
Dunn’s adverse report. Dunn is a capable man, and the
writer wishes him luck as an examiner, because learned
geologists have been most unfortunate as experts. More
than one good geologist condemned Mount Morgan, and
several praised Taranganba ; Bayley’s was unfavourably
reported on by a geologist. It is rarely t'-at geologists
succeed in doing what Dr. Peters undoubtedly did at
Mount Lyell—seeing beyond the point of the miner’s pick.

A Coolgardie digger complains that the inside
arrangem nts of the horse are altogether on a wrong

daily ; a small “ shout ” means 55., a “ long-sleever ” half-
a-sov.
* » * *
A new-chum, named Arthur Baker—it is always
the new-chum that finds the nugget—has got a 140-oz.
piece at Kurnalpie.
* * * «
The United Coolgardie directors and share-
holders are at a loss to know why an attempt has been
made to jump one of their leases, Ritanita, on the ground
of non-compliance with labour conditions. Ample funds
have been from time to time forwarded, and advantage
was not taken of the general exemption granted during
the dry weather. Whether they retain or lose the lease,
jumping is a good sign of value. Most of the big mines
of Australia have at one time or other been jumped, but
it is hard to name one where jumpers have come into
possession. Bibby, who holds the transfer of the lease on
behalf of the syndicate, has gone to Perth to see what’s
up, thence to Coolgardie to take over other properties
secured by the syndicate.
* * * *
The W. A. Day Dawn G.M. Co. association has
issued its prospectus. Capital, £220,000, in 110,000 £2
shares, of which vendors take all but 10,000, which have,
it is declared, been applied for several times over. The
£20,000 is to be used in increasing the milling plant and
in opening up what appears to be a big mine ; anyhow,
the capital is quite big enough for a start. It is owned
and bossed by the Broken Hill crowd.
♦ * * «
The story goes that only 6000 shares in Bayley’s
South were sold at the float; and getting rid even of that
number was a tough job. One evening they were, say,
175.; next day, after the big reef was struck, they were
selling at a fiver —and the syndicate had in its bag all
the balance between 6000 and the total number! A
clear case of men making money against their will.

• * * *
A number of correspondents ask The Bulletin
what is the truth about the John Jerger gold-saver.
The exact truth can only be ascertained by use
under actual working conditions. “Show’’-tests are
utterly unreliable. Some fossicker who has long been
working poor but steady ground by other means could
tell one all about the John Jerger in a couple of days.
* * * *
The Melbourne Freetraders, whilom so com-
pact, have sadly jarred. Murray Smith, Langton, H. R.
Reid, Stevenson, and the rest, are at sixes and sevens in
enmity, through financial disputes. Banks, Manchester
Lane Warehouses, Mutual Stores, all provide fuel.
* » * *
The Melbourne Fat party are scheming
again for a railway to the northern suburbs, where the
decrease in population is greatest. Anything to stem the
flow of unemployed townsmen towards the soil which
waits tor labour. Melbourne has already got too many
suburbs, and too much railway, but another million of
money (“raised locally on debentures”) is wanted, to be
expended on the reproductive work of connecting these
played-out districts with the almighty city. Fortunately
there is little likelihood of the job coming off.

T. A. Richard, an Amer. mining man who
lately travelled Australia and now writes in the Engineer-
ing and Mining Journal, describes the percussion-table
used throughout Bendigo, as a concentrator of very im-
perfect type. Strange that amongst the great batteries
of Bendigo (one 105-heads) none other is used. With
skilful manipulation—and every concentrator needs
attention—Halley’s percussion-table will do as clean work
as any concentrator, and in first and working cost it is far
at'-ad of many.

“ The men I am afraid of are the men who

The oldest Australian church is St. Luke’s,
Liverpool, N.S.W., dating from 1819. Its earliest
registers bear date 1811, and the first entries are
signed by Samuel Marsden.
A young American doctor claims per-
manganate of potash to be an infallible specific for
poisoning by opium and its preparations. He
first swallowed 3gr. morphine—lgr. is a fatal dose
—and then 4gr. permanganate in water ; no ill-
effects resulted. If solid fact, it is a great dis-
covery.

lent by Sir Henry Loch—is sufficient of English

Ex-millionaire philanthropist Charrington,
interviewed at Sydney, has given the fruit of his
mature experience on the Australian labour ques-
tion. He thinks the Sydney wharf-labourer can
“make good wages.” Much he knows about it.
Is an average of under 30s. weekly, of which, say,
11s. goes to the landlord, called good wages ?
N.S.W. Labour electoral leagues are using
heavy powder over the “municipal platform”:—
One-householder-one-vote, with six months’ resi-
dence proviso ; popular annual election of mayors;
Saturday elections, 8 a.m. to 5 p.m., poll to be
declared same day; rates to be recoverable from
owners only ; compulsory registration with local,
councils of property-ownership, vendors to furnishr-
before sale, proof of title to municipal solicitor,
plan of subdivision to be approved by councils,
etc. ; munioipalisation of lighting, water-works,
etc.; municipal works by day-labour; reserves
vested in councils, and plebiscite to precede en-
croachments thereon or alterations therein ; plebi-
scites to precede council loans; free public libraries
and reading-rooms, etc.
The Bulletin rises bo ask, for the second
time, whether the various Australian Govern-
ments are going to put up the supall amount
required to bring back the seceded “ New Aus-
tralians ” who are now hopelessly stranded, with
their wives and families, in Paraguay. A few
months ago these Governments were joyfully
unanimous in their willingness to spend many
thousands, without Parliamentary authorisation,
to entertain a bored prince and his bride.
Queensland cheerfully pays a big salary to
Hume Black to act as a sorb of immi-
gration lecturer, cheap labour “ad.” and land-
grant railway - boomer in England. Victoria
has lately “blewed” a lob of money in
paying the expenses of Premier Patterson’s lec-
turing tour throughout the country and Robert
Reid’s hazy “ mission ” to Europe. N.S.W., not
so long ago, fooled away immense sums in making
roads through some politicians’ private estates. A
mere fraction of the cost of the cheapest of these
fads would do all that is required. If this country
leaves Britain to pay the expenses of rescuing
and sending back its distressed citizens, or if it
abandons them in Paraguay, it will deserve to be
the scoff of every civilised nation. And whatever
is to be done should be done quickly, if it is to be
of any real service.

Personal Items.
David Blair has written nine lives of Capt.
Cook.
A Queensland editor : “ Hoolan and Glad-
stone have resigned.”
The book to be written by a Queensland
M. describing his travels, will be called “The
Drake’s Progress.”
Premier Kingston has gone to Westralia
over a big “ timber ” case, representing a wealthy
syndicate, and to prevent litigation.
The late John Macfarlane, M.L.A., was
father of the current Queensland Licensing Act—•
the most drastic anti-publican law in Australia.
Anglican-Primate Smith, who, with his
three daughters, left for England per Orizaba,
last week, denies that he is about to get married
again.
Miss Shaw, of the Times, has a stamp-
oolleotion hobby, which applies to Govt, frank
■tamps. While in Australia she secured complete
sets of the local issues.
Canon Chase, the Melbourne Anglican
clergyman who has, perhaps, been longest in local
harness, is a charming old gentleman—the very
model of Irving’s Vicar, in “Olivia.”
Lowe, a brainy member of the Age staff,
which he is now leaving, wrote up most of the
anti-Speight articles. He is a brother to the
editor of St, James’ Gazette, and his sister
female-edits the Queen.
The veteran surgeon Fisher, of Melbourne,
died last week. A couple of years ago he figured
as defendant in a sensational abortion-case, but
managed to pull through. Local artists lose a
good friend in Dr. Fisher.
There are now in England, living in clover,
over 30 ex-“ colonial ” Anglican bishops. Your
Episcopal dignitary, unable to bring the squire
along with him, cannot appreciate the free, un-
sectarian air of Australia.
Gov. Duff says that during his residence in
N. Wales he has been asked to weigh a baby,
to throw a boomerang, and to grant an immediate
divorce. And being a particularly ignorant
Governor he didn’t know how to do any one of
them.
Varney Parkes purposes to earn his politi*
cal spurs if elected to next N.S.W. Parliament.
Hitherto he has given the best of his mind to
money-making, admitting that the spectacle of
his father deterred him from depending upon
politics.
A reformed South Australian drunkard
talks of presenting his saviour, Miss Jessie Acker-
raann, with JJIOOO, in order “to secure her a
home in her old age.” But maidens of
mann type never have an old age, except in their
teething childhood.
In a Swanston-street (Melb.) window is
displayed a framed series of portraits of Australian
democrats, labelled “J. Smith,” “J. Brown,”
and so on—with one exception. The central
portrait is “R. J. Seddon, Esq.” “Democrat
Esquire !” ye gods !
The residence in Melbourne, some time
back, of the former lover of the late Prince
Imperial of France probably accounts for the
recent presence of a cup “ presented to the Prince
Imperial by the ladies of Chiselhurst ” in the
window of a local pawnbrokery.
The Sydney never-touch-its “congratulate
General Neal Dow upon attaining the 90th anni-
versary of his birth, and upon being spared by our
Heavenly Father to witness,” &c. ; also, by
inference, they congratulate the Almighty on
having so spared Dow. Further, by more in-
sidious inference, the grog-seller who should attain
his 90th year will have been spared by Beelzebub.
When McCosh Clark was Mayor
of Auckland (M.L.)he received a depu-
tation urging the repeal of the Contagious
Diseases Act. These he called a “ pack of
old women.” Asked why he had so
insulted them, he explained that they
vwere past bearing.
When supposed to be for ever out
of N.S.W. politics, A. G. Taylor was
one day asked by Willis, M.P., to
“come and see friend George” (Dibbs),
Dibbs was absent, so Nick took his
chair at the Colonial Secretary’s office,
and inquires, “ When shall I sit here?”
“Ah,” replied Taylor, “some time
aftsr it goes to the second-hand shop
—maybe.”
King Leopold, of Belgium (who
was mentioned in the Stead case), dis-
appeared the other day. Later on, his
Majesty was found in Switzerland no
doubt in select company. This latest
royal vagary recalls the story of a for-
mer drinky N.S.W. Minister of Lands.
When a certain Cabinet was formed
there was included in it a bearded in-
dividual of shady morals who, however,
didn't drink much. “Hullo!” exult-
antly exclaimed the bearded one to a
brother politician, as soon as the list
came’ out, “I am in the Govt., after
all!’-*' - “Yes,” was the reply, “they
couldn’t well do without you. Blank
is also in it, you see. When in office
before, he was always getting lost, and
they never could find him when he was
wanted, but he’ll never get lost now,
for you know every (Saxon word) in
town.”
John Howard Payne, who wrote “ Home,
Sweet Home !” never had a home, and George
Macdonald, the author of “ Annals of a Quiet
Neighbourhood,” is the father of 11 children.
And Henry Parkes impresses on Forrestralia the
necessity of purity in public life.
There are three Vic. Cabinet Ministers
who can deliver a sermon as well as a speech.
Patterson has been disporting himself at several
Melb. evangelistical churches; Baker preached
lately in the Cousin Jack holy circles around
Long Gully ; and Richardson has not been idle.
The Maori King, Tawbiao, sent a message
to Premier Seddon that he would be glad to meet
him at Kihikihi; to which Seddon answered tha b
he would be glad to meet the king at another
place—Hamilton. Neither potentate would give
way, so they did not meet. The white man might
gracefully have conceded the point. A bigger man
would have done so.
JACKY JACKY, the Dora-Dora Black.
If rumour speaks truly much influence i s
being used to secure knighthoods for two retiring
S.A. Civil servants : Samuel Deering, Assistant
Agent-General, and G. W. Goyder, Surveyor-
General. Each will also receive some thousands
of pounds as compensation for the loss of his
position.
“Tom” Slattery, as N.S.W. Minister for
Justice, has absolute power to release prisoners or
keep them in gaol. The Governor will, as a matter
of form, sign any recommendation the present
Minister for Justice forwards. In his two last
terms of power Parkes reserved this right for him-
self or for the Cabinet. He distinctly withdrew
it from his Minister for Justice.
New York Evening Sun deliciously places
Dame Margaret Windeyer, “ the wife of the
Senior Colonial Justice of New Zealand,” as the
leader of the Woman’s Suffrage movement in
Maoriland, and says of her daughter that, when
she “got hold of a vowel, she gave it three or
four twists before letting it go.” However, she
couldn’t have given it as many twists as the Sun
gave the real facts.
Outgoing Vic. Railways - Commissioner
Murray has publicly shown his soreness at
parting with his post. His wails savour of
Mr. COLQUHOUN, the new N.S.W. Crown Solicitor.
want of courage, as his regime, it is claimed,
was noted for the number of surreptitious dis-
missals of men with Speightian sympathies.
Kibble, Francis, and Murray appear to have
been most antagonistic to their predecessors,
and the discharged railway men, who look upon
themselves as having been sacrificed for their love
of the old master, are chuckling over the vengeance
that has overtaken one of their alleged destroyers.
When the late Dean Backhaus (R.C.) was
stationed in Adelaide during a lengthened drought,
the other denominations were praying for rain and
asked the doctor to join. But his German rever-
ence knew too much : the barometer was too high
just then. He watched for a fall in the glass, and
when it came he publicly prayed, getting the rain
and the kudos, too. •
Matthew Davies, after his
acquittal, received nearly a van-
load of congratulations from
Governors, Judges and other
bigwigs, from whose mind a
great weight was lifted when
their brother aristocrat escaped.
The jubilee of the estab-
lishment of the biggest S.A.
milling firm will be celebrated
May 1. Strange to say, the
founder of the business, John
Dunn, is the only survivor of
the four partners who ran the
concern in its younger days, and
he is 92 years old. This month,
at the Mount Barker Show, ho
w.on first prize for sheafed hay
with part of the crop cut from
a paddock over which he drove
the second Ridley reaper ever
constructed, more than half-a-
century ago.
Queen Victoria, although
indifferent to the age of her
bonnets, and careless about the
hang of her dowdy dresses, is
a martyr to high-heeled boots.
No elderly lady in the wide
world wears her heels higher
than Vic. Regina. On State
occasions, when she has to stand
upon a hidden hassock, with a
view to impressing her loyal
subjects, the Queen may sport
easy slippers, as also when she
is riding through the streets
perched upon a mountain of
cushions, but for the sake of
consistency she must add an
inch of leather to her ordinary
walking or standing stature.
The poor old lady was born
with abnormally brief under-
standings, and she does her best
to live up to the fictitious figure
which the English t. lustrated
press has conferred upon her.
Robert Reid, the Southern imperialistic
drummer, shortly after being appointed Minister
of Defence, was driving pasta detachment of local
militia and as lie drew near it happened that the
order to change arms was given. The soft-goods
magnate, perceiving the evolution, took it fora
salute in his honour, arose in his carriage, and
solemnly lifted his belltoppor, much to the amuse-
ment of the Tommy Atkinses who, unaware of
Reid’s identity, put it down to the action of an
affluent looney.
Annie Besant, who comes
in August to boom Theosophy,
has a handsome face, though
nearly always sad in expression.
She invariably dresses in loose
tea-gowns, never wears corsets,
and can, without notes, talk un-
ceasingly for hours about spooks.
She is always more or less sure
of her facts, and has been known
to digest a cab-full of books for
a single lecture. Her hair has
the knack of changing colour.
At breakfast-time it is often
black, bub an hour or two of
proof and letter - reading will
make it a snowy-white.
The retirement of the
gilded Pastor Talmage, the
Most Travelled’s latest lion (“as
was to be ”), from the pastorate
of Brooklyn Church is, perhaps,
due to the fact that his violent
style of preaching has for long
rather bored his flock. The late
fire in the Tabernacle, it is
averred, was due to the burning
character of his eloquence. Thus
(from his sermon on the Yel-
lowstone region): “ The cliffs
present the upholstering of the
Lord God Almighty . . Torrents
of light tumbling on floors of
darkness, auroras crouching
among the rocks, sunrise and
sunset married in a cathedral
of basalt, with a ring of rain-
bows.” Then, “ Jehovah seems
to have passed Himself in the
grand canon.”
The King of the Belgians
has been addicted to secret
sprees of the sort just cabled
about, for the last thirty-five
years. He’ll die incognito, more
likely than not.
Hamilton Clarke, speechifying in London
on Victorian music as “one of the survivors,”
relates his meeting with the Mayor of C
The latter, an individual in shirt-sleeves, held his
hand over a dirty bar and handed Ham. a tumbler
of the “vilest whisky he had ever tasted.” Most
improbable, this. No Australian Mayor would
have handed him a tumbler of whisky. He would
have passed the bottle. We don’t measure out
people’s liquor in this beastly country.
Premier Dibbs (N.S.W.) : “I have never
been tight in my life.”
Says Crick: “ I’ve been tight, boys, full many a time,
And, many a time, do I hope to be more so.”
Says Dibbs: “ I have never been tight; it’s a crime
I avoid, and there’s nothing on earth I deplore so.
If it came to a bet as to which was most able,
In fair-fashioned duel of drinking at sight,
To drink his opponent clean under the table,
I’d bet on the fellow who ‘never was tight.’ ”
Ifc might have been told of Dismal Jemmy
Garvan, M.P. (N.S.W.), of “sacred and solemn
duty” celebrity. “ A Kentucky Congressman has
a sad way of speaking when he wishes to be impres-
sive, which sadness always is intensified in the last
part of his speech. Tom Reed came into the
House the other day while Breckinridge was
winding up a speech, and listened for a moment.
Then he turned to General Cogswell and said :
‘ Can you tell me the name of the deceased ? ’”
There is a symmetrical similarity of curve
about the portraits of N.S.W. racing-men pub-
lished by Sydney Telegraph. “Mon Dieu!
quelles protuberances 1” as Max O’Rell said of the
deep-bosomed Dianas of Britain. But the protu-
berances are all in a healthy spot. In fact, Syd-
ney’s prominent racing-men seem to realise the
physical ideal which Dr. Anna Potts marked out
for her egg-shaped woman—broad in the middle
and tapering to the extremities. And why not?
Parkes not long ago declared that he would
never again go before an Australian jury to ask
for justice, his implication being that prejudice
against him would blind some of the members to
the facts of his case. On its being pointed out to
him that one-man-one-vote made Parliamentary
elections trials by jury, hu referred to the
“majority” element in this latter matter, and
indicated that similar verdicts in legal cases
would be an improvement. But, then, majority
verdicts are taken in civil legal cases.
No unvaccinated person is allowed to vote
in Norway.
The Melbourne papers now invariably
allude to him as the common hangman. Lord
bless the system that makes the hangman
common!
The Bulletin is assured, and is requested
to state, that it is quite untrue that Mr. R, A.
Price, a Parliamentary candidate for the Glou-
cester (N.S.W.) electorate, ever moved a vote of
confidence in himself at any meeting of electors.
A striking feature of N.S.W. Agricultural
show was the big clock erected by Stewart Dawson
and Co. in the top part of the main pavilion over-
looking the whole of the grounds. The very large,
clear dial could be easily seen from any part, a big
improvement on previous years.
Linotype type-setting machines, if gener-
ally adopted, will, in Sydney alone, render work-
less several hundreds of compositors. At present,
type-setting costs 13d. per 1000; the machine is
expected to do the work for under 3d. The differ-
ence, barring interest on the cost of machines, and
depreciation and repairs, goes into the pockets of
the newspaper-proprietor, to whose income thou-
sands annually are thus added at a swoop. It is fully
expected by the trade (in fact, it has seemingly
been hinted by the importers) that, pending other
arrangements, one at least of the evening papers
will be set at the office of its morning contem-
porary : “ The machines are not to be idle in the
daytime.” Seventy compositors employed at
Sydney D.T. office are all to have an equal
chance of learning to use the machine,
under the tuition of an imported expert.

LIKELY ENOUGH.
Those who show most proficiency will be
retained, up to the nujnber of (including foremen,
,&c.) about 25 in all; the rest will have to go—
Heaven only knows where, for there are already
hundreds of “comps.” outof work in Australia. It
is understood that the acceptance of work is to
involve the signing by each operator of a practical
renunciation of trades-unionism. If the machine
has come to stay, then the trade of compositor
is dead, for, in the ordinary course of things,
the machine will soon be worked by
women at fourpence an hour. At present, it is
understood that the Sydney operators are to get
Is. 6d. per hour for a minimum of 7000 “ems,”
with a seventh extra for any excess. In America,
it would appear, the operators get half-a-dollar an
hour.

Mrs. M. Blakley, Wilson-street, West Wallsend,
N.S.W., who writes on February 14, 1893
About two years ago I was very much troubled
with dyspepsia, pains in the back, and continual
headaches. I was never free from severe fits of
retchiner, everything of which I partook seemed
to turn into water. At last I became so bad that
I was advised by the doctor.who was attending.cn-
me to go into the hospital. Having a young
family it was impossible for me to do so. I believe
I tried nearly every remedy, but received no relief
until my attention was drawn to the great cures
performed by Clements Tonic. I began to mend
before I had finished one bottle. I continued
taking it for some time, and I am happy to say it
thoroughly cured me. It was not a temporary
cure, but a permanent one. You are at liberty to
make what use you like of the above statement, —
Yours gratefully, Mrs. M. Blakley, Wilson-street,
West Wallsend, N.S.W.

Melbourne Argus devotes a column-long
sub-leader to the great old men of the world, and
never once mentions Parkes. It is petty jealousy
like this that retards Australian Federation.
Civil - servants are heavily pensioned,
because, often occupying a certain position, they
cannot step down. How could Cabinets, Parlia-
ments, municipal councils, be run on such a
principle ?
Wesleyan Parson Maddern proposes that
his church should “set apart a man to enter
N.S.W. Parliament and look after its morals.”
And who would look after the morals of that
man who is “ set apart ’ ?
The Poonindie mission station, Port Lin-
coln district, S.A., reserved for the use of the
blacks half-a-century ago,; is now being cut up into
working men’s blocks for their hardly less poverty-
stricken white brethren.

Government would have come into power. Next

The Cabbage Garden definitely resigns
supremacy. Hayter has long done his best to put
a good face on things, but he is getting old now,
and admits that N.S. W.’s population is 1,223,370,
and Victoria’s only 1,174,022. So he has to make
way for a younger man.
Sam Hordern and Alderman Meeks, both
of Sydney, are spoken of as among the likely
Dibbsian nominees to N.S.W. Legislative Council!
If these are samples of Dibbs' I iemocrats, imagina-
tion cannot conceive what the Earmarker’s
acknowledged Tories would be like.
Matthew Davies is one of the principal
reasons why the Victorian Government doesn’t
call Parliament together. The “ politics of the
Mercantile Bank case” were suppressed in the
Law Court, but the dread of their coming up
again in the ’Ouse is always before Patterson’s
eyes.
The half-dozen Democrats left in Melb.
are staggered by the news that ex-Railway Com-
missioners Kibble, Francis and Murray are re-
spectively to be pensioned on £7OO, £6OO and
£6OO per annum. With the other hand, Patt.
retrenches the sick pay of the ill-paid school-
teachers.
Where were the signatures of the 100 or
so N.S. Wales Ms.L. A., who now petition for the
gaoled bank-directors, when the Broken Hill
miners and other strikers were imprisoned ? Most
of these then said that it was injudicious, if not
reprehensible, to interfere with the course of jus-
tice, and that it would be altogether wrong for
members of Parliament to cabal for such a
purpose.
The Age, in justification of the federal
movement:—
To preserve the continent for the white man is the
general desire ; but there is an obvious temptation to the
capitalist to call coloured labour to his aid in the sub-
tropical colonies.
Black’s not quite white since the Argus became a
penny.
Gladstone’s farewell address to his consti-
tuents warns Democracy that it is entering upon
a period of great moral danger, and urges that
precedence be given to the interests of the whole
instead of part of the community. That’s pre-
cisely what Democracy is doing. British Govern-
ment hitherto has been Government for the
classes only ; now it is to be Government for the
masses.
A well-informed Maoriland correspondent
to The Bulletin
Stout’s entry into Maoriland politics is widely suspected
as treachery to the democratic wave now carrying all be-
fore it. He is the hope of the re-actionaries—of this
there is no doubt, and he cordially detests Seddon.
The Tories are championing Stout, and he shows no dis-
like to their doing so. You will find my words confirmed
by time and events.

It was reported that the Postal Conference
had agreed to raise the interprovincial newspaper
postage to Id. for the first 40z., and for each
additional 2ozs. The Age makes Premier Patter-
son thrill with horror at the bare idea. “As a
matter of fact,” remarked Mr. Patterson, “ the
intercolonial postage on the Age newspaper would
be doubled. Altogether it is an absurd proposal,
and will not be tolerated by the Government.”

ment Labour Bureau, urges the adoption of the
Maoriland public-works policy of letting contracts
to gangs of unemployed at schedule prices. If
this is done, Government should beware of com-

co-operatives must combine naturally, appoint
their own overseers and gangers, and frame rules

The Bulletin invites attention to the
detailed report in N.S.W. Hansard of the
Assembly debate re the shocking Reseigh-Martin
prosecution law-costs scandal, disclosing an almost
unprecedentedly shameful legal imposition on the
Treasury. Barrister Reid, Opposition leader,
posed as really indignant at the legal grab ; but
let anyone follow and read between the lines of
his speech and say whether any lawyer can be
relied on as turncock in the flushing of the legal
gewer.
Wagga (N.S.W.) branch of the Amalga-
mated Workers’ Union has appointed a Central
Works Committee, whose duty appears to be to
tender (co-operatively) for public works and other
contracts. Wagga branch has agreed to advance
50 per cent, of the contract-price on all contracts
undertaken, receiving as security meanwhile the
contract agreements, vouchers, etc. This idea, if
developed wisely, is capable of achieving grand
industrial results.
The Vic. Government’s conscience ! It
hanged Mrs. Knorr for sending innocent babes to
Paradise ere they could be sin-stained in this
wicked world. It has commuted the death-
sentence, passed upon a man who violated a little
girl, to 10 years’ imprisonment, with three flog-
gings of 25 lashes each. The brain-convolutions
that can devise such subtle distinctions in the
penalties prescribed for variations in crime should
furnish an interesting study to psychologists.
A N.S.W. statesman, giyen to making
protestation that nowhere in the world is there
greater freedom than in Australia, admitted
lately on cross-examination that when he used the
word “ freedom ” he meant the power confided to
the governing party—“ freedom,” as he said, “to
do what we Ministers like.” The fight for liberty,
according to him, is a fight to enable the represen-
tatives of the Crown to govern the country “ on
our own broad sense of right and wrong.”
“One of my subjects” —was a phrase that
dropped quite naturally from Gov. Duff’s tongue,
at Sydney Animal Show, last week. “ One of my
subjects, asked me to grant her an immediate
divorce.” So that all N.S.W. citizens, in Gov.
Duff’s opinion, are Gov. Duff’s subjects. Let this
be remembered as the inward naturalness of a
Governor’s opinion concerning himself, his posi-
tion, and his relations towards Australians, when-
ever his nonentity and mere figureheadedness is
again broached. “ One of my subjects ! ”
Afc Dunedin, Wellington, Christchurch,
and several other Maoriland towns, the number of
public-houses is to be reduced one-fourth. Dunedin
was just 18 votes short of absolute prohibition, but
the victory of drought and cold tea will doubtless
be achieved next year, and meanwhile even 25
per cent, is something. Port Chalmers also just
missed absolute prohibition. Oamaru and Bal-
clutha actually got there. There is no other town
on this earth that is quite so desolate as Balclutha,
and now, when thirst is added to its other
miseries, it ought to bo an excellent place to
die in.

Clement Wragge has taken to naming the
storms which come along after the letters of the
Greek alphabet, and that of last week was ac-
cordingly christened Epsilon. Thissounds learned,
no doubt, but the man who is out in Wragge’s
storm would quite as soon Wet Wragge stuck to
plain English and named it “ Disturbance Hex.”
Mr. Holman writes :
Dear Bulletin, —Your par. about my‘‘reported utter-
ances ”is un-BuLLETiN-esque in two ways. It’s not up to
date and it’s not fair. It’s not up to date because six days
before it appeared I gave the explanation clamoured tor
at the end ; and gave it in the same paper as the
“utterances” were originally published in; and it’s not
fair because it is a repetition of a lie that has been thus
already nailed down. I can only regret that my
explanation should have escaped- The Bulletin’s all-
seeing eye, and meekly repeat it here.
Ist.—l have to explain why I didn’t sign the printed
pledge-form of the Central Committee. I didn’t do it
simply because I was selected at Leichhardt two weeks
before the printed form was issued, or even suggested.
But I did sign a pledge which in every particular agreed
with the form afterwards issued (which form was only
meant as a model, any way). Is that satisfactory?
2nd.—l cannot explain what I meant by saying “that
the pledge was impracticable, but that 1 woujd work on it
all the same," because I never did say it. This is a “re-
ported ” utterance. I used that phrase about a p/oposal
to extend an Eight Hours Day to all occupations, and
the intelligent reporter, or his editors, grafted it on to my
remarks on the pledge.
3rd.-—I have to explain that you are manufacturing
confusion on the pledge business. The November Confer-
ence laid down the essentials of a pledge but no form of
words. Any form containing such essentials is constitu-
tional. In answer to numerous requests the Central Com-
mittee drew up a form satisfactorily covering these
essentials. But every candidate has been left free if he
please to draw up a more grammatical or more poetic
form of his own, provided that it is right on the essen-
tial points. The one or two that were rejected were so,
not because their form was'different, but because they
were wrong on essential points.
4th.—l have to' explain that Mr. G. D. Clark not only
refused to sign the “solidarity pledge,” but also, which
was the immediate cause of what you call his “ downfall,”
refused to pledge himself to stand-down if not selected.
This was the real trouble in his case, though the solidarity
pledge would have been also demanded had Mr. Clark
given this one.
As to my “bigotry,” my “inconsistency,” and my
“ piratical ” manners, those are matters of’ opinion, upon
which my own information being more complete and
reliable than the fellow’s that supplied the paragraph, my
opinion is likely to be as good as his. Thanking you for
the opportunity of reply.—Yob rs, W, A. Holman.
IF GLADSTONE DIES.
If Gladstone dies?
How long will this forgetting world remember ?
Until the next ensuing Days of Ember ?
Not so. The storm’s forgotten with its thunder ;
Dead demi-gods don’t last a nine-days’ wonder,
The dingoes scramble for the vacant show,
The world doth wager on the dingoes—so,
If Gladstone dies.
If Gladstone dies ?
Where is the chasm in the cosmos ? Where
The vacuum ensphered within the air?
Would space, suppose you, miss its brightest sun ?
Or would the orbs, unheeding, onward run?
Stab water and withdraw ; now, find the gap
Your dagger made. Just so will be the hap
If Gladstone dies.

All through the heavy rains of last week a
man and .four .children slept huddled up in a hand-
cart, with an old blanket, at Windsor, in
sunny N.\S. Wales. The poor devil was a
widower out of work, and wheeled his family
round the country in the hand-cart, begging for
subsistence And the cable states that Agent-
General Samuel contemplates a special trip to
Italy to buy (for £11,000) a statue of Governor
Phillip, the first N.S.W. gaoler—an obscure naval
officer) by-the-way, of whose personal attributes
absolutely nothing is known. This monstrous job
is a legacy of the Parkes Government.
* * *
A New Guinea missionary lately told his
Sydney audience that he called one of his converts
a pig and a dog for taking two wives. It was the
only way, he said, to bring bis sin home to the
benighted heathen —but all the same, it appeared,
the darkey hung on to the extra girl. Which
recalls the story of the Maori chief whom the mis-
sionary tried to wean from polygamy. “Is not
the Bible true ?” enquired the chief. “Yes,” said
the missionary. “ And was not Solomon the
wisest man who ever lived ?’’ “ Yes.” “Well
then Solomon had 600 wives. Kapai Solomon !”
■ * * *
After refusing a board of enquiry to ex-
Constable Hewlett, who was dismissed for making
serious charges against his superior officers, the
S. A. authorities have been compelled by public
clamour to alter their decision. They let them-
selves down lightly, however, by the pretence that
Commissioner Peterswald, who will be seriously
compromised if the accusations are proved true,
has asked for a board. The chances of fair play
are very premising, seei»g that the subordinate
was thrown out “ on his ear ” for daring to open
his mouth.
* * *
Jobbery, writes a correspondent, fairly
ramps in the Westralian police department, A
sergeant, Houlighan, who has seen 14 years service
—mostly in the tropical N.W.—lately applied for
a change on the ground of ill-health. It was
readily granted ; an intimation reaching him that
he would be henceforth stationed at Perth where
he would rank as corporal. The sergeant sent to
replace him had been exactly 14 days in the force,
but be happens to be a brother to Gov. Robinson’s
private secretary.
* # #
A recent Christchurch (Maoriland) fashion-
able marriage was conducted on the “rational dress
system;” in other words the bride and brides-
maids all wore knickerbockers, vests, and coats.
But they didn’t go in for the plain, serious-looking
male article, as is evidenced by the following
gorgeous description
Mrs. Wilkinson wore stone-blue bengaline coat and
knickers, cream gold embroidered vest, a wreath of
natural flowers, and bridal veil (ye godsl knicker-
bockers, orange-blossoms and bridal veil !—it sounds
like stewed rhubarb and mashed potatoes on one
plate!), draped prettily, but'not covering the face as
usual. Miss Walker wore a suit of lemon merveilleux
satin, the close-fitting tunic being trimmed with rich lace
and edged with a niching of itself; the general effect
somewhat suggestive of a Russian skating dress. Mrs.
Burn wore a suit similar to the bride’s, hut of gold-
brown cashmere, the vest trimmed with broad silk em-
broidery, and the edges of the coat finished with gold cord ;,
to my taste the most effective postume of the whole. Miss
Parry’s suit was of a navy blue cloth, the vest trimmed-
with gold tinsel gimp, and the coat edged with gold cord.
Miss Meredith’s suit was of a crimson corded stuff, and
the coat finished with silver cord and the vest with broad
cream silk braid. The gentlemen of the party, with two
exceptions, wore knicker dress also.
Pity there isn’t a little more information about
these two male “exceptions..” Did they wear
skirts and tall hats, or what?

Dear Bulletin, —I have a grievance. I
live in a suburb of filthy Melbourne, where typhoid
is carted around in disgusting pans, where cows
lap dp infection for their milk from open drains,
where houses are permitted to be built on foetid
foundations of filled-in swamp, and where each
municipal dignitary, from the Mayor of Melb.
downward, is an accessory before the fact to a
shamefbl death-rate. Of late lam harassed by an
elderly policeman, who calls once a week or so, to
know whether the baby has been vaccinated. He
is seemingly a pompons believer in smallpox-
scares, and a man without any useful purpose in
life. He has my sympathy. The grievance afore-
said is not against this misplaced myrmidon, but
against the authorities who command me to have
sores produced upon an infant in a poisonous atmos-
phere. Vaccination as a counteraction to smallpox
may have served a good purpose in Jenner’s time.
Jenner is dead, and the most cogent arguments in
favour of his discovery are dead also, yet vaccina-
tion, under wholesome conditions, may be desirable
even to-day. But is it not monstrous that the
Law, which denies us reasonable protection
against typhoid, diphtheria, and other aggressive
evils, should affect to be so concerned about an
effete disease that is conspicuous by its absence?
We are individually compelled to take dangerous
precautions against non-existing Small-pox by the
very people who are responsible for existing
terrors. “Get your baby vaccinated or go to
prison,” say the authorities. “Reduce the
dangers of vaccination by doing your duty, you
official fever-mongers,” I reply. Meanwhile, that
monotonous “ bobby " is hammering at the front
door.—Yours, E.F.

Significant “ad.” from Sydney Evening
News :
TZ"IND Lady to Adopt Respectable Baby from birth ;
expected middle April. Mrs. May, G.P.O.
This “ad.” opens up “awide field for conjecture.”
What is “a respectable baby ? ” Also, can any
baby be respectable? A baby is an unfinished
being without manners, who confidentially
dribbles at the mouth when spoken to, and who
dabs his bread and jam on the pants of total
strangers. Also, he looks up chimneys, and
he sits down in pools of water and com-
plains bitterly when lifted out, and he dis-
respectfully throws crusts at his parents. If an
infant can do all these things and yet be respect-
able, then what on earth does he do when he isn’t
respectable ?

* * *
Mr. Panton, P.M., Melb., to a small boy
efficiently arrested for being a vagrant:—
The only way to cure such a case as yours is to apply
the cat. Boys who are allowed to behave as you have
done are on the straight road to become criminals; indeed
you are criminals already, and a source of expense to the
country. We will give you three months in gaol.
Mr. Panton would cure boys of their criminal
inclinations by giving them the accepted brand
and mark of the vilest criminal. He probably
would also try to cure short-sightedness in youths
by poking out their eyes. When the writer went
o school, his teacher, when he convicted a boy of
Untruth fulness, used to stand him before the class,
labelled: “This boy is a liar.” After that it was
found the boy always acted up to his reputation.
Now that master, grown old, points with pride
to the fact that three of his pupils are lawyers,
two tried to be, and one is in Parliament.
The right-thinking people who love to point a
moral, with a cat or a soaped rope, will regret to
hear that Victoria has been deprived of a grand
example. W. Johnson is not to be flogged. He
was sentenced to 25 lashes for gross indecency, but
being found, at the last moment, to be a hopeless
paralytic, he escapes. An explanation is offered
as to the reason for this exception in favour of the
paralytic by a system that has cheerfully hanged
or mangled scores of diseased criminals.

The Sydney 22-footer IREX.
she went down on her bended knees and begged
and prayed to he let in.” His orders were to

caretaker with discharge. He had locked the
lodge-gates, and retorted that he would only take

Then he went back to his plants, and as the
Governor couldn’t think of climbing the wall, he
had to stay outside. That is the sort of man to
leave in charge of your property. Ho is like
a 371 b. bull-pup named Satan, formerly owned
by a Sydney man, who took off his overcoat one
day in a billiard-room and left Satan to watch it.
Satan sat down on it and declined to budge until
his master came back two days afterwards.

The idea that what is known as the French
method of detecting criminals was never employed
in Australia is not absolutely correct. Fifteen
years ago the evidence against a burglar in Sydney
consisted of the impression made by his thumb on
a piece of soft putty on a pane of glass.

EDMUND FISHER
A LATTER-DAY EPIC.
Perhaps the banking catastrophes of the 19frh century
may form the subject of an epic poem in the 20th.—
Brisbane paper.
The bankers came down like a wolf on the fold
And swore that their coffers were bursting witli
gold ;
And their balance-sheets shone with a varnish of
lies,
Like the glamorous gleam of a pair of black eyes.
And the knowing ones carted their money-bags in,
Arid the Verdant ones came With their fists full of
tin ; |
And the bankers they bade ’them be cal mi and
content, ’' \ ■>. |
And count on a divvy of twenty per cent, v !
So they counted on divvy’s and'castles in Spain
While the land was aflood With the finest cham-
pagne,
While the whisky like rain in a rivulet ran
From Beersheba’s bounds to the borders of Dan.
But the public awoke from its slumber at last,
And burst on the banks like a southerly blast;
Caucasian, Kanaka, and Chinaman John,
They went for their dollars—their dollars were
gone.
And the widows and orphans are loud in their
wail,
And the public is calling for justice and gaol;
But the might of the hanker , altho ’ he be down,
Is more than a match for the might of the Crown.
His raiment is glossy and sleek as of yore,
And bland is his smile and as broad as the door;
And he chuckles and chuckles when no one is nigh
And winks at the Judge as he passes him by.
So drink if you will to the bandits of old,
To burly Bill Sikes and Barabbas the bold;
But fill up your glass to the brim when I say
“ Here’s a health to their masters —the knights of
to-day!”

P.L.


TO THE LADIES.
It is generally admitted that you, ladies, have on
your shoulders the responsibility of the household,
and the care of providing for the welfare, at home,
of your husbands and the children.
You no doubt act up to the best of your abilities,
and yet there is one pertinent question which we
should like to ask you.
Do you give your children a drink
OUT OF THE TAP
when they are thirsty ?
If you do, you are running a fearful risk, especially
in the country, of bringing unwelcome visitors into
your home.

# # *
A Melb. man who, two years ago, was
worth .£50,000 in cash, now sleeps regularly in
Fitzroy Gardens—when he isn’t moved on by the
police. He is only one of a multitude—the great
multitude of decayed, red-nosed men, with
greenish tail-coats, and broken boots, and their
hair sticking up through broken hats, who ask you
in a voice of fog and gin if you “ don’t think
there is going to be another boom ? ” There will
be another boom, no doubt, but not for them.
Very few men g. t two chances in the land-boom
industry. When the next frenzy comes along
younger men will run the swindle, and the old
boomers will elbow each other in the soup-kitchen,
and swap lies about the allotments they sold and
the banks they ran in the garish dayaof ’B7-
• * ■
Perhaps the most remarkable feature of the modern
world is the di gree in which men have mastered the
secret of keeping their working power to an extreme old
age. . . . The Pope is in his 84th year. The Queen
keeps her working power and keenness of intellect un-
broken at 75 years cf age. The English Commander-In-
Chief, the Duke of Cambridge, is 75. —Melb. Argus.
Newcastle (N.S.W.) is very Republican,
and, save in newspaper reports, anything ap-
proaching enthusiasm among the vast majority of
its people over a vice-regal visit is ever conspicuous
by its absence. It was curiosity and nothing
more that brought a crowd to the local railway-
station on the occasion of Gov. Duff’s recent
arrival. The people, largely composed of unem-
ployed miners with time hanging heavily on their
hands, went to see Gov, Duff, just as they would
roll up to see the circus lions arrive; and not to
welcome him, as the papers put it.
None of the papers reported the enthusiasm
attendant upon Carington’s first visit to the coal
city. He alighted from the train; the crowd was
there, but it had only come to see, and raised
never a cheer. Then a railway-porter, a bit of a
wag, stepped forward and addressed the silent
crowd : “Now then, you lazy , give the poor
Governor a cheer ! What else do you think
you’re made for?” And then there was enthusiasm,
heaps of it, with the genuine ring about it, and
yet none of the papers noticed the incident. lut
the porter was promptly dismissed.

Ip the course of a recent Sydney divorce-
case, Judge Windeyer practically started to sool
the co-respondent on to the private detective—at
least, it sounded very much like that. In this in-
stance the detective burst in a window and put his
head through the gap in search of evidence.
“Such a proceeding,” said Mr. Windeyer, “ was
illegal, and a person doing that might run a very
good risk of being dealt with as a burglar—tha
was, being shot by the inmate of the house.” As
the inmate, on such occasions, is generally the co-
respondent this opens up a new view of the case.

A curiously humorous Sydney newspape r
incident of last week was the fact that an M.P.,
lately mixed with a divorce scandal, signed his
name boldly to a series of articles, among which
was this quotation from Tertullian re the vogue of
common property-holding among the old-time
collectivists :—“ All things are joint property with
us except wives.”
A Maorilandress, one of the
newly-henfranchised, now visiting Syd-
ney, was asked how she had approached
the voting question at last general
election. “Well, you know, there
were two candidates,” replied the
lady, “M. and N. George was sup-
porting N., but Mrs. So-and-so told
me that he quarrelled with his wife and
stayed out late o’ nights. So I told
George I was going to vote for M.
So George said ‘ Well, we quarrel some-
times and sometimes I do stay out
rather late, but I suppose if I were
standing you’d not vote against me ? *
So of course I had to agree—George
always could reason so well—and I
voted for his man. But poor Susan
—that’s my servant —I thought that
as she wasn’t a married woman she
needn’t vote for George’s candidate, so
I got her to vote for M., and you
should just have heard George when I
told him at tea-time ! ”

A handballer has been fined
for Sunday play in Melbourne—Mel-
bourne, the loathsome sink of financial
corruption, where colossal daylight robberies go
unpunished, and the knuckly fingers of sainted
swindlers squeeze the throat of the Law.

The retrenching Minister Baker (Vic.)
lately ukased that all married women in the Edu-
cation Department must go. Indignation
and hysterics followed. Then the order
was changed, and it was made known that
all female teachers with large families,
invalided husbands, drunken husbands,
and husbands who were absent in un-
known parts might remain. Now it
transpires that the husband of every
second matron in the Department is
either a frantic dipsomaniac with
chronic snakes in his hair, or a hopeless
loafer, or afflicted with something in-
curable, or else visiting Central
America or the Argentine Re-
public. The maiden ladies throw
up their billets for husbands;
the married ladies throw up
their husbands for billets.
Evidently men don’t improve
on acquaintance—any more
than do women.

Because the She-Gimlet climbs into the pulpit
and crows from that roost. Witness Palm-Sun-
day’s doings in Wollongong (N.S.W.) Wesleyan
church.

Melbourne is what Hobart used to be in
point of marriageable girls.
Rue Australia seems easier to reach than
to quit. Its Lane is a cul-de-sac.
* m *
A married lady writes to a Melbourne
paper to complain that, owing to the hurry and
general high pressure of modern life, she seldom
sees her husband, and is almost deprived of the
pleasures of his society. There are, she says, 168
hours in a week. Of these he spends 54 at busi-
ness, 15 in “evenings out,” 56 in sleep, and the
remaining 43 in her society. The 56 hours sleep are
seemingly not spent in her society. Evidently
this lady is really writing to the papers about the
evils of the single-bed system, concerning which
Chief Justice Madden so learnedly discoursed the
other day, but doesn’t like to say so.
They are about to abolish the old cemetery
at Williamstown, quite an ancient institution,
where interments took place over 40 years ago.
It was within the boundary of this historical spot
that John Price was battered to death by the
convicts.
• # *
A Sydney witness last week set down his
vocation as “pastoral student.” What’s the
matter with “ jackeroo?”
# # •
k At Barcaldine (Q.) a local citizeness was
accused of having stolen certain spokes,
the property of a wheelwright. Found
guilty and fined 10s. or one week. But
the cells in the local limbo were already
occupied by male offenders and prisoner
was penniless. Whereupon the local
police promptly subscribed the 10s. and
sent the lady home rejoicing. Find the
moral.
* • *
The members of a Sydney news-
paper staff are such very good friends
that they lend around to one another
the new books which they handle. This
amity has, however, disadvantages ; for
sometimes the same author will be
quoted or alluded to in nine different
parts of the same issue, and it has
happened more than once that the commendation
he receives in one column will be discounted by
reprobation in another.
* * •
The Adelaide police band is under orders
to go and play to the lunatics once a month.
# • *
A wealthy Sydney sporting man of trade
who has been entertaining the Bong-tong lately,
says he is afraid he must give it up. You can’t
lend money to everybody.
AN OPTICAL ILLUSION.
The development of the male conscience
keeps step with the march of woman-franchise.
Todd, a Maoriland civil servant, has just suicided
leaving behind him a card on which was written
that he was doing the deed “ because of a lovely
woman to whom he had broken his promises, and
to whom he now made a thousand apologies.”
The Maoriland Salvation Army has estab-
lished an Index Expurgatorins, and lights public
bonfires where Besant’s novels are burnt in com-
pany with “ Bluecap the Bushranger ” and “ The
Green Dingo of the Western Plains.” Even
“Robert Elsmere” and “Barabbas” are de-
nounced, and the only undamned exceptions so
far are “Pilgrim’s Progress ” and “Uncle Tom’s
Cabin.” The old Puritan religion-run-mad again.
Why not burn a few witches to keep things
humming ?
# » •
The children of Israel were never so much
in evidence as they are at the present time in
Melb. The daughters crowd the Block and fill
the dress-circle of the theatres, and the sons seem
to be the only remaining “club men” in the
Southern capital.
On 31sfc Dec. ’93, there were 3377 public-
houses in N.S.W., or three for every thousand
persons.
For Fitchett : “ There was a man once—
a satirist. In the natural course of time his friends
slew him, and he died. And the people came and
stood about his corpse. ‘Retreated the whole
round world as his football,’ they said indignantly,
‘and he kicked it.’ The dead man opened one
eye. ‘But always towards the goal,’ he said.”—
Maarten Maartkns.
“It is not who does a thing, but whether it
is done well, that is the important matter for
an intelligent community to consider.” Thus
Pilcher, Q.C., at a Sydney social gathering. Yet
the same Pilcher helped to consolidate the rules
under which a Parramatta attorney’s clerk was
fined a big sum, with a gaol-alternative, for
doing work, admittedly well, but for not being the
legal “ who.”
THE ROYAL SECRET.
They do not linger long below
Who live but lazy lives.
The slothful navvies come and go—
The active “toff” survives.
And stoutly as the ivy green
Maintains a crumbling tower,
So doth the never-resting Vic.
Preserve her working power.
The common soldier passes hence
(Killed, maybe, by a shell),
But Gampbridge, thanks to Providence!
Is always doing well.
The gutter loafer, pale and mean,
Dies off unlike a flower—
His Sov’reign Lady, bright and keen,
Preserves her working power.
The city boss, amassing wealth,
Outstays his threadbare clerks.
No “ hunemployed ” has half the health
Of good old ’Enry Parkes.
Longevity is labour’s prize—
In point of fact, its dower.
Could England’s Queen do otherwise
Than keep her working power?
The drones who dig for coal, or gold,
And in the dampness grope,
When spoken of as “ very old ”
Are younger than the Pope.
Death is the wage for jobs we shirk ;
Before that thought we cower.
The Queen, by sticking close to work,
Preserves her working power.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The clever division had a disappointing time at
the opening day of the A.J.C. Autumn meeting.
Patroness, a six-to-four favourite, made every post a
winning one in the Two-year-old Handicap, after which
her people just escaped a coup with old Marvel in the
Autumn Stakes. He was quietly backed at 20 to 1, and
getting well away established a lead which was not
wrested from him until after the distance, when the
favourite, Light Artillery, took command and won by a
couple of lengths. Thespian, at 10 to 1, won the High
Weight Handicap ; and the St. Leger came on the hoard,
for which the public thought Carnage had only to come
out and win. They backed him down to sto 2on accord-
ingly. At the distance Carnage was leading, but the
hearts of backers sank when Sailor Prince passed him and
won by a couple of lengths. Mr. “Bill” Long’s Gerard
started first favourite for the Doncaster, but was unable
to run into a place, Mr. “Bill” Forrester’s Donizetti
winning without a dollar of stable money on him. So
much for the judgment of the two most.knowing “ Bills”
on the Australian Turf. One thought his horse, who
hadn’t a ghost of a show, could win, the other won with
a horse which he didn't think had a ghost of a show.

In the Adelaide intercollegiate boat-race for the
Blackmore Challenge Shield, on 17th March, the St.
Peters boys were seven lengths ahead but stopped rowing
ere they had actually breasted the post, and the Alfreds
drifted past first. The umpire awarded the prize to the
latter, but they chivalrously declined to take it, and
handed it over to St. Peters. Only boys would do that
sort of thing.

Dear Bulletin, —“ W.,” in endeavouring to put
others straight, falls into error. Your contributor did
not refer to fights between big and little, but to fights
between stocky, muscular men and those of the lanky
(Fitzsimmons) type now so successful. In wrestling,
weight and strength tell still more. Then he compared
the average glove-fight and the average P.R. affair—not
exceptional cases like the Smith-M'Carthy. Mace was
not the only scientific fighter of his day. Otherwise, how
could Goss, no bigger, give him such tough battles, and
Brettle, much smaller, beat him ? Then there were
cleverer men with the gloves, but little thought of in
those days. To say that King equalised matters with
Mace is pure rubbish. In their second, as in their first,
meeting, Mace was winning easily, and became careless.
A big man, even to the last, has a terrible smashing left,
and, Mace being off his guard, King landed the ever-
memorable blow. But nothing could induce him to again
face Mace who persistently challenged him. Bendigo—a
little man ! Why he could fling a brick across the Trent
at Nottingham-a feat atw’hich Donald Dinnie would not
sneer. Heenan was strong on his feet, certainly, but did
he not go blind five minutes after the fight? The fact is,
that had the Yankee not tried the cruel and unusual
method of strangling a much smaller man on the ropes,
the result was almost a certainty for Sayers.—Con-
tributor.

Every patriotic Australian should smoke an Australian-
wood, Australian-made pipe. Taylor Bros., of 248 Pitt-
street, Sydney, whose advt. appears on page 4. ha\e made
a boom in Australian-wood pipes. Their leading line is
their already famous aromatic Needlewood pipe, which
possesses all the best qualities of briar and meerschaum
combined—coolness, sweetness, dryness, self-absorbency.
Needlewood, of which Taylor Bros, are always buyers, is ;
indigenous to Australia, and is a beautifully-grained wood.
It is difficult to season, and Taylor Bros, are working from
a stock which has been kept for years. Taylor Bros, will
make up pipes from wood sent by constituents, and will
(as per advt.) mail a single pipe by post to any part of the
world on receipt of remittance. Send a Taylor Needle-
wood to your friend in England as a typically Australian
present.

It reads like the inscription on the tomb of a
long-extinct Pharaoh, that cablegram setting forth that
Bubear has been rowing again against some other tenth-
rate nonentity on the Thames. The forgotten ruin was
beaten by twenty lengths. How these old-time scullers
keep rising again long after their repute has fallen into
the sere and toothless leaf, to potter about abjectly on the
river, and make a feeble pretence of being still objects of
interest to a world which has forgotten that they ever
existed 1

Dear Bulletin, —Let me say to your peace-
loving correspondent that the prize-fighter is not always
a cur upon the field of battle —witness Shaw', the Life-
guardsman, at Waterloo, who disposed of 13 Frenchmen —
[lt was only five a few years ago.—Ed. B.]—before he
went under. Also, that he can behave with bravery else-
where is proved by example in Conan Doyle’s article of
the other day on the subject. As to the dude with spats
on his feet being a better man at a forlorn hope than the
bruiser, I would say that probably the Chinese hawker is
superior to both. The average Chow whose knees knock
together at the sight of the ten-year-old “ lallikin ” with
a shanghai, will go on to the scaffold without turning a
hair; hut is this the sort of pluck we wish to develop?
How often has a man need to form one of forlorn hopes
compared with proximity to the innumerable accidents of
every-day life w'hioh call for presence of mind, suddenness
of action, and absence of fear ? The prize-ring also trains
a man to another sort of courage generally known as
“ heart,” the quality which enables him to be knocked
down fourteen times in the first two rounds and
then ultimately win the match. One of the writers
also asks, “ Where was there a greater prize-fight-
ing nation than Rome?" I unhesitatingly answer Greece 1
Greece, at the time of Pericles, was more athletic-mad
than any country before or since. The sports of the
Greeks were democratic, of the people, by the people, for
the people, while the gladiatorial displays of later Rome
were simply exhibitions paid for by the “ mugs ” of the
Eternal City for their own amusement, spectacular per-
formances to cater for blood-loving jeunesse doree, much
as the aristocracy of England to-day parts its £l5 a head
to see Jackson punch Slavin. The managemsnt may be
bad, the exponents may be blackguards, but with the
sport itself there can be no fault to find; and when the
wave of Socialism has swept over the lands washing it
thoroughly, the ring will be found again as clean as it
was on the plains of Olympus.—Yours faithfully, McG.

“Griffo” and Dixon are said to have been

» ♦ * •
The real objection of a section of the sporting
public to the betting machine is the fact that the public
would always follow the stable money and so bring
about a reduction of the diva, on the likely horses. A
Melb. sportsman, to meet this, suggests a "blind totali-
lator.” The money and the name of the horse to be
backed, he proposes, should be received under cover by
the racing clubs. The totalisator should be closed the
day before the race, and the investments placed opposite
the names of the horses in accordance with the instruc-
tions of the backers. Sixty per cent., he suggests, should
go to the backers of the winner, 20 to the second and 10
to the third. The remaining 10 per cent, should be
divided between the racing club, controlling the meeting,
and public charities. This is worth a trial.

As a publican will not employ a drunkard, so a
shady stable makes use of a “cronk ” jockey, and then
refuses him ordinary employment. Many a promising
lad has thus been ruined. The swindler values honesty
as Bung values sobriety, in his own interest.
