Thus a bookmaker, returning from races at
Flemington—“ In Ingland, yersee, they back ’orses on
their form, and it’s better for backers and better for book-
makers, too, because you all know what yer doin’.
There’s a little pullin’, but not much. Since the Jockey
Club wiped Charley Wood, and some of them, out they’ve
’ad to be careful. In Ingland it ain’t a Lit like what it is
’ere. There the public know as much as the book-
makers when the bettln’ opens, and here, of course, they
only know what they can find out, and that’s wrong, as a
rule.” Then the speaker shook his head sadly, and said
he would have stopped in Ingland, only the winters was
bad for his chest.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Henniker Heaton is a man of varied parts
(writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent). His
latest appearance was as a speaker at a luncheon on the
occasion of the opening of the Nimrod Club, a new pro-
prietary institution for English gentlemen devoted to
field sports. “ How did Heaton get there ?” But was he
not some years ago a mighty follower of Sydney hounds ?
Was he not the special hunting correspondent of the
Nooze, and did he not perform prodigies of ground and
lofty tumbling with his celebrated horse, The Gift? Ill-
natured fellows like Fred Want and Jack Lamb used to
say that he never jumped a fence, but what of that? He
was always able to tell much more of a stiff run than those
jealous equestrian impostors ; and doth not his appear-
ance on horseback in Hyde Park daily give the lie to those
who say he can’t ride?

A BUSHY’S RACEHORSE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He had finished second
In a scrambling of hacks
And his owner then reckon’d
Him a god of racing-tracks:
“Jim Jock sez, and ’e oughter know’d,
’E's the best ’orse ever 'e rode.”
“ Why, they call that a start I
'E was three lengths be'ind—
Just as far as that cart—
But since that time—you mind—
When I gave ’em that larst beating—
They haven’t given me no show at a meeting.’*
“ Around at the back there
They was jostlin’ Mm rough,
But ’e came like a hare
Down the straight—‘Good enough,’
Sez Jim Jock, and 'e oughter know’d,
’E’s the best 'orse ever ’e rode.”
The winner has been up
Six months if it’s a day
And fit for a Melberne Cup—
‘ A grass-fed ’aok,’ they say ;
But Jim Jock sez, and ’e oughter know’d
If that’s grass-feeding he’d be blow’d.
“ I’ll run ’em a mile,
In a month, weight for age,
But it gives me a smile,
And it seems a bit strange
To think me as soft as I look ;
They want it now, to suit their book.’*
Thus in tones very loud
He extols his own gee
Both to me and the crowd
And, of course, I agree—
For “ Jim Jock sez, and 'e oughter know’d,
’E’s the best’orse ever he rode.” /
Queensland.
GARM
Sydney’s First Town Clerk.
A grand living figure in the life-history of N.S.
Wales is John Rae, now in his 82nd year. Silver-
haired and rosy-faced, brisk in movement and
almost boisterously merry in conversation, with a
strongly-marked countenance indicative of intellec-
tual power and natural kindliness, John Rae is an
illustration of the truth that healthy mental
activity promotes and expands vitality.
He was born at Aberdeen (Scotland), 9th Jan.,
1813, and educated at the Grammar School and
Marischal College. He studied law, and in ’32
graduated as M.A. at the Aberdeen University.
In ’37 he carried off a =B2O literary essay prize
given by the Edinburgh University, and tells a
story of how, when the competition was over, but
before the results were known, being all but
drowned in a boating-accident, his last conscious
thought was—“ Bother it! I shall never know
who won that prize!”
In December, ’39, Mr. Rae arrived in Sydney—
the same year that Alfred Stephen came over to
N.S.W. from Van Diemen’s Land. Rae’s first
Sydney billet was that of secretary and accountant
to the North British Australian Loan and Invest-
ment Co. In ’42, he wrote the letterpress for
Sydney Illustrated, and is still the possessor of
what is believed to be the only extant copy of that
work. In July, ’43, he was appointed Town Clerk
of Sydney—the first Town Clerk of the newly-
incorporated municipality. The document em-
bodying this appointment has been mounted and
framed, and is to be seen on a wall in the present
Town Hall. Mr. Rae has a water-colour sketch
of the miserable structure that did duty as the
Town Hall in ’43, on the site now occupied by the
Ereemasons’ Hotel, York-street. The Sydney
Corporation might effectively flank John Rae’s
official appointment with, on one side, a picture of
that gabled cowhouse, and, on the other, a repre-
sentation of the architectural splendours of the
present Town and Centenary halls.
In ’53, John Rae published a blank-verse render-
ing of “Isaiah,” with explanatory notes. Not
only this, but he bought a fount of type, from
which he set up all his own copy, printed it him-
self, and book-bound the issue with his own
hands (!). In Jan. 7, ’54, the Sydney Corporation
was abolished, and the city was placed under the
administration of three commissioners—Gilbert
Elliott (chairman), E. Darvall, and John Rae.
In April, ’57, the Corporation was resurrected and
the Commissioners were dispensed with. After a
leisure spell, Rae was appointed accountant and
secretary to the old Sydney Railway Co., just
then in negotiation with the Government for the
sale of its property to the State. The Government
Railways Act, 22 Vie., No. 9. came into force on
Ist Dec., ’5B, and found Capt. Martindale gazetted
as a sort of Pooh-Bah over railways, roads, and
telegraphs, under the high-falutin title of “Com-
missioner for Internal Communication.” John
Rae became Under-Secretary for Works, and on
Martindale’s resignation of his Pooh-Bahship,
•Rae was, in addition to his Under-Secretaryship,
appointed Railway Commissioner, without salary !
Eor 16 years he received no salary while filling a
position that had cost the country <£looo a year,
his other secretarial salary being £2OO short of
that amount. “ Works ” Sutherland recognised
this injustice by inducing a later Parliament to
vote, for his term of office, ’72-5, a sum of <£Boo to
John Rae, being at the rate of .£2OO a year for
four years. Again in ’B9 Minister Fletcher, the
“ Works ” of the abbreviated Dibbs Ministry
(January-to-Maroh) recommended Rae’s claim of
<£2400—12 years at <£2oo a year—and its approval
had got as far as the Executive, when Dibbs went
down and up popped Parkes, M’Millan, Bruce
Smith and Co., who repudiated the claim. The
excuse given was that Rae didn’t require the
money as he was “well enough off without it.”
Rae’s official reports give a complete history of
N.S.W. railways for 30years, ’46-76. Australian,
English, European, and American newspapers
united to pour undiluted praise on Rae as having
been the first to publish reports showing the
amount of work actually performed on each line each
year—a clear and lucid system that has since been
copied by almost every railway management in
the world. Various Governments bestowed
epistolary honours upon him, and, in a short time,
Rae had (broadly speaking) free railway passes
over the planet. He has a life-pass for N.S.W.,
of course, but, “barring trams,” doesn’t use it
more than three or four times a year. In 77, Rae
was relieved of his gratis railway office, but
retained the Works Underseoretaryship till’BB.
Goodchap followed Rae as railway chief.
Sydney Mechanics’ Institute owes its first spasm
of life to John Rae, who, soon after his arrival in
Sydney, galvanised it with lectures on the poet
Burns, In 75, he printed, bound, and published
“by hand” —“Gleanings from my Scrap-book,”
brightly reminiscent of old Cornstalk times. He
had married in ’45, Miss Elizabeth Thompson,
who died in 77. His living family numbers four.
Mr. Rae has a hobby for mechanics, and has in-
vented anepicycloidal'‘chuck,’’attached to a“Rose
engine ” or lathe, and giving eccentricity by a flange
movement. The charming and intricate geome-
trical designs that can be obtained by this, by
merely turning a handle, cannot be described, and
must "be seen to be credited. The English
Mechanic and World of Science has published
photos, of Rae’s “chuck” and of its sectional
parts; while the correspondence thus provoked
between Rae and Sir John Scott, a British
enthusiast in that class of work, is highly instruc-
tive and interesting.
Mr. Rae’s sketches of “Old Sydney” are the
only reliable extant pictures of what the N.S.W.
metropolis was in the “forties” and “fifties.”
Before photography was known and practised,
Mr. Rae had rigged up a camera obscura (he has
it all) and, by its aid, had made the sun, as it
were, map out “Old Sydney” in sections, while
he laboriously and faithfully filled in every detail.
His collection of old-time pictures is saturated
with interest. And he intends —this octogenarian
—to take sketches of the same areas as they are
now in ’94, as a contrasting collection, ihese
pictures show the present busy Oxford-street as
cattle-paddocks; many-housed Woolloomooloo as
a rural scene of windmills; the medico-filled
Macquari e-street as having only two houses and
the public library; the old Government Printing-
office as a row of dwarf hovels ; the present
Wynyard-square as the old Military Barracks ;
and the unfinished R.O. cathedral that was burned
down and replaced by a new structure on higher
ground on Rae’s recommendation to Archbishop
Folding. In one picture—“ York-street, looking
south ’’—the markets, police-office, and temporary
cathedral —the writer’s attention was directed to
two black objects in front of the police-office.
“Do you know what they are?” “No.” “ Two
men in the stocks ! It’s the only time I saw such
a punishment,” said Mr. Rae; “and then, you
see, I only saw it in the camera, which simply
pictured the whole surroundings faithfully. I
didn’t know myself what it meant till I enquired.
But there they are ! ”
John Rae’s personality is afire with camaraderie,
a fact which should excuse the guilt of this long
notice. In ’BB, after leaving the “Works,” Rae
became a member of the Civil Service Board con-
stituted under the Act of ’B4, His 5-year term
of office expired on the last day of last year, and
he is now turning his “chuck” with picturesque
results.
Experiments at Hergott Springs, S.A.,
clearly show that dates suitable for commercial
purposes can be grown in the Far North.
Dr. Renwick, the N.S.W. Commissioner,
is back from the great swamp city of Chicago :
“ I never met such cold in my life. Why, one day it
was 20 - below zero. That’s 52 below freezing-point.
During the winter there the ink is frozen solid, and looks
just like a lump of coal.” “Then how did you manage
to write your letters ? Did you use a pencil ?” “ Oh, it
used to take three of us to write a letter in the winter
time.” “Three of you?” “Yes; one would take the
ink-bottle and wrap it in a cloth, and put it on the stove
to melt the ink and keep it boiling.” “Boiling?" “Fact,
I assure you.” “ And what was the cloth for ?” “Oh, in
case it exploded. A second man would dip a pen in the
boiling ink, and then hand it to me at the table, and I
would scribble away as hard as ever I could till the ink
froze on the pen, and then they would hand me another
pen fresh from the hot ink. That was the way we had to
write our letters. We had always to melt our ink before
writing.” “Well, it must have been cold!” “Cold!
Why, my jaw was sometimes frozen so hard it was like a
piece of iron.”
His lower maxillary was frozen! Why didn’t
they send Jawbone Neild to Chicago?
THREE FORTUNATE WOMEN.
We will introduce you to the oldest one first. Her
name is Mrs. Jessie Russell, and she lives near the
city of New York, in America. She was born in;
London in 1787, and is therefore 105 years old.
She remembers many incidents of her childhood,
and still has traces of former beauty. Her eyes
are bright, and become animated when she talks.
She retains her mental faculties completely. She
descends two flights of stairs to every meal, and
returns without assistance. Up to three years
ago she was a regular attendant at the Greenwood
Baptist Church. Her sight is excellent, and she i
reads the newspapers every day, and takes great \
interest in the news from England. She has
never had any serious illness in her life, and her *
appetite and digestion are almost as good as they :
were fifty years ago.
The second woman says: “ Ever since I was a
child I have suffered from illness. Thirteen years
ago a tired, languid, and heavy feeling came over ;
me. The white of my eyes became tinged with
yellow, my skin was sallow, and my hands and
feet were cold and clammy. My mouth tasted
badly, especially in the morning, and I was often
sick, vomiting a sour, frothy fluid. I had a pain
in the chest and sides, heartburn, and flatulency.
For four years I suffered like this, and I shall bo
glad to recommend the medicine that finally cured
me.” (Signed) (Mrs.) Francks Emily Smith, 49a,
Woodhouse Street, Leeds, January 25th, 1892.
The third woman says: “ I have been delicate
all my life. For the past six years I have always
felt tired, languid, and weak. I had a poor
appetite, a bad taste in the mouth, and pain after
every morsel I ate. I was dizzy, and often felt as
if I was going to fall. I always felt sick, and
would retch and strain, but could bring nothing
up. There was a dull pain at my right side and l
a dreadful pain between the shoulders. I never
knew what it was to be well. After being on my
feet for a while my legs would swell very much;
No doctor was able to help me, nor any medicine.
that is, up to the time I took the one which gave
me my health back, I never felt so well in my life
as Ido now.” (Signed) (Mrs.) Sarah Sharman,
Glinton, near Market Deeping, February 19th,
1892.
Why do we put these three women in a group—
the one venerable woman in America and the
two others here in England ? They are perfect
strangers to one another and always will be. We
do it to show how long and happily a woman may
live if she only happens to escape the malady
that everywhere threatens her sex; and also to
show that those who have been burdened and
cursed with it may be cured and once more taste
the joys of health.
Mrs. Smith concludes her letter in these words ;
“After taking three bottles of Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup all pain and sickness left me, and
I knew no more of the ailment that had troubled
me so long, indigestion and dyspepsia. I know
others who have been benefited by the Syrup. All
sufferers should use it.
Mrs. Sharman adds that after she had doctored
and doctored and taken everything, almost with-
out getting any good from it, Mr. Webster, ai
grocer of Glinton, said, “ Why don’t you use
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup ? ” She acted on
his suggestion, and health wasn’t long in coming.
“I cannot speak too highly of Seigel’s Syrup,”
she says. We should agree with her in that
opinion, for a remedy that will in a few weeks put
an end to a case of chronic indigestion and dys-
pepsia is certainly worth a good word or two.
Now here you have these three Englishwomen
—one so fortunate as to have lived more than a
century without an illness ; the others (still more
fortunate) have known the sadness of suffering
and the pleasure of recovery.
Oh, my ! oh, my ! how hard it is to tell who is
best off in this queer world.
“ Stretch my grave as long as possible, so
I can have all the land I am entitled to.” — Burns.
According to late cables European Anar-
chist societies are insuring the lives of members
destined for dangerous service. A grim idea-
blowing Capital into hash and making its
executors cash up liberally for the transaction.
Mr.— or is it Miss ?—Montgomery, of
Maoriland, sends The Bulletin “Madame Beran-
ger,” a one-act drama, which, despite slight
novelty and occasional crudity, has both power
and promise. The writer’s verse appears in “ The
Tohunga,” a booklet illustrative of Maori cus-
toms ; and his metrical accompaniment to the
effective sketches by Mr. K. Watkins has caught
a glow from the noble poetry of a noble race.

AUSTRA LIA.
Save our Bodies—then our Souls.
[FOR THE BULLETIN .]
(To those clergymen to whom they may apply these
verses are dedicated .)
Hark ! the Sabbath-bells are ringing from the
churches’ lofty spires,
Into proud cathedrals bringing hearts that throb
with base desires,
Ere ye preach in polished diction of the future,
vast and dim.
Ere ye emphasise some fiction with a pious prayer
or hymn,
Listen to that thrilling summons that your brazen
bell still tolls,
“ Save ! oh save ! the people’s bodie ■, and ye then
may save their souls !”
Is it fiery zeal and fervent, that which gives such
haughty mien
To yon proud and stately servant of the lowly
Nazarene !
While he rides in cushioned carriage by those fast
high-steppers drawn
To some money-monger’s marriage, deck’d in lace
and sleeves of lawn ;
Does lie hear those mournful murmurs when he
lakes his tithes and tolls,
“ Give us food ! we die of hunger ! Save our
bodies, then our souls !”
What made man the slave we see him, but your
soul-destroying laws,
When true hearts have fought to free him, did ye
help the holy cause ?
No! ye sought to forge the fetters for earth’s
heroes true and brave,
Men whose names in gleaming letters blaze as
meteors round each grave,
Men who marched to death undaunted, Truth and
Right their only goals ;
In your love, so loudly vaunted, have ye tried to
save their souls ?
While ye pour out wealth like water, sending forth
your mission bands,
Pioneers of fire and slaughter in a hundred heathen
lands;
From your fever-stricken alleys, from your cities’
squalid slums,
Sweeping through fair sunlit valleys as a rising
tempest comes
With a rumbling sound like thunder when ’mong
mountain rocks it rolls —
Give us work! or food—we’re starving! Save
our bodies, then our souls ! ”
Will ye dare to meet the Master when before His
judgment seat
Placed with those who’ve journeyed faster, though
they walked with bleeding feet—
Cold and hungry, lired and naked, all who’ve
sinned and suffered here—
All who’ve died of thirst unslaked in Life’s
deserts, dread and drear,
When the great Recording Angel there his radiant
scroll unrolls,
D .ire ye say ; ‘‘We saved their bodies, then we
sought to save their souls ? ”
Maoriland.

A Dreamer.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
She came as such a hush that comes
Between the beat of sounding drums,
When silence seems to thrill with sound ;
She came as comes the summer night,
Alive with silver sound and sight,
And lit by one round rose of white.
And whiter than white flowers in bloom,
And sweeter than sweet rose perfume
The lovely dreamer seemed to be.
Her eyes were blue and dreamy eyes,
Enchanted and serenely wise,
Like two calm planets of the skies.
Her form was such as poets see
Down dusky glades of ancientry,
Or sceptred on a fairy throne ;
Her voice was soothing as the breeze
That makes a harp of forest trees,
And melting in a thousand keys.
In days worn out and wan as these
No fairy lands, no fairy seas !
O, lady with the pale, sweet face,
What purpose makes thy feet to tread
Through ruined dreams and legends dead,
High fancies lost and visions fled ?
O, lady, come from Arcady,
In days like these, for thee, for thee,
What place? what hope ? what joy remains?
Return thee to thy sylvan glade,
String roses in a blushing braid,
And there unmake what time hath made.
RODERIC QUINN.
The following: more or less reliable news
item is going the rounds of the press :
A lease of nine hundred and ninety-nine years, made in
the days of King Alfred, has just expired in England.
The land was leased by the Church to the Crown, a id
reverts now to the Church of England after a thousand
years —a striking illustration of the stability of the law in
that isle.
The legal stability isn’t so very evident. It was
the Saxon Catholic Church which leased the land,
and now the Anglican Church gets it and the un-
stable law leaves the original church in the
lurch. Moreover, the law is so shaky that if the
lease had had another 20 years to run, there would
have been no Church of England to resume posses-
sion at all. Further, the story isn’t true in any
case.
For the inhabitants of the fashionable
Sydney suburb of Manly Beach, and especially
for the little knot of absentee Manly property-
owners who, in what they conceive to be the direct
interest of their own pockets, hold meetings in
Sydney to denounce the carrying-out of a system
of sewerage :—“The last U.S. cholera epidemic
was in 1873, and resulted in 7356 deaths. During
the 20 years that have elapsed since then there
has been an annual loss of from 40,000 to 50,000
lives, due to typhoid fever, yet there has never
been a tithe of the excitement over this terrible
mortality that was caused by the ‘cholera scare ’
of 1892. In addition to the deaths, there are
about 500,000 people every year ill with typhoid,
the cost of all this —loss of citizens, money and
time is not less than <£15,000,000 annually. Yet
this enormous loss is entirely preventable. Up to
1859, the city of Munich was a nest of typhoid.
There were no sewers and there was no public
Water supply. There was a system of cesspools,
and the drinking-water was taken from shallow
wells. The deaths from typhoid were 24.2 per
1000. In 1859, the citizens began a system of
sewerage, and introduced a supply of wholesome
drinking-water. Now, the typhoid death-rate has
fallen from 24.2 to 1.4 per 1000. If the death-rate
from typhoid in the U.S. were reduced to the
Munich percentage, it would result in the saving
of 40,000 lives per year. And typhoid is not the
only disease caused by want of good drainage.’’
Some one once said that Oscar Wilde’s
mind was as foul as Thames River at London
Bridge. “ His men are roues and profligates ; his
women either are, or have been, or will be,
Aspasias; his situations are Bacchanalian episodes.
Indeed, with him everything is a joke—honour,
truth, female purity, conjugal fidelity, marriage,
maternity.” It has been remarked that at least a
score of the aphorisms scattered through his
“ Woman of No Importance ” arrest the attention
like the prick of a dagger. “ Women represent
the triumph of mind over matter; men, the
triumph of matter over morals.” Ladies will not
thank him for the epigram: “Twenty years of
romance make a woman something like a ruin ;
twenty years of marriage make her rather like a
public building.” “Women have a better time
than men; there are far more things forbidden
them.” “ A bad man is a man who admires inno-
osnce; a bad woman is a woman of whom men
never tire.” “ All men are married women’s pro-
perty ; in fact, that is the meaning of married
women’s property.” Again: “The happiness of
a married man depends on the woman he has not
married.” Of wedlock: “How can a woman be
happy with a man who treats her as if she was a
rational being?” “Men marry because they are
tired, women because they are curious.” As good
as La Rochefoucauld’s ; “ The Book of Life begins
with a man and a woman in a garden, and ends
with Revelations.” One can judge Wilde’s idea
of life from the saying : “If I have no temptation
in the course of the week, it makes me quite
nervous about the future.” He lays down a few
rules of life, among them : “ A man of social tact
should talk to every woman as if he loved her and
to every man as if he bored him.”

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpenoe, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

At Chaffey Bros.’ Mildura (Vic.) irrigation
colony there are now 4000 people with average
holdings of 20 acres. Two young men there (so
say the Ohaffeys) cleared in raisins .£l6OO from 40
acres last season ; while at Renmark one man got
,£5OO worth of dried apricots from_ acres,
planted four years. These are exceptional cases,
no doubt. This year, it is said, 600 tons of dried
fruit, worth .£30,000, is being harvested.

March 28, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Sydney Liedertafel’s 73rd concert in Sydney
Town Hall, though not unspotted, was still a
success. A buffo chorus, “Italian Salad,” was
the most heartily-received item, partly because
the lack of tone-volume, which is becoming a
defect of the S.L., was less noticeable in it, and
partly because people love to laugh at a high-class
concert —it amuses them as much as smiling in
church. The re-appearance of the fetching Ada
Crossley, in cerulean satin like a blue coot, was
rather anti-climax, I thought, after the tears and
huzzas of her “ben.,” and the presentation of
another large lyre (gracefully handed by Mrs.
Burdekin in silken garments and impressive dia-
monds) looked as if the other affair had only been
a dress-rehearsal.
Bouquets hurled at Miss Crossley made the
platform look like a triumph of Spring, and when,
on her final disappearance, the Liedertafelians
pelted her with cut flowers the enthusiasm was
tremendous ; one fiddler turned a severe flip-flap
in his hurry to get within aim with his contribu-
tion. But to be pelted with cut flowers, though
complimentary, is sometimes as dangerous as to
be hit with a brick. Lady Halle —the only other
concert-performer I ever saw similarly assailed—
unblushingly dodged the cabbage-roses as if they
had been bad eggs; but Ada Crossley stood fire
unflinchingly. I saw two savage-looking dahlias
catch in her fringe—they would have involved
raw beef and cold flatirons had they landed an
inch or two lower down.
Was it a Melbourne fashion, I wonder, that
made Miss Crossley bring most of her bouquets
back on to the stage with her? To me it looked
like a guarantee of bona-fides: “No deception,
ladies and gentlemen ; they haven’t gone back to
the florist’s.” When a bouquet goes behind the
scenes anything may happen to it. For example,
I once saw a very graceful nosegay go up with
banners flying to an undulating artiste, and ten
minutes afterwards it was quietly brought round to
the donor, who grabbed it sotto voce and instantly
fled into the outer darkness. I don’t know to this
moment whether or not those flowers were hired,
like a cab, at per quarter of an hour.
N.S.W. Agricultural Society formally opened
its annual show on 22nd inst. with Gov. and
Lady Duff, attended by Major Leigh, on the
ornamental dais, and a pleasant air of success
pervading the well-stocked pavilion, which looked
like a harvest festival. But it was in the luncheon
marquee that the real official proceedings eventu-
ated. However, the only noticeable feature of
“the apostles’” table was the discovery that
Major Leigh can’t eat and keep his eye-glass up
as George Reid can.
Gov. Duff doesn’t drop his final gees in speaking
but he doesn’t always use good English. “Of
greater ability than me,” was the tail-end of one
vice-regal sentence calculated to undermine the
hardly-acquired grammar of the Points. It was
as bad as the President’s new pronunciation of
“decade” —altho’ an error of syntax is more
fashionable than a mispronunciation in these over-
educated days. Everybody bent a willing elbow
to all the toasts —indeed, when the “ Agricultural
Society” was given, several members enthusiastic-
ally drank their own healths with three cheers and
a tiger ! And I was amused by one politician,
with a faded flower in his coat, who dinned on his
plate with a fork every time the Governor rose and
sat down, as if a crockery-tattoo had all the
virtues of a national anthem. He recalled the
theatre-usher who clashes two horny palms in the
dim light behind the circle whenever a high-priced
“ fro it ” makes an unreceived entry.
After the very well-done luncheon, people rushed
forth into the fresh air—the vice-regal party
augmented by the Duff girls and Captain Feilding
—making a bee line for the centre of the ring,
instead of packing into the flag-draped box on the
stand. There was no autumnal dressing on the
stand, owing to the patchy weather, and the only
figure that attracted my eye was Essie Jenyns
Wood spanking about with hubby in a mail
plimton and pair, and wearing a fine cream serge
made with a long coat lined with primrose silk ;
primrose silk folded belt; black jet bonnet with
primrose bow at the back, and a cream-lace
parasol.
I thought the equestriennes over hurdles the
most sensational exhibit, but none of the clever
horsewomen were well-tailored, and, in point of
hats, two went very wide of the mark, indeed. One
had a curious touch of red in her beaver, as if she
was smouldering on top, and the best rider of them
all wore a balloon-shaped cap that filled with wind
every time she rose to a hurdle. It looked as if
she got over by inflating her head-piece with gas.
One point worth noticing about the lady-jumpers
was that none of them wore gloves—a sensible
practice which might be adopted with advantage
outside the show-ring, it seems to me.
In the gentlemen’s jumps, I couldn’t help notic-
ing how the amateurs who rode in cords and tops
took the shine (for style) out of the gaudy (silk
jackets that seemed like so many Lowry-parrots
getting over the rails. I don’t object to racing-
colours, but why on earth should a show-rider
dress like an organ grinder’s monkey to put his
animal over five feet? By the way, the horses
were the whole show, as far as moat of the women
present were concerned. It is only the agricul-
tural person who goes every day, and takes his
dinner in a red handkerchief, who is an authority
afterwards on the girth of the prize pumpkin.
The first appearance of the B. and B. 00., at
Sydney Lyceum, on Bun Saturday, was rather
spoilt, from a society point of view, by the semi-
religious character of the day. It is good form,
you know, to repent your sins steadily up to
Easter Monday—and then sow a fresh crop during
race-week. The overflowing circle looked like a
festival at the Synagogue, and Mrs. Brough’s
array of society admirers was meagrely repre-
sented by a lady in pea-green, who sailed behind
in an unimposing procession of one between the
acts. There were flowers in the air and a recep-
tion for each mummer—but yet the B. and B.
resurrection was not the success a concretely-
fashionable audience would have made of it.
There was one society lady in the circle
bedizened in paste, from whom I would have
expected better things ornamentally, but a woman
in a very wiggy-looking wig had a lofty society
precedent for her borrowed curls, anyhow. The
incident of the evening in front, though, was when
a plump lady, in a stage box, airily, and after
much coquettish by-play, hurled a red, red rose at
an admirer in the stalls. Upon which, “Strike
*ne with a feathah !” piped an affected voice from
Olympia. But if ladies start showering thorny
roses on to stall Johnnies, the bald-headed brigade
will have to hoist its umbrella between acts.
The Amazons haven’t changed their clothes
since we saw them last. The same effective sar-
torials prevail from Alpha to Omega as of yore—
which is a pleasant thing and leaves you free to
enjoy the highly discreet way in which the B. and
B. ladies sling their half-smoked cigarettes into
the wings. Brenda Gibson is the only Amazon
who is game to look as if she meant to burn it
down to the stump. Mrs. Brough puffs just a few
hurried whiffs, but Grace Noble takes only one
blow and looks as if the cigarette was going to
bite her. But there is never any realism about a
comedy actress getting through a whiff of tobacco.
To calm her obvious scruples the hero might be
made to say, “Will you take a cigarette, Miss?
It’s marked down in the business.”
The B. and B. Co., by the way, sounded some-
thing like infants crying in a boiler-factory on
their maiden trip in the big Lyceum, which seems
a slop-fit for them after the cosy Criterion. Mrs.
Brough (who looks extremely well). Miss Romer,
Boucicault (his Gaelic mustivey was gummed on
unevenly in the first act), Titheradge, and Carey
were fairly audible all through, but the rest spoke
in whispers as if the corpse of the author was
lying next door. I noticed the same thing at
Melbourne Princess theatre, which is miles too
big for the lung-power of some of the company.
After all is said, the wicked downpour on
Saturday—the maiden day of the A.J.O. autumn
meeting—didn’t spoil the races from a fashionable
point of view. Society wouldn’t have gone to
Randwick on Bun Saturday if the sun had risen
as clear as an orange. Gov. Duff had publicly
declared (in effect) that he wasn’t going to break
the back of the Lenten season prematurely by
patronising a “ certain contingency—to wit, a
horse-race,” as the suppressors of gambling say,
and Society stopped at home hoarding up its
autumn gown for Easter Monday. So if the first
day was simply mudlarks, it didn’t matter a row
of pins to the members’-standites.
General Hutton’s firm attitude as to supplying
vacancies in N.S.W. Artillery force with “Im-
perial officers ” —instead of with the local young
man who fagged thro’ his exams.—suggests a
strong army of backers. Indeed, it looks to me
as if the ladies of the Barracks ruled in N.S.W.
to-day, just as effectually as they did in the bad old
historic times when the officers’ wives used to
encourage the (logger to give the floggee “ another
dozen, just to amuse the baby.”
The Imperial officer is the dream of N.S.W,
military belledom. The real, live, mysterious
soldier who has smelt powder (of all sorts) and
who proudly lets the shaving accidents on his
manly cheek tell of the stirring chances of his
wild calling to the romantic damsel tired to death
of the peaceable home-grown warrior with naught
but a yearly sham-fight to his credit—is like a
novelette with a picture on it to the Australian
society - woman. Therefore, General Hutton’s
Imperial reinforcement is bound to come. It is,
my budding heroes, one of the privileges of living
in Australia to work “like a Chinaman” at your
glorious calling, and then see the coveted vacancy
neatly filled by an Importation.
Latest “informations” re Brian Darley—the
tall and husky hero of Sydney’s ever-green divorce-
case—tell of his playing Charlie, “with great
success,” in a New York representation of
“ Charley’s Aunt.”
The parties to that Sydney scholastic libel-case
must be congratulated on having settled the action
on the basis of a <£loo verdict and a written
apology, for, if the lady plaintiff had gone into
court and cast her melting eyes at the jury, those
good men and true (emphasis on the men) would
have awarded no end of damages and added a
rider that defendant be taken out into the wilder-
ness and lost. The worst legal antagonist in the
world is a pretty woman.
Apropos of law-suits, I hear that the hoary hero
of the latest scandal has melted out of sight,
nobody knows whither.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
I hear from Brisbane that their Governor’s elder
daughter has accepted the widowed affections of a
local M.P. and ex-Premier, and promises a
mother’s care to his “ dear girls.”
The lady’s father has himself been married three
times, so he probably encourages widowers within
his gates on principle. Experience has taught him
that widowers mean business as a rule. Although
a middle-aged relict is not the Romeo of one’s
dreams, a wise woman doesn’t stick out for
romance in her wooer when she has a step-mother
no older than herself, and a step-sister who is—
well, younger than either of them. Apart from
these considerations (which will obtrude them-
selves, in spite of the best endeavours to keep them
under), the lady may be honestly congratulated
upon escaping the perils of a youthful spouse. I
begin to think the youthful spouse is intended,
these modern times, for very old brides of the
Burdett-Coutts pattern.
The present Lady Norman was, as a child, a
ward of the Norman family, and was educated in
England, along with Sir Henry’s daughters by
his first marriage. When the girls grew up they
went over to visit their pa in India, taking with
them a dear little friend, who was soon as
great a favourite with the warrior, their parent, as
with everyone else. Before the Misses Norman
knew what was coming, they were called upon,
for the second time in their lives, to welcome a
step-mother, who, in this case, was no other than
their quondam protegee, now the popular chatelaine
of Brisbane Government House.
One of Sir Henry’s “original” offspring is
matron of a military hospital at Gibraltar, and
when Lady N. wants to propitiate good old dames
at hospital committee meetings, etc., she talks
maternally about “my daughter the nurse!”
“ Our daughter the matron! ” Another sister was
married just before papa’s appointment to
Bananaland, the chief feature of her trousseau
being, so I am told, its extreme simplicity.
It is the third and last of this first family wno
is reported to have met her fate in Brisbane, so
their young step-sister will soon have the matri-
monial arena all to herself.
When pleasures are lawful they cease to be
attractive, and just because the ’Varsity boys had
a grand opportunity for noise, at last Saturday’s
“ Commencement,” they did not avail themselves
of it to any extent. A riot was only what was
expected of them, therefore the prospect lost its
charm. Now, at the theatre, especially if the rest
of the audience seem nervous or pernickety, or
during lecture hours, these victims of an excessive
meat-diet feel themselves possessed with a desire
for lunacy which nothing short of pandemonium
can satisfy.
Talking of student mania recalls to my mind an x
idyll of the last terra at our “ noble University.”
One day, while a lecture was going on, the skirt
of a lady medical student, through some defect in
its construction no doubt, was observed to be
gaping open in the region of the “back hooks.”
The underlying raiment had followed suit, with
the result that some inches of the wearer’s spine
were laid bare to the scientific gaze of her brothers
in research.
Women who hear this story invariably protest
that the vista must have been calico, not skin, but
the boys hold to their version, and at the time a
prize was offered to whoever should, by means of
improvised squirts, &c., awaken the unconscious
damsel to a sense of her position by a cold splash
of ink. Quarts of the dusky fluid were expended
in this attack upon the spine, but by special
intervention of Providence, not one drop reached
the goal, and the lecture over, the lady went her
way at peace with all men. Later on she must
have marvelled at the spotted and smudged con-
dition of her upholstery.
The peaceful suburb of Kew hardly knew itself*
the other week, with two weddings in three days-
One of the brides, the daughter of a Melb. bank
inspector and a relative of well-known Toorak and
Buninyong families, introduced into the solemn
sacrament more worldly display than might have
been expected of her father’s daughter.
When a Patriarch groans if his friends’ boys go
to “ the Cup ” and shakes his head mournfully if
their girls are addicted to adorning the poor
perishable body, one is surprised at seeing his own
descendants, apparently with the paternal sanc-
' tion, go in wholesale for pomps and vanities. To
make room for spectators, by turning the seats
out of the Tabernacle, and have the decorations
done by professional, and possibly ungodly, florists,
would not have met with the good man’s approval
at anyone else’s wedding; while to sign all the
necessary documents and declarations in the
church, as part of the tableau, instead of in the
vestry as usual, would have been regarded by him
as vain and worldly ostentation. The bride was
calm and collected throughout, only allowing one
tear to hang becomingly on her dark eyelash,
when her father referred to the noble way “in
which his lassie had,” etc., etc. The travelling
costume of white (gown, hat, boots, gloves, and
sunshade, all to match) was even more becoming
to its black-haired, blue-eyed wearer than the cere-
monial toilette itself.
The other wedding, though less expensive per-
haps, was noteworthy from the courage displayed
by the popular bride. She was linking her for-
tunes with those of a widower missionary and his
small child, and cheerfully looked forward to a
home in the lonely South Sea Islands, which had
witnessed the death, under most sensational cir-
cumstances, of her predecessor, the rev. gentle-
man’s first wife. For my part, I have a sneaking
belief in ghosts.
On the last day of the polo tournament at Rich-
mond the play was exciting, but if not cruel, it
certainly had the appearance of being so. A grey
pony, with bleeding sides and the rider’s spurs
digging into its wounds all the time, was a de-
pressing spectacle. All the fashionables were
E resent, in most cases wearing their race raiment.
iady Hopetoun, our recognised polo enthusiast,
looked rather sombre in a black carriage cape and
chapeau of piebald straw, with a spray of red roses
nid-nodding aloft. She had with her Miss Wilson,
of “ Ercildoune,” dressed quietly, in the demure
English style, which doesn’t seem in keeping with
our bright sunshine and blue skies.
Miss Wilson wore a butcher-blue jacket and
skirt, with a rosette to match in her small sailor
hat. Her pretty sister was attired in a similarly
designed grey frock, with pink vest and hat
trimmings. It is said that “Salmon” didn’t
intend allowing his daughters to go to the races,
or club ball, or, in fact, any of the festivities.
Only at the eleventh hour, “ the Countess, ” pity-
ing these martyrs to noblesse oblige, invited them
to attend such gaieties as still remained, under
her aristocratically-fledged wing. Whereupon the
careful father, satisfied that his children would be
suitably chaperoned, blessed them, and bade them
go their ways rejoicing.
Everyone has been jeering at those Brisbane
ladies who, at a Suffrage meeting, saluted one
another by such names as “ Old cat ” and “ Old
maid.” Male boasters declared that herein was
“ woman’s intellectual inferiority manifest, her
inability to view matters from a business rather
than a personal standpoint.” We don’t hear
any more of this bombast since the Mutual
Store fracas, at which some of the leading
merchants of Melbourne were to be heard
squeaking out in voices, thin with excitement,
such personal remarks as “ Old fool 1 ” “ Bad old
man 1! ” “ Ugly old man II!”
Ours is always described as the vainer sex, but
it certainly looks as if men cared more about being
young and charming than is generally admitted,
when the most cutting epithets that they can find
are “ Ugly ” and “ Old." At such a meeting as
this that wonderful creature, Maoriland Mayor
Yates, would have been a decided acquisition. In
her presence even an ill-timed smile is not allowed
to pass without rebuke.
When women get the chance they ate thorough
martinets. The first Lady Brassy was an example
of a she-disoiplinarian. During her wanderings
in Bananaland she visited an up-country opal-
mine, in company with “ Tom ” and a mounted
escort of bluejackets off the Sunbeam. These
hardy mariners displayed a loose, bounding style
of horsemanship, which soon had the effect of
working their wide, nautical “bags” up to their
knees, greatly to the amusement of the plain bush
folk en route.
When the head of the expedition perceived the
decollete condition of her followers’ shins, she
commanded a halt and issued these orders.
“Brown! draw up your sock!” “Smith, pull
down your pants!” “Jones! you look like a
fool!” The culprits lost no time in obeying, but
before long their ankles were once more bared to
the breeze. The lady began to be annoyed, and
the ridicule of passing swagmen and sundowners
increased her irritation. In the end, she decreed
that each gallant tar must sever his clean, starched
pocket-handkerchief in twain, and with the
halves thereof, reef his fluttering lower sails firmly
to his ankles. That night in the township
“pub” when “Jones,” the good boy of the
“Sunbeam” crew, had enjoyed a few whiskies,
he remarked—“ That’s the worst of a she captain,
she’s always huntin’ and uaggin’ about, and a
feller gets no peace.”
Anyone, who makes a study of photographic
albums, must notice how few, when compared
with the early days .pf the Hopetoun regime, are
the pictures of urchins seated in goat-carriageei.
When Hopetoun, junr., otherwise Lord Hope, if
you please, arrived in the “colony,” he possessed
one of these turn-outs and was once or twice
photographed therein.
The picture took greatly with local mothers who,
in some cases, went to the length of ordering a
miniature cart and turning loose upon their
cramped suburban quarters one or more of those
blatant aggressive pests, usually known as “Billy
goats.”
When Claude or Tommy, as the case might be,
was divested of his Fauntleroy suit and, much to
his own mortification, degraded into a white
tucked frock and cloth pilot jacket (d la Hope)
his fond mother had him “ immortalised ” in his
goat-carriage. Then she went about telling her
friends that hundreds of people had mistaken the
picture for a portrait of “ His Excellency’s little
boy.” For some reason or other it’s no longer
lawful to drive goats in harness, so paterfamilias
can once more recline in safety under his own
vine and fig-tree, or talk “poultry ” with the next-
door neighbour, without fear of rude and sudden
assault.
Talking of the Governor, people say that ever
since one of their servants was carried off by the
fever the Hopetouns have a nervous dislike to
spending typhoid season at the big House. This,
perhaps, accounts for the Arab-like wanderings,
the comings and goings, the sojourning at head-
quarters for a night, and departing unto the
uttermost parts of the land in the morning, which
enliven vice-regal existence from December to May.
After her Hopetoun (meaning the township of
that august name) trip, the Countess intends
visiting her brothers up North. These lively
youths are certainly blessed with an attentive
sister. Didn’t she convict one of them of flirting
with his grandmother (figuratively speaking), and
at infinite trouble to herself, and carry him
“home” to his mother’s apron-strings? What
made her action the more heroic was that her
departure, at that juncture, left the field open to
two upsetting sisters -in - law, who instituted
obstacle races in the classic groves of Government
House, and, in fact, played up in a most unstately
manner. They were wholesome affable amazons,
nevertheless.
Miss Bon Watson, one of the pets of Melbourne
society, has at last accepted a suitor—Mr. Bert
Armytage, a young squatter, whose brother so
chagrined the “ first eleven,” some years ago, by
marrying a dashing girl connected with a Collins-
street caravanserai.
Apropos , Mr. Olive Clarke, whose body was
brought from London to Melbourne for interment,
the other day, would, in all probability, had he
lived, become the husband of the pretty* daughter
of a legal family resident at St. Kilda. — Van M.

Harold Boyd, the Ripp-lene man, late of Aus-
tralia, writing of the Indian coalfields “ In Bengal they
are indeed developing a‘black country.’ After getting
well away from Howrah, the E.I.R. takes the traveller
over many miles of pronounced coal country. An English
miner would stare if you told him of a coal seam 9611.
deep, yet this is a reality, and may be seen a few hours
journey from Calcutta. And in (hose mines what an
abundance of coal 1 Coal everywhere! Coal under foot,
coal in the vast arches over our heads, supported by so i
walls of the same material. And coal, too in many
instances, yielding so small & percentage of ash as to vie
with the best English article. g

Dear Bulletin,—lb is long since I troubled you
with a letter. I do so now, through seeing you refer
sometimes to Mashonaland and Matabeleland as places to
which there is likely to be a rush. I arrived in Fort Sajis-
bury, 15th July, ’9l, with five others well fitted-out with
nine months provisions, also tools, dynamite and every-
thing required, besides waggon and oxem We had four
donkeys to oti y > fhe Tsetse flv and
even they won’t last very long. (Strange they don’t seem
sufi••rjasfS’ttisss?s*;.' l tss
not in Loben.’s country. We went as far as the Hunyati
RWer that divides the two territories, but were shy of
?““ ain r ikstj? z “ ’is-
c™Ke. the country‘is cleaner-wotked’-out then any
part of Australia. I could not determine the age
of the workings—but should say they are not less
than a hundred years old—they might he three,
There are in some places patches of old workings a mile
long and a chain or so wide, as if they had been taking
out small surface leaders (all the Mashona workings seem
to have been for quartz), but nothing carrying more than
colours could we get on the bottom of any place we
cleaned out._ There were places which the mullock-heaps
indicated might lie worked down 30 or 40 ft., and which
I should have liked to sink on, but all such places
were held by the Chartered Go. s police with pegs and
paper. I went to Dr. Jamieson to ask about some work-
ings which, according to dates posted on them, had been
pegged six months; he told me that that ground was
protected, and I suppose they are protecting it still
Whatever people worked that country were as thick as
bees, for you come on places among the workings where
•r h rao e e8 h o a f Ve the ei hkbilatfons “n'sSfl Mseen-each j’ust a
round ring of baked mud eight feet in diametef, the
bottom of what was once a hut; likewise the stones which
they ground the quartz with. I think, too, that they
knew how to follow a little gold as well as do some of the
smart hands who are looking for it now. I also believe
that the country, as a rule, was poor. We can’t tell, but
a dwt. per week might have paid them. Anyhow, a Joz.
per ton prospect was the best ever I could raise,
and I pounded rocks till I began to get lop-sided.
Toe C. Co. gave all the fellows who joined what they call
“ the police ” 10 claims and a farm ; also, I think, 3s. per
day. An outsider could get a farm, too, of 3000 morgen—
-6000 acres—by paying £3OO. I did not bother much about
the condi I ions, but some £3 would have such a stretch of
country kept for you just long enough to make some
improvements—and then the Co. would put on the place
whatever tax or rent their elastic conscience dictated,
After doing three months we packed up and started for
Umtaii in Manicaland, 170 miles N.E. of Salisbury, and
25 miles from Massi Kessi—where the “ great” engage-
ment took place between C. Co.’s “police” and Portu-
g.iese. Manica seems to have been worked for alluvial.
It, is'very mountainous, and the valleys have been
worked for miles. Here, too, nothing but a colour
could we get, though we found many sound blocks
in amongst the old workings. There are a few reefs
with however, only about the same prospects as
those in Mashona. I don’t think much of the goldfields
inO Co. possessions. The Portuguese have a grand piece
er country from Massi Kessi to Port Beira for sugar-grow-
inu- or any purpose of tropical plantation. There are
miles of country where the soil is equal to that of Port
Mackay, but it’s of no use to the owners. Nothing in any
of the settlements except soldiers and officers and tootling
on a bugle day and night, and flags flying from every
possible place where one can be tied up. The filth lying
round in the settlements would make a pig sick. If you
do wad . e through all this perhaps you’ll build out of it a
par. calculated to prevent men from going a long journey
to find nothing.—Wokabut, l Ravenswood, Q., 3rd March,
1894.

Facts which should be weighed well by any
Australian bent Matabelelandwards. The easiest ai d
nlost expeditious stage of the journey is the first-
from Australia to the Cape. That accomplished, train-
fare from Cape to Johannesburg, third-class, £4 17s. 6d ;
251 - i ue , gage> free—all over that weight, 3d. per 11). is
charged ; coach-fare from Johannesburg l to Pretoria, £2 ;
p re tbria to Fort Salisbury by weekly mail-coach, £32 10s.,
plus expenses on journey, which takes a week ; 251 h.
weight of luggage, free ; all over that, 2s. 9d. per lb. is
charged. Also, if the traveller remains for over a week
w ithin the borders of the S.A. Republic he is poll-taxed
ios. and made to feel like a Chow. Four months ago the
population of Fort Salisbury was 1800, all of whom were
either out “putting down the niggers,” or down them-
selves with fever.

The story of a mine is generally written on the
tombstones of its shareholders, night or nine years ago
the Globe was one of the most hopeless concerns on the
West Coast of Maoriland. It had an immense, hungry,
barren reef, and year after year it toiled, and made calls,
and broke the hearts of its shareholders to no purpose,
At last, when many of the shares were forfeited for un-
paid calls, and couldn’t be auctionedevenatJd.each.it
closed up. An ancient mariner was put on at a micro-
scopic screw to see that no one ran away with the shaft,
or the rusted battery, or the silent, moss-grown engine-
house. One day the aged guardian, for want of better
recreation, went forth with a pick and discovered a big
new rtef. Then there was a whoop of joy, and Globe
shares went up to 425. The old shareholders might have
sold out and retired, but (hey didn’t. There was a
carnival of dividends for seven years or so, and just when
it looked as if the prosperity was going to last for ever,
the bottom fell out of the mine a s'condtime. So the
other day one of the largest shareholders, who had
blewed all his dividends in wild-cat mines, solemnly put
his affairs in order, left his bank-book on the table along
with a package containing his false teeth, and then went
forth and drowned himself. The Globe mine had killed
him.
* ♦ * ♦
Has the glory of Bendigo finally departed ? All
along the New Chum line deep-sinking has of late been a
disappointment. Presumably there must be some depth
at which the gold finally gives out, and what everybody
wants to know is whether the deepest mines have already
reached that point, or whether there is something yet to
be struck down in the unfathomable bowels of the earth
which will resurrect and even eclipse all the ancient
glories of Bendigo. At present, the prospects seem to
point rather to the former possibility. Meanwhile, the
shafts keep going down, and if they don t strike gold
they may at least settle a theological question which no
one has ever yet solved. The Hustler’s line is a trifle
deader th'an even the New Chum ; and the Garden Gully
group is a painful story of hope deferred which maketh
the shareholder sick. The share-list is a long and dismal
category of stocks which are trying to keep above the
shilling level, and mostly failing dismally in the effort,
and very, very few of the fortnightly returns get above
XOOoz. Often half of them don’t get up to ten.

Dear Bulletin, — I visited Anakie, Q., a month
or so ago, to see what the much-talked-of Dobson’s claim
was like. The shaft was then sunk 40ft. on an ironstone
blow. This stone when dollied resembled yellow ochre
in appearance, and when panned off showed a tail, say,
|-of-an-inch long. These operations had to cease for the
gold is so fine that it can only be saved by an expensive
process. Given chlorination and Anakie might be a big
thine', for all round this place gold-carrying ironstone
abounds.—S.

At Coolgardie, The Bulletin presumes, a man
is reckoned a fool who spoils good water with whisky.
But even now, things cannot be muqh worse than in
Cenlral Queensland before the bores went down, when
hotel visitors had to do with a dry polish, and the land-
lord at the bar handed you the brandy bottle to help
yourself, but measured out the water in a teaspoon—if
you were so soft as to want water.

The long-lost Lower Cape (N.Q.) “ deep lead ”
has, it is said, been picked up again by two jockeys.
One hundred men are on the ground. The Towers warden
reports—sinking 70 feet, wash thin.

From Rocky River (N.Q.) rush few men are
coming back —perhaps because there is something good
or perhaps because of floods. The gold is scattered over
11 miles, and is like that of the Coen—mint value, £3 7s.
It is, however, persistently rumoured that the rush is a
duffer,

• » » *
Why don’t the whites take to vegetable-grow-
ing? The writer has heard no less an authority than
Angus Mackay declare that the whites, if they seriously
began, would easily beat the Chow at vegetable-raising.
What is more—there is a white at Botany (Sydney) who
has done so and is making a good thing out of it. Let a
company, managed on the lines of the Fresh Food and Ice
Co., begin, and there is a grand investment.

A Melb. daily remarks that a new local cemetery
will soon be a necessity. On account of the coming
winter, of course. This may explain why some dismal
relics of the Boom have been seen with their heads
together lately, in the old fashion. Working up a boom
in grave allotments.
* * * *
In one asylum in England there are eight
lunatics who went crazed through the failure of the City
of Glasgow Bank some 16 years ago. How many bank
lunatics will there be in the various Australian asylums
when all the mental wrecks of the reconstruction year are
gathered in ? And how many crazed ghosts will be wait-
ing beside the Styx to gibber horribly at the Melb. and
Sydney boomers, when their spectres in their spectral
top-hats appear on the other side ?
♦ * * *
Among the U.S. railway bankruptcies of last
year were the great Northern Pacific with a debt of
£25,000,000; the Atchison, Topeka, and Santa F6—the
biggest railway co. on earth, with nearly 13,000 miles of
line and an ugly record of previous reconstruction, and
built, like the Northern Pacific, mostly out of land grants ;
the New York and New England system ; and the Read-
ing railway—one of those Pennsylvania lines which
squatter Abbott holds up to public admiration in his
perennial letter to Sydney D.T. as a glorious example
of the advantages of private railway enterprise and the
horrors of State ownership. Which reminds The Bulle-
tin, by the way, that squatter Abbott, of Wingen, hasn’t
been heard from on this subject for quite a long time now.
« * * *
Welcome Gully, Daylesford, is the scene of the
latest Vic. gold discovery.
* ♦ * •
There is in Sydney a man who has proved to the
satisfaction of a number of men in the trade that salt,
equal to best imported, can be made in any quantity
from sea-water at Bulli for £1 a ton—the amount of the
import-duty. Most of the required capital has been put
together, and very shortly the Pacific salt-works will be
in full blast.
• * * •
It is officially denied that outside of the com-
missioners’ fees, any N.S W. bankruptcy officials get pay
beyond their salaries. All the sani", it is true that certain
of these officials divide annually hundreds of pounds in
copying-fees —a system in vogue also at other State law-
offices, and which wants looking into. The perquisite
system causes many abuses.

A German importer in Melbourne lately for-
warded to a German firm in London a £5 Bank of England
note, in payment of certain goods. The Louden firm, in
acknowledging receipt, debits the Melbourne firm with
7s. 6d., being Is. 6d. in the £, “which we have had to
pay for changing the note into currency.” For cold
cheek this is unique.

Ghosts of a Gallows.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was a very small boy, some 18
years ago, I had the run of Melbourne Gaol.
Gates, such as they are there, for the most part,
did not limit my freedom, for I was so small that
I could squeeze through the interstices of the bars
of which they were made. “Oit in the stilly
night ” did I startle the lonely warder on duty in
the silent prison.
Fittingly with my morbid environment, and
doubtless encouraged by it, my disposition was
gloomy, not to say ghoulish. I took a delight in
the gallows and all its hideous appurtenances,
animate and inanimate, from the hangman, Gately,
who was my particular friend, to the victim whom
the hour faithfully provided ; and from the rope,
renewed for each execution, to the soap wherewith
it was greased. Then there was the gallows
graveyard in which I often sat and indulged my
boyish meditations. I can let anyone have an
“ Elegy in a Gallows Graveyard ” cheap. Hang-
man’s acre in Melbourne Gaol was what was
known as “ The Boys’ Yard.” It was a narrow
strip of high-walled space in which juvenile
larrikins were set by day to break stones and
gather what wisdom they could from a contempla-
tion of the initials of the hanged, carved into the
wall opposite each other. By night, no doubt,
the gallows ghosts whose corporeal substances
were quicklimed at the south end of the yard,
came forth and disported themselves with spiritual
skipping, swinging, or other pastime in which a
rope plays leading part. One night I went to see,
but saw nothing, which, however, I attributed to
the fact that
A cock at sight of sun mistook for moon
Crowed forth the hour of dawn some hours too soon,
and so caused the deceived spirits to prematurely
vanish.
Some fifty or more strangled saints lie buried in
this spot, of which there are many legends whose
time is not yet come.
# # •
My chief interest in the luckless wretch
on hand was always consummated on the night
before the strangulation, which almost invariably
took place on a Monday. The Sunday evening
before, during church-time, when it was not likely
I would be impeded in my mission by paternal
interference, would see me, a seven-year-older,
slip from a door leading from my father’s quarters
into the front yard of the gaol. Across this I
would dart into the main building, along a passage
to a barred gate, which gave me no check, through
the new wing, under the gallows, along a lane,
through the hospital garden, and then up some
well-worn steps that led to the chocolate-coloured
old wing, in which were the cells of the con-
demned. The high, long, narrow place was always
but dimly lighted, and steeped in a palpable
silence, which was only emphasised by the low
whistling or pathetic croning of some prisoner
whose sorrows thus salved themselves in song.
I always made straight for the condemned cell
in occupation, easily distinguishable by the light
which from its door of bars shot across the corri-
dor. In the rays of this the warder on duty sat
on guard. From him I got welcome. Every
warder was my friend, but apart from such a fact
my company would prove a break in the depressing
monotony of the night duty.
Of valedictory interviews with tne condemned I
have eight records. Among these stand out those
with Weaohurcb, my present story ; Basilio Bon-
dietto, against whose execution old George Stephen
(and David Gaunsotf, by the way) vigorously pro-
tested—l have the legal Nestor’s final protest
before me now ; James Ashe, who “ had no know-
ledge of a Creator or any religion, had lived the
life of an ignorant herd boy, and died penitent.”
How thac sort always does die penitent! And
finally with Ned Kelly, historically heroic, literally
commonplace.
• • •
“This man was a deep scoundrel,” says
Evening News Heaton of Weachuroh in his
diabolical “Dictionary of Dates.” If he was,
then it was only that sort of scoundrel for which
rotten prison systems are mainly responsible. In
the eyes of all right-thinking persons he was, of
course, the very worst type of desperado, because
he set “ law’n’order ” and every other “respect-
able ” consideration at defiance in the vain hope
that thereby he would obtain a fair open trial and
justice of an ideal quality. He is further interest-
ing as being the pet illustration of the efficacy of
hanging, or rather the insufficiency of imprison-
ment.
# # «
He was variously known as John Hallara,
John Taylor, and John Weaohurch. The last was
probably his proper name. In appearance he was
like the common variety of Chinaman, sallow com-
plexion, low-built, and with slanting, sleepy eyes.
He was possessed of a rude sort of capacity for
disputation, and that bush-lawyer quality, unillu-
minated _ by any sort of cultivation, ultimately
proved his destruction. He questioned all things,
and so firm fixed was he in his own interpretations
of them that his eagerness to put them to the test
consumed him, until at last it became a sort of
insanity, under the influence of which he com-
mitted deeds which gave the law an excuse to
hang him.
He was cradled in crime, and in gaol was his
grave. Born in 1829, he took the lawless path
before he was old enough to have understanding
of any other. He was first consigned to a Re-
formatory, in itself a sufficient sink of contamina-
tion to defile one far less susceptible than he.
Burglary was his speciality, and in 1862 he was
first committed in Victoria for that crime.
It is no wonder that a man like this was in
constant trouble both in and out of gaol. In gaol
he disputed the legality of almost every act of the
authorities, sometimes sanely enough, but more
often frivolously, and like an imbecile. Punish-
ment naturally resulted, and his original sentence
was increased. In this was his grand opportunity.
He was convinced beyond moving by any known
power, including fear of death, that such increase
of his term was totally illegal. Of course the
authorities would not admit his contention. It
was deemed impolitic even to consider it or give
the prisoner any satisfaction in such a dispute, or
indeed any other. It was “subversive of dis-
cipline,” “bad precedent,” and much more besides
in the shibboleth of official stupidity and red-tape
thraldom.
“Then,” declared Weachurch, “if I am denied
a hearing and justice by fair means, I will obtain
them by foul,” He meant that he would resist
what he considered his wrongful detention, by
such acts as would compel the authorities to
place him in the dock, where he would have an
opportunity of publicly declaring his grievance.
This was no empty boast, for subsequent to his
pronouncement of it he was punished for more
than a hundred breachesof thePentridgediseipline.
So intractable did he become that he was kept
closely confined, but even then he found means to
pursue his purpose.
Inspector-General Duncan was a God-fearing
man, and when the convict requested an interview
with him, he granted it, and entered the cell well
armoured in the spirit of the Lord, Notwith-
standing, he was carried out prostrated by a
wound in the groin inflicted by Weachurch with a
knife, which, despite of official vigilance, he had
secreted. For this he was sentenced to death, a
decree which, however, was commuted to ira-
prisonment for life. Inspector-General Duncan
was only scratched* and was well compensated
later on, when Parliament voted him £5OO or so
as a solatium for the injury.
On another occasion Weachurch complained
that the close confinement was affecting his health.
He was, therefore, given a hoe, and set to cut
down weeds in a remote paddock, under the super-
vision of Warder Ooghlan. In a little while he
reported himself at the prison, and calmly
announced that he had just killed the warder.
Happily, this was not strictly accurate, but it was
not far out, for Ooghlan was found with his head
split open, and like unto death. He, however,
recovered, and Weachurch was again put on his
trial for attempted murder. The benevolent and
eloquent Ireland undertook his defence and
secured an acquittal. In gratitude, Weachurch
indited a poem to his able and generous advocate.
1 his metrical effort was far less excusable than his
attempt to murder, but no action was taken to
avenge the violated muse.
Among other acts of violence committed by the
aggrieved convict was the burning down of the
shoemaker’s workshops, causing a damage
estimated at .£4OOO. But he was not again able,
for a long period, to compel the authorities to
send him to public trial. However serious his
offence he was brought before the Visiting-Justice,
and it was the series of petty trials which he
invoked which caused the magistrate’s court at
Pentridge to be known as the Star Chamber, a
nick-name which it retains to this day,
At last the authorities took drastic steps in
their own defence. A cell was converted into a
cage, to be seen yet by the curious, and in this
Weachurch was confined like a wild beast. Special
rules were made directing how he was to be
treated. At exercise he was always to be escorted
by two warders. His clothes were to be changed
weekly. The cage-door was never to be opened
exeept in the presence of two warders, and much
more of a similar character. But all the ingenuity
of the authorities could not conquer the cunning
of the convict. One day, warder Moran, with
another, entered the cage to repair the prisoner’s
irons, and was struck down with a bed-bolt
wielded by Weachurch, just as Parson Hill had
been slain some years before by convict Ritson.
There followed a third trial for attempted
murder. It was unique, but its place is not here,
I have before me Weachurch’s brief drawn up in
his own misspelt quaint diction and crazy scrawl,
In his written speech he says : “The gallows is
ready and yorning for me, and my base and
maliceous enemies are ready to shed my blood and
gloat over my miserable corps. . . The name
of Weachurch has become a mock and a by word
for mothers and nursemaids to frighten children
with.” Then followed his defence, so eloquently
characteristic of his ruling crankiness: “Inno-
eence of the assault is no part of my defence,
because I wilfully and deliberately committed
that assault after due consideration, and I call
God to witness that I did it not out of malice or
ill-will (although I confess that I did not like
Warder Moran’s way of doeing his duty) or with
any intent to kill or do anybody searious injury,
but I done it with the greates of deliberate caution
and with no other view than geting sent to where
I now stand so that I might have an opportunity
of exposing the wilfull and maliceous injustice
that I have been so long subjected to, and seek
legal redress.”
His legal redress took the shape of the sentence
of death. The gallows was prescribed, when
obviously the case demanded nothing but a lunatic
asylum and a straight-jacket. But such are the
inscrutable ways of civilised and christianised
men I
• *
This wa s the poor wretch whom I trotted
up to Rape at on that Sunday evening 18 years
ago> All was still as usua l in the gaol at such a
t j me- Noiselessly I went along the corridor of the
old w j ng to t ] ie warc ier on duty, who gave me
we i come . a nd when I peered through the bars of
the con( Jemned cell door, Weachurch gave me
we ] come a l so
“Ain’t you frightened of me, little man ?” he
ag k ed
Inspired perhaps by an unconscious generosity,
or perhaps w i th a feeling of safety in the presence
o{ a warder we ]] armed w j t h deadly weapons, I
anawere d the pathetic query in a manner which,
con templating it now with an appreciation of
art i st i c e ff ec t, I cannot regard as anything short
0 f b eau tiful if not sublime.
j squeezed through the bars and stood in the
cell of the condemned man. He took me on his
knee.
“ Do you know who I am ?” he asked,
I nodded my head after the uncouth fashion of
juveniles of seven. Had I spoken, great was my
veneration for hanges, I should have said ;
“Yes, sir! - ’
His only acknowledgment of my nescience was
a heavy sigh.
“Do you know what is to be done to me?” was
his next.
I nodded again. Glad am I now that his legal
acumen was not alert, so that he was not moved
to test my affirmative by cross-examination. It
would not have been pleasant to have had to say,
“ You are going to be hanged !”
“Do you know why ?” he next asked,
I shook my head emphatically, and he seemed
pleased. I suppose^because it gave him an oppor-
tunity to tell his grievance.
“It is because I sought justice,” he said,
“Never seek justice, my boy. Let people do
what they like to you. If you don’t you’ll only
be hanged like me.”
I wasn’t experienced enough to know whether
he was serious in this advice. I have my sus-
pioions now, however.
At last I thought I must be going, and I said
good night. I shall never forget that parting.
The miserable wretch pressed my boyishly soft,
small hand in his, rough and so often less gently
used. Then he kissed the top of my head, leaving
it wet and warm with tears,
Next day he was hanged,

, wished. another trial, and had hope of
it. right up to his last moment. And 1 have often
wondered why he did not make a final bid tor it
iu his wonted fashion, by taking me by my
juvenile heels and dashing out my juvenile brains
against the cell wall.
I suppose it didn t occur to him.

LIFE.
This, the most splendid prose poem in the
English language, is a recent utterance of R. G.
Ingersoll, the greatest living American orator :
Horn of love and hope, of ecstasy and pain, of agony
and fear, of tears and joy—dowered with the wealth of
two united hearts—held in happy arms, with lips upon
life’s drifted font, blue-veined and fair, where perfect
peace (inds perfect form—rocked by willing feet and
wooed to shadowy shores of sleep by siren mother singing ,
soft and low—looking with wonder’s wide and startled
eyes at common things of life and day—taught by want
and wish and contact with the things that touch the
dimpled flesh of babes —lured by light and flame, and
charmed by colour’s wondrous robes —learning the use of
hands and feet, and by the love of mimicry beguiled to
utter speech—releasing prisoned thoughts from crabbed
and curious marks on soiled and tattered leaves—puzzling
the brain with crooked numbers and their changing, tan-
gled worth—and so through years of alternating day and
night, until the captive grows familiar with the chains
and walls and limitations of a I'fe.
And time runs on in sun and shade, until the one of all
the world is wooed and won, and all the lore of love is
taught and learned again. Again a home is built with
ihe fair chamber wherein faint dreams, like cool and
shadowy vales, divide the billowed hours of love. Again
the miracle of birth—lhe pain and joy, the kiss of wel-
come and the cradle song drowning the drowsy prattle of
a babe.
And (ben the sense of obligation and of wrong—pity
for those who toil and weep—tears for the imprisoned and
despised—love for the generous dead, and in the heart
the rapture of a high resolve.
And then ambition, with its lust of place and pelf and
power, longing to put upon its breast distinction’s worth-
less badge. Then keener thoughts of men, and eyes that
see behind the smiling mask of craft-flattered no more
by the obsequious cringe of gain and greed—knowing the
uselessness of hoarded gold—of honour bought from
those who charge the usury of self-respect—of power
that only bends a coward’s knees and forces from the lips
of fear the lies of praise. Knowing at last the unstudied
gesture of esteem, the reverent eyes made rich with
honest thought, and holding high above all other things— |
high as hope’s great throbbing star above the darkness of
the dead—the love of wife and child and friend.
Then looks of grey, and growing love of other days and
half-remembered things—then holding withered hands of
those who first held his, while over dim and loving eyes
death softly presses down the lids of rest.
And so, locking in marriage vows his children’s hands
and crossing others on the breasts of peace, with
daughters’ babes upon his knees, the white hair mingling
with the gold, he journeys on from day to day to that
horizon where the dusk is waiting for the night. At last,
sitting by the holy hearth of home as evening’s embers
change from red to grey, he falls asleep within the arms
of her he worshipped and adored, feeling upon his pallid j
lips love’s last and holiest kiss.
Nemesis —an old, old man and a young and
pretty wife.
“If Cleopatra were alive to-day the
length of her nose would have little to do in
determining her career. Noses, as other traits in
the present advanced state of surgery, are merely
determinate features. If a pug-nose does nob
harmonise with the other features or a pensive
disposition, it is easily changed by removing a
wedge-shaped piece between the nostrils. This is
done without pain by spraying the nose with
cocaine. If a Roman nose gives one the air of
being too dominant, the hump is removed by
making an incision, cutting the flesh away, and a
steel burr, run by an electric motor, easily grinds
down the hump. The skin, then trimmed to fib,
sewn, and properly bound, soon heals. Cocaine
again renders the operation a mere detail. In the
same manner large mouths are transformed into
dainty shapes by removing the surplus ab bhe
corners and sewing bhem up. The surgeon who
is bhe authority for bhese statements says bhab bhe
time will come when skin-grafting will be so easily
managed that another countenance may be ordered
like a new bonnet.” —Ex.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
“It is often said that if the fears of hell-
fire were suddenly removed men would abandon
themselves without restraint to their propensitie 3
and appetites; recklessness and despair would
take possession of the human race, and society
would be dissolved. But I believe that the fears
of hell-fire have scarcely any power upon earth ab
all, and that when they do act upon the
human mind it is to make it pious, not to make it
good. Prevention of crime, it is well known, de-
pends not on the severity bub on the certainty of re-
tribution. Just as a criminal is often acquitted by
the jury because the penalties of the law are dis-
proportioned to the magnitude of the offence, so
the diabolic laws which inflict an eternal punish-
ment for transitory sins have been tempered by a
system of free pardons which deprive them of any
efficiency they might have once possessed. What
would be the use of laws against murder if the
condemned criminal could obtain his liberty by
apologising to the Queen ? Yet such is the
Christian system, which, though in one sense
beautiful on account of its mercy, is also immoral
on account of its indulgence,” (from Winwood
Reade’s “Martyrdom of Man.”). For instance,
the late Mrs. Knorr, baby-farmer, pub herself
right by apologising to God, the fear of hell-fire
having made her suddenly pious without making
her the least bit better than before.
