The Woman of the Future.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
O 1 The Woman of the Future ! Sound the
trumpets —beat the drums !
She has donned the coat and breeches and in
triumph on she comes ;
fthe has fixed her vengeful optic on the trem-
bling tyrant Man,
She has sworn to quit the bondage of the wash-
tub and the pan.
She has sworn to crush the despot and to pu l
his best cigar,
Sworn to spout from many a pulpit and to
practise at the bar ;
Sworn to clip her flowing ringlets, whether
auburn, black, or brow u,
Ami to raise upon her upper lip a tiny crop of
down.
She will come as comes a conqueror and she’ll
scorn to bill and coo,
And she’ll whistle for her darling when sl.c
comes to win and woo ;
And she’ll brave the boot cap icious of our own
irate papa,
And she’ll hug us in a frenzy when we bid her
“ Ask mamma !”
And she’ll leave us in the evening, saying,
“ Rock the cradle, John !
If you’re lonesome, darn some stockings, dear,
or sew some buttons on ;
Pray, be careful that you don’t disturb the
baby’s soft repose,
And you’ll find his feeding-bottle close beside
his little nose !”
Yea ! She’ll hold the land in awe from far
Beersheba unto Dan,
And she’ll take us to the opera and go out “ to
see a man
And with cursed cunning ogle (ah, ye husbands!
there’s the rub !)
Will she leer upon the barman when shecalleth
at the pub.
And she’ll chuck the handsome youths she
meets beneath the chubby chin,
Aud she’ll tell you with a hiccup, “ Sack and
sugar’s not a sin !”
And she’ll wander home at 2 p.m. aud tell her
trusting hub.,
“ We were slaying of the microbe at the Gay
Galooters’ Club.”
And the pride of Man shall dwindle and his
glory fade away,
Like the glory of the sunset in the train of
parting day ;
And a huge discarded petticoat shall be his
funeral-pall,
And a cackling Hen-Convention scream a paiau
at his fall.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Sydney Municipal Council issues about
7000 writs per annum for the recovery of rates.
At the inquest on the body of Martial
Bourdin, alleged Anarchist, who blow himself up
in Greenwich Park, the jury returned a verdict of
felo-de-se. That is a British juryman’s idea of
being nasty. But it would really have been much
more insulting to have brought in the customary
“temporary insanity.”
During the year ended Ist October, ’92,
clergy of the English State Church distinguished
themselves as follows : Convicted for drunken-
ness, 121 ; assault, 109 ; cruelty to animals, 11 ;
various other charges, 84 ; eloped with other men’s
wives, 17 ; cast in damages for breach of promise,
14 ; suicided, 12. God bless the Establishment!
Numurkah (Vic.) Standard is as good as
a natural history. A delighted subscriber sends
Thk Bulletin a copy, dated Feb. 13. which con-
tains a column leader on poultry-fancying, and
a half-column sub. on the dog-market, while
another half-column is taken up with a panegyric
on a dead cat who was “ the heaviest, largest,
most intelligent, and most peaceful proper-
mannered cat that ever looked into a fish-tin.”
This editor should be keeper of a Zoo—or
resident.
Heine in Paris, July, 1842 : —“A weary>
sleepy, yawning state of peace. All is still as a
night of deep snow in midwinter. Only a light,
monotonous fall as of drops. That is Interest
(Zin/.e) which keeps trickling into Capital; you
can really bear how they grow, these riches of the
rich. Between whiles, the light sobs of poverty.
Sometimes, too, there is a sound as though a knife
was being sharpened.”
This is the confused story of a youth, now
in serious trouble. The story is so much confused
that it is difficult to tell where the moral comes
in, so the bald facts are laid down, and each
person can find his own moral. He was the
enthusiastic secretary of a suburban teetotal
society in an Australian capital, and being
taken unusually bad with the divine afflatus
he decided that he was a “ Numble In-
strument ” selected by Providence to lecture
on the drink curse. Accordingly he hired
the local town hall, and persuaded a well-
known light to promise his services as chair-
man. Also, a respected clergyman and other
celebrities undertook to be present on the plat-
form, and a brass band was engaged. But when
the night arrived the young man was missing and
there was no lecture. The miserable youth’s
courage had failed him at the last moment and he
fled in a bad attack of stage-fright, and left the
brass band in the lurch. Next day he was
arrested on a charge of obtaining tan boots by
means of a valueless cheque. So far as can be
judged by the evidence, the intentions of the un-
happy youth were perfectly honourable, but he
fell a victim to an over-sanguine temperament.
Being convinced that the day after the meeting he
would be able to open a large banking account
with the collection-money, he bought great quan-
tities of tan boots in different shops and gave
post-dated cheques in payment; and when he
funked there was no meeting and no collection,
and the cheques were all n.s.f. This shows, for
one thing, that the tan-boot curse is about as
undermining as the drink curse.
Justice in the Punjaub (British India) is
administered in a curious fashion. The occupants
of the gaols are mostly old men—very old men as
a rule, and utterly past work. When a crime is
committed and there is strong evidence against
some man or woman who is still of working age,
the accused’s oldest and most helpless relative
always comes forward and pleads guilty, and all
the rest of the family and the neighbours give
corroborative evidence. The idea of letting a
bread-winner go to gaol while there is a bedridden
grandfather who can go there instead and be fed
at Government expense is such obvious waste that
the Punjaubi never dreams of allowing it. A few
years ago a young woman was charged, on good
evidence, with infanticide, but a venerable harri-
dan belonging to the family promptly assumed
the whole responsibility of the infant, and her
confession was backed up by such a mass of evi-
dence that the Court lost its head and sent her up
for the rest of her life. And on another occasion
when a sturdy native was nearly convicted of
murder and robbery, his‘grandfather, who was
paralysed in both arms, was assisted into Court
and solemnly pleaded guilty. This time the law
objected, but half the neighbourhood swore to
having seen him commit the crime, and it was
universally attested that the old man had
recovered the use of his arms for an hour or two
and had perpetrated the murder in the interval,
so there was nothing for it hut to send him to
gaol-with the rest of the cripples.
A paragraph re Maud Hobson, who plays
the name part in “A Gaiety Girl,” has gone the
rounds unoritioised. It is to the effect that Maud
received a choice variety of Xmas boxes, including
i i r i i i a. •
a necklace of absurd value, two or three carriages,
sundry horses with fodder attached, and a couple
of men servants. This latter item sets one think-
ing. The British flunkey is largely a creature of
circumstances, who often consents to be trans-
ferred along with the fittings and other furniture
from one employer to another. But the
nght of Jeames to give a month s warning
to the temporary possessor of his padded
calves and immortal soul is generally ac-
cepted as a portion of the British coustitu-
tinn The camins of London rnav nnf their
lion. me gamins or London may put their
tongues out at the plush-clad menial and call him
by many contemptuous epithets, but, thus far, no
boy has twitted Jeames with being a Xmas-box.
In short, The Bulletin wants to know more
about the pair of men-servants who were made
over to Maud Hobson by deed of gift. Were their
salaries paid up to the day of their demise, and
are they under contract to hang on to the lady’s
fortunes, come what may ? Also, can Maud, for
her part, give them to a johnnie without consult-
ing the original purchaser, and could they be sold
by auction in the event of Maud’s establishment
being broken up? Are they two special atten-
dants on whota Maud had previously oast her
virgin eye, or did they enter as strangers at
her gates and briefly announce themselves as
seasonable offerings ? Had they previously decided
the vital question of whose place it was to fill the
coal-scuttle ’ What would barmen to them if
coalscuttle. yvnat would happen to them it
‘Sisrtsi
blanky coal-scuttle herself? These are but a few
of the conundrums which never suggest themselves
to the sub-editor as he clips the monotonous para-

Little differences of opinion like those
expressed re the inspiration of the Bible, at the
recent meeting of the Presbyterian Assembly in
Sydney, play the deuce with the waverers. The
Moderator asserts that the Bible cannot be wholly
inspired, the Rev. Dill Maoky says that if the
Bible be not verbally inspired it is valueless as a
guide to faith or practice. It used tube the Word of
God—now part of it is the Word—but which part ?
Missing word competition for parsons only.
Your true believer is the great doubter, but he
seldom has the courage of his unconvietions.
Revolution—Reform resisted too long and
yielded too late.
Estimated 1894 sugar crops—Queensland
103,500 tons ; N.S.W., 17,500 ; Fiji, 25,000. By
the time the projected beet industry is firmly
established in N.S.W. and Victoria there will be
no Australian market left. Then beet and cane
can compete for export trade, and we shall see
which is fitter to survive. But beet isn’t ready
yet, by a long, long way, in spite of Melb. experi-
ments.
Touching epitaph over the dust of a local
celebrity’s third wife in a Maoriland West Coast
cemetery :
Remember thee! Ay, while memory holds a seat in
this distracted globe. Here lies Mary Ann Emily
daughter of George , of , the honoured
wife of S. F. , of Ibis town. A woman of infinite
tenderness and generous impulses, whose ear was ever
open to a tale of distress, and whose purse was always
ready to alleviate it. A woman who, in life, had the
privilege of meeting on level ground (he good and
reverend man who lies south from this when each were
about their several works of mercy. A woman who was
a pattern wife as surely her husband's word was law, and
she made him her god. A woman who, given fair oppor-
tunities in youth and lively sympathy of the sex in
middle life, might have attained a ripe old age. A
woman, in short, who, for large-heartedness, unselfishness
and integrity and piety, as few could compare. This work
poor tribute of respect placed here in loving memory by
the man to whom this woman gave all her wealth of love
is a lasting reproach to those who failed in their duty
when, sick unto death for 17 months, she laye a-thinking
a-thinking, a-thinking. Aye, countrymen, if when you
make your prayers, God should be so obdurate as your-
selves, how would it fare with your departed souls?

She was—ah! what was she?
